==PAGE:8==
Abstract
As of 2022, there is no direct-evidence of active volcanism in Venus. The detection and quantification of
volcanic activity in the Venusian surface is a prominent objective within the planetary science community, as
it plays a central role in deciphering the planet’s origin, geological history and the driving factors behind its
runaway greenhouse atmospheric effect. The synthetic aperture radar onboard NASA’s Magellan space mis-
sion imaged 98% of the planet’s surface and unveiled, with the finest resolution to date, a volcanically-shaped
landscape with a wide array of volcanic and tectonic features, predominantly extrusive, effusive volcanism
encompassing a variety of mafic lava flows. With the future observations of the newly approved EnVision
and VERITAS missions to Venus, both carrying new-generation radar systems as main payload, image inter-
comparison with Magellan products aiming at lava flow change detection is a unique opportunity to gather
the first direct proof of ongoing eruption activity on the planet. Nevertheless, it is a challenging endeavor
considering the observational differences between the missions in terms of resolution, viewing geometry and
carrier frequencies. To address it from a global perspective, and considering its difficulties, a change de-
tection strategy is proposed and discussed in this work. Several facets of the task are explored in detail,
particularly the radar signatures of the different lava morphological units, the theoretical and probabilistic
framework for incoherent change detection between 2 SAR systems with different resolutions, and the cre-
ation of an empirical model to simulate images of the future radar systems, given the Magellan observations
and a blend of Earth observation data from the NovaSAR-1, TerraSAR-X and SRTM missions. Emphasis
is put in Magellan image processing, particularly in topography processing and SAR despeckling techniques.
The results show the ability of the algorithm to detect future changes with very high detection probability
and low false alarm rates, given an ideal empirical model response, for a variety of Venusian locations and
scenarios, including considered hot-spot locations such as Maat Mons and Iduun Mons. Change detection
is shown to be robust, given the radiometric differences among the different terrains observed, regardless
of the shape and extension of the changes, and the possible sources of error are categorized and discussed.
Further work is required to expand and perfect the Magellan-EnVision/VERITAS empirical model, specially
to include more and diverse EO data and to evaluate the behavior of all possible land covers in the Venusian
surface besides lava flows. Moreover, a more accurate mathematical characterization of the SAR images that
includes image texture could be useful to enhance the algorithm performance, and consequently the change
detection theoretical framework should be enlarged. The results of the study suggest that change detection
between the Magellan radar and the future radars onboard EnVision/VERITAS can obtain positive, precise
results in case of ongoing volcanic activity if the backscatter changes due to radar system changes are well
characterized.
7
==PAGE:9==
Introduction
A lot has been said and studied by the scientific community about Venus, a priori the more Earth-like planet
in the solar system in terms of size and distance from the Sun, as well as chemical composition. Neverthe-
less, after a successful space exploration record, the planet’s surface and atmosphere has been shown to be
radically different from that of the Earth, with conditions wholly opposed to terrestrial life, including an
almost total absence of water, and a thick, toxic atmospheric cover composed primarily by the greenhouse
gas CO2 [98]. The reasons that fueled the creation of this hellish landscape are not fully understood, and
consequently the planet’s geological and climatic history remains elusive.
Discovering and quantifying the relationship between internal dynamics and volcanic activity with the cli-
matic conditions of Venus, which present no life-driven impact factors, is also of paramount importance when
trying to understand the Earth’s own climate change and the atmospheric conditions of other terrestrial
worlds such as exoplanets [133].
For these reasons, the National Aeronautics and Space Administration
(NASA) and the European Space Agency (ESA) selected in June 2021 3 new interplanetary missions to be
sent to Venus by the end of this decade or the beginning of the next [167]. One of the NASA initiatives is the
Deep Atmosphere Venus Investigation of Noble gases, Chemistry, and Imaging (DAVINCI) mission, which
will include a suite of spectrometer instruments and feature an atmospheric probe with the main objective
of modelling the atmosphere and understanding its origin [139]. The other discovery-class mission selected
by NASA is the Venus Emissivity, Radio science, InSAR, Topography, And Spectroscopy mission (VER-
ITAS), an orbiter that features a spectrometer and a Synthetic Aperture Radar (SAR) named the Venus
Interferometric Synthetic Aperture Radar (VISAR) as main payload [107]. On another hand, ESA approved
the medium-class mission EnVision, which also includes an advanced multipurpose radar as main payload,
simply named the Venus Synthetic Aperture Radar (VENSAR) [133].
Spaceborne radar systems for imaging purposes are a requirement to observe the surface of Venus, as their ob-
servation bands are not affected by the thick and optically opaque atmosphere of the planet. Therefore, both
VISAR and VENSAR, which are both new-generation radars that present an order of magnitude resolution
improvement with respect of previous radar observations of the planet’s surface, are particularly well-suited
to observe whether the surface of the planet is experimenting geological and volcanic activity. The question
whether Venus is volcanically active at the present day is at the center of the debate, as it is the possible
detection and quantification of volcanism that would provide most of the missing pieces preventing the full
understanding of the planet’s geological history, it’s climate, and consequently the coupling between both
[121]. Radar image intercomparison of volcanic surface locations observed at different times is one of the
methodologies that provides a well-defined framework for volcanic change detection, and given the oppor-
tunity that arises with the newly selected radar missions, it is a major focus of study by their respective
engineering and science teams 1. NASA’s Magellan radar space mission [16], operational between 1990 and
1994, provided SAR images of approximately 98 % of the surface, and it is to date the best reference of
the planet’s surface characteristics. The comparison of Magellan imagery with future VISAR and VENSAR
imagery offers a unique opportunity to measure volcanic activity with an approximately 40-year temporal
baseline, which should be able to reveal surface changes if they are in fact occurring in certain locations. An
1Acknowledged in personal communication via email by Dr. Scott Hensley at JPL and Dr. Phillipa J. Mason at the Imperial
College London.
8
==PAGE:10==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
operational and robust SAR change detection strategy is therefore required in order to detect and precisely
measure these changes, but this is a challenging task even for Earth Observation (EO) standards, as the
radar systems involved present significant operational differences at several levels.
Given the scientific importance of quantifying eventual volcanic activity in Venus, the study and definition of
a suitable methodology for Magellan and VISAR/VENSAR change detection was chosen as the fundamental
topic of this MSc thesis project. The several factors revolving around it, as well as the definition of a narrow
and specific framework of the topic that could fit the extent and expectations of a MSc thesis project, are
addressed in the following background section. This is followed by a brief description of the project’s goals
and a justification of the methodology from the point of view of different scientific and engineering factors.
Finally, the several steps of the methodology are introduced to provide a global view of the work performed,
and a chapter overview is included to provide the reader with a clear and to-the-point understanding of each
section’s contents.
2.1
Background
In anticipation of this MSc thesis project, preliminary studies were performed by the author in order to
comprehend and characterize possible Venusian surface changes on one hand, and to study which SAR tech-
niques would be possible and optimal to detect them in the other. This study was a DTU special course
project (code 81114) supervised by Jørgen Dall, with the title ”Study of geologic processes on Venus, ob-
servable with Synthetic Aperture Radar” [140]. As regarded in several Magellan-era studies [20] [17] [39],
the dominant surface features of Venus are products of volcanic activity; up to 90 % of the surface is of
basaltic 2 origin, and the landscape is dominated by large lava plains, a wide array of lava flow morphologies,
and volcanic constructs such as shield volcanoes and domes. There is a predominance of extrusive, effusive
volcanism in the surface, which implies a preponderance of Hawaiian type eruptions (majoritarily explosion-
less and ashless eruptive events with outpouring of lava), and this is explained by the extreme pressure and
temperature conditions of the Venusian surface, but also by the lack of water and the overall magma com-
position of the planet, as well as its slightly lower surface gravity than on Earth [96]. Therefore, measuring
lava displacement appears to be one of the best-suited methods to detect possible volcanic eruptions in Venus.
On another hand, from the radar perspective there are several change detection techniques that can a priori
be exploited in the framework of the Magellan, VERITAS and EnVision radar missions. Of particular interest
is the Differential Interferometric Synthetic Aperture Radar (D-InSAR) technique [145] [142], which has been
used on Earth to detect up to millimeter-accurate displacements in case of earthquakes [97], landslides [113]
and also volcanic eruptions [63]. This technique uses multiple observations of the same area at different times
to isolate the phase difference between the acquisitions that is solely associated with a change on ground,
and it requires of high coherence between observations. Coherence between observations in itself can be used
to map changes in a technique known as Coherent Change Detection (CCD), more specifically the loss of
coherence of some areas with respect to others can be associated with surface modification processes such
as vegetation growth, man-made constructions, and quite effectively, the emplacement or displacement of
lava flows [43] [89]. To achieve significant radar coherence between observations requires of the same radar
observational characteristics, and generally, a positive relationship between the temporal baseline of the ob-
servations and the velocity of change; if the change is fast the temporal baseline needs to be short enough to
smoothly detect it.
The initial repeat pass interferometric layout of VENSAR [121] is not contemplated anymore in the current
EnVision mission status due to technical reasons, and only VISAR will be tuned to be able to produce
D-InSAR/CCD scenarios of about 12-18 chosen sites of 200 by 200 km [168]. Moreover, due to the long
temporal baseline of 40 years between the Magellan observations and the future ones, and the fact that
2Aphanite igneous rock formed by rapid cooling of low-viscosity lava flows rich in iron and magnesium (also called mafic lava
flows).
9
==PAGE:11==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
intermission radar coherence is not possible due to the use of different carrier frequencies, D-INSAR and CCD
techniques are not possible in the case of image comparison with Magellan. Since, in addition, interferometric
performance simulations have been currently more explored both on Earth and Venus, also in preparation
for the new missions, this technique was discarded to be explored in this project
Figure 2.1: Magellan processed SAR images showing a variety of lava flows. Digitate lava flows emplaced
over a basaltic lava plain north of Sif Mons shield-volcano, Eistla Regio (1). Lakshmi Regio flows (2). Lava

On another hand, when it comes to the so-called incoherent change detection techniques (don’t rely on
in radar coherence), the use of the offset-tracking technique was investigated [60].
Offset-tracking, also
sometimes called feature-tracking, is an incoherent change detection technique that exploits image cross-
correlation, and has been used in scenarios such as glacier motion [136], dune displacement [131] and also
landslide events [110]. Nevertheless, this technique requires feature preservation to a certain degree, and the
unpredictable nature of the flowing of lava makes it for the most part unsuitable in possible change detection
scenarios. Since this technique was discarded, the logical choice was to base the change detection strategy
between the missions in incoherent techniques using test statistics, which ideally quantify SAR changes by
means of image rationing [30] [64]. These techniques are globally referred to, during this report, with the
label of test statistics. Discussion on the optimal methodology within this family of techniques is thoroughly
addressed in chapters 3 and 5, but with this conclusion and the several factors introduced above, a specific
change detection scenario as the study case for this work was defined: the detection of lava flow changes
between the Magellan images and the future VISAR/VENSAR observations by means of incoherent change
detection.
In order to tackle this challenge, several data products have been seen as necessary and adequate to achieve
the project’s objectives (see next section), and are briefly introduced here. Magellan SAR imaging products
are the main dataset of the project. In addition, Magellan altimetry-derived topography products are also
used, and also a variety of EO data from radar missions that share similar design characteristics with the
VERITAS and EnVision missions, as they can be useful to predict their future observations. These datasets
10
==PAGE:12==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
are SAR imaging products belonging to the TerraSAR-X mission, the X-SAR Shuttle Radar Topography
Mission (SRTM) and the NovaSAR-1 mission. Their data characteristics and pre-processing is addressed in
chapter 4.
2.2
Objectives
The several objectives of the thesis, derived from the scope and particularities of the topic highlighted as a
conclusion in the previous section, are divided between primary objectives, which represent the several end-
goals of the project, secondary objectives, which are related or directly broader consequences of the primary
objectives, and technical tasks, which represent the main technical steps that need to be resolved in order to
achieve the primary objectives.
Primary objectives:
• Design a strategy able to detect possible surface changes in Venus due to new or advancing lava flows.
• Define an automatic, robust SAR change detection method that could be applied to the future VERITAS
and EnVision missions, together with products from the Magellan mission.
• Quantify the performance of the implemented change detection method.
• Provide a comprehensive framework to quantify possible lava flow displacement scenarios.
Secondary objectives:
• Establish the challenges and particularities of SAR image inter-comparison of different radar systems,
with different observation characteristics, targeting a planetary body.
• Assess the adaptability of the method to other Venusian surface changes, such as aeolian transports
and other sedimentary or weathering processes.
Technical tasks:
• Process the Magellan topographic product to make it suitable for comparison with SAR imaging prod-
ucts.
• Utilize a suitable despeckling or segmentation routine for Magellan SAR imagery.
• Design a SAR test statistic for change detection adapted to Venusian lava flows and the observational
capabilities of the different missions.
• Study how to integrate the performance of the test statistic with Magellan data and simulated VERITAS
and EnVision data.
• Elaborate the bases of a backscatter coefficient empirical model to predict VISAR/VENSAR images
based on knowledge from Magellan and EO observations.
• Design a performance test for the test statistic.
• Establish the error sources of the change detection strategy by assessing the performance of each
processing step.
11
==PAGE:13==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
2.3
Justification
There are several important points and perspectives that, stated together, justify both the objectives of the
work and the methodology followed to achieve them. Most of them have already been introduced in some
form or another in the previous dissertation. From a scientific perspective, the quantification of possible
Venusian volcanic activity is a necessary key milestone to provide answers to a wide array of unanswered
questions in the fields of planetary geology and climatology. Moreover, 2 of the main space agencies in the
world and the 2 with the largest approved budgets, NASA and ESA, have seen fit to approve new space
missions to Venus this decade, in great part precisely to try to answer these questions. This alone, together
with the fact that the missions were approved solely a year ago, demonstrates how relevant and momentous
the topic is on one hand, and on the other, how many studies and possibilities are yet to be explored in order
to make them successful.
The characteristics of the Venus atmosphere make SAR instruments the ideal systems to monitor the sur-
face. As the temporal gap increases between observations, more possibilities of volcanic-related changes in
the surface are expected to happen; because of that, the comparison of Magellan imagery and future VIS-
AR/VENSAR imagery offers the best-to-date opportunity to record a direct proof of eruptive activity. It
is estimated that the imaging at Magellan-level resolution or better of only ∼10% of the Venusian surface
would yield better than 99 % chance of detecting a new lava flow, even if the volcanic activity is at the low
end of the predictions [101]. The important differences of the missions’ designs in general, and of the radar
systems in particular, together with the nature of the changes to monitor (flowing of lava), make incoherent
test statistics the most suitable change detection technique for the task. This conclusion was pursued and
obtained in anticipation of the thesis, being the justification of the specific change detection strategy to
pursue the main objective, and hence this previous work acted as a technical justification for the project as
a whole [140].
Other instruments or approaches could be used to detect volcanism in the Venusian surface, although there
are few alternatives when it comes to recording direct-proof of it. There exist important indirect evidences
of active volcanism, namely:
• The recording of episodic injections of sulfur dioxide by the Pioneer Venus mission (one of the earliest
missions to the planet) throughout its operational history [9], variations which were also detected by the
Spectroscopy for Investigation of Characteristics of the Atmosphere of Venus (SPICAV) spectrometer
onboard ESA’s Venus Express mission between 2007 and 2012 [94].
• Thermal near-infrared brightness ”hotspots” in the surface detected by the Venus Monitoring Camera
(VMC) and the Visible and Infrared Thermal Imaging Spectrometer (VIRTIS) onboard Venus Express
[85]. Their characteristics resemble volcanic hotspots detected during eruptions on Earth, and their
locations correlate with important Venusian volcanic features.
• Brightness temperature increases recorded by the Magellan radiometry mode that are consistent with
theoretical subsurface temperature increases caused by ongoing lava flows [83].
• Observed Olivine weathering rates from backscatter and near-infrared observations that are only con-
sistent with ongoing lava flows with ages of the order of days-weeks [128].
All of these observations, which are compiled and further described in [140], offer solid arguments in favor of
active eruptions in Venus, and are in great part the main reasons behind the agencies’ selection of VERITAS,
DAVINCI and EnVision. Nevertheless, they are not considered ”direct” proofs of surface changes, and such
direct ”imaged” scenarios are only achievable by SAR systems, at least in the near future and particularly
given the designs of the only approved initiatives to record them.
In conclusion, the main traits of the strategy presented in this work are possibly the only justifiable ways
to directly detect volcanism on Venus. Besides, it is also worthwhile to mention that no rigorous change
12
==PAGE:14==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
detection program has been publicly reported on Magellan data to date (noting the difficulties of varying
incidence, look direction and data quality that Magellan would present with other missions) [101].
There is another reason justifying this thesis topic, and that is the author’s personal reasons to pursue it. This
work offers the opportunity to blend together significant remote sensing and SAR-related knowledge, of great
interest to the author, with an interplanetary scenario usually out of reach of the EO-level observational
capabilities, a possibility that the author has been awaiting for some time. This is, ultimately, the main
reason behind this choice of topic.
2.4
Methodology outline
There is a wide spectrum of topics that are part, to a greater or lesser extent, of the development of this MSc
thesis work, and they are often unrelated and part of different scientific and technological branches. This is
why it is fit to offer an overview and a temporal evolution of the different aspects of the work; to provide a
global perspective that can be later used to contextualize each section’s contents.
The core tasks before the definition and choice argumentation of the specific change detection strategy are
data reading and processing, in one hand, and the study of the radar signatures and characterization of
the Venusian surface on the other. Data from the Magellan mission and 3 EO missions is read, organized,
calibrated and filtered to a certain degree. Particularly, altimetry products from the Magellan mission require
an elaborated processing chain due to their much coarser spatial resolution (when comparing to the Magellan
SAR image products) and a series of inherent errors and limitations in their acquisition. Parallelly, an array
of locations of volcanic constructs, mainly lava flows of different morphology and shape, are analyzed from
the radar perspective to define what information is most usable in order to discern them and delimit.
When these 2 arrays of tasks are completed, and conclusions on the several critical factors related with defin-
ing a change detection method are reached, an end-to-end definition of the algorithm is provided (see section
5.2.2). After this step, which represents the core of the methodology, the several necessary tasks and studies
that are required for the algorithm are addressed. These include the generation of local incidence angle masks
derived from Magellan topography, the selection of a SAR image reconstruction technique in order to reduce
SAR intensity variability (speckle), the characterization of the observed backscatter values of Venusian sur-
face features in relation to the SAR viewing geometry, and the expansion of this modelling/characterization
to possible VISAR/VENSAR scenarios by means of EO data.
Thereafter, the probabilistic framework of the test statistic to detect possible changes is addressed with
theoretical demonstrations, numerical analysis and examples of probability scenarios including thresholding
methods and their performance expectations.
Finally, when the full results of the algorithm chain are exposed in chapter 6, several practical considerations
of the global performance of the algorithm are exposed and discussed, and performance tasks are presented
to evaluate the outcome of the different scenarios considered.
2.5
Chapter overview
The previous section aimed at describing the methodology as a whole by means of dissertation of the top-
ics, their temporal structure and the relationships they share. In here, the several tasks and their related
knowledge are linked with each of the chapters and sections of the work, in order to provide the reader with
a to-the-point description that can be identified with each of them.
Chapter 3 defines all necessary theoretical aspects that are required to follow the methodology. The chapter
is divided into 3 knowledge sections: planetary science (3.1), SAR theory (3.2.1) and image processing
13
==PAGE:15==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
techniques (3.3), each of them with their respective levels of subsections. All required theoretical content
regarding the current understanding of Venus, its surface processes, and particularly its inferred volcanism
can be found in the first section. The second section, the most extensive, deepens into the several facets
of SAR observation. General important traits, mathematical characterization, and the necessary steps to
obtain meaningful SAR images are described, focusing particularly on the SAR backscattering coefficient σ0.
Subsequently, several important aspects of 2 large topics of SAR theory are theoretically addressed; SAR
image reconstruction on one hand, and SAR change detection on the other. Their aspects relevant to the
thesis topic are approached. Finally, there are several resources in the field of image processing that have been
used in this work and don’t necessarily fall within SAR image processing only. These are 2-D interpolation
techniques, 2-D filtering techniques, and Geographic Information Systems (GIS), among others, and they
have been put together in the final theoretical section of chapter 3 (see 3.3).
The next chapter, chapter 4, thoroughly introduces all the datasets that have been used in the study, as well
as the missions that acquired them and their characteristics. It is structured mission by mission, and besides
data and mission description, each section describes the preprocessing that has been applied to the data,
tailored to both the data requirements and the goals of the work.
Following the data chapter, the bulk of the work performed is presented in chapter 5. Chapter 5 summarizes
the different studies performed in order to define and design the change detection strategy, and describes
their methodology and conclusions. The chapter first section provides the core arguments on the strategy
selection. Subsequently, the strategy is defined from an end-to-end perspective following a comprehensive
balance of the different objectives and the different challenges identified. After the strategy selection, all the
following sections of the chapter refer to the design and assessment of the different parts of the algorithm.
These are: Local incidence angle (section 5.3), Despeckling analysis (section 5.4), the preliminary design of
the Magellan-VISAR/VENSAR empirical model (sections 5.5 and 5.6), and finally the definition of the test
statistic probabilistic framework (section 5.7), including an assessment of the change detection performance
analysis.
Chapter 6 is the results chapter. These are the results of different combinations and scenarios that might
apply to SAR imaging comparison between Magellan and VISAR/VENSAR, and they offer a view and
quantification of the global performance of the change detection strategy fully studied in chapter 5. Even
though the particular results of each of the algorithm steps are given in Chapter 5, so to say ”out of context”,
the final results, i.e. the ones that try to satisfy in full the primary objectives described in 2.2, are delivered
here. The global performance assessment of the algorithm chain, and the quantification of the main possible
errors, can also be found here. Finally, after the work results, a conclusion summary is given in chapter 7,
and an enumeration and discussion of future steps in chapter 8. In the end of the study, a bibliographic
compilation of references is included, as well as the necessary appendixes to clarify or fully characterize
certain tasks performed throughout all the study.

==PAGE:16==
Theory
This chapter presents the necessary theoretical description of all the different knowledge, techniques and
topics that are required to follow the work’s methodology. Most of the topics are introduced as separated
and self-sufficient points, detached from the work’s methodology, since they will be used and referred to in a
practical way in chapter 5.
3.1
Planetary science
Planetary sciences encompass a variety of topics such as astronomy, atmospheric science, geology, space
physics, biology and chemistry [160]. In this MSc thesis case, it is pertinent to introduce specific knowledge
about the planet Venus; its general characteristics, its exploration history, and more in detail its surface
conditions, possible ongoing changes and, particularly, all the up-to-date volcanic-related knowledge that is
necessary to pursue the goals of the work.
3.1.1
Venus, the Earth’s twisted twin
Venus is the planetary body of the solar system that, when it comes to the main planetary variables, presents
the closest match with our own planet Earth [98]. The equatorial radius is 6051.80 km (94.90 % of the Earth’s),
its total mass amounts to 4.87 · 1024 kg (81.50 % the mass of the Earth) and its volume is 92.84 · 1010, which
represents the 85.70 % of the Earth’s volume [164]. Their mean density is also very similar, and because
of the mass and volume relationship, together with a number of observations from different missions and
different studies, it appears that their fractional chemical composition is also alike. Nevertheless, what in the
collective imaginarium preceding the space exploration era was seen as an Earth-like world, turned out to
present sharp and surprising differences with planet Earth. It is important to understand these differences
and how they couple together, as they are decisive factors that relate to the planet’s shocking surface and
atmospheric characteristics on one hand, and opportunities and constrains to the missions sent to Venus on
the other.
One significant difference between Venus and the Earth, and actually with all the planets of the Solar Sys-
tem except Uranus, is that it presents a retrograde rotation, meaning that it rotates clockwise (and not
anti-clockwise like the Earth) from the perspective of the Sun’s North Pole (the Sun in Venus rises in the
west and sets in the east). This contradicts the direction of the vast majority of the angular momentum
of the solar system, but studies suggest that terrestrial planets with very dense atmospheres can find it
energetically-efficient to evolve towards such a retrograde scenario if they have the majority of characteristics
that Venus possesses [56]. However, the rotation speed is very slow; in fact, it is the slowest of all the solar
system planets [98]. This makes the Venusian sidereal day (a full rotation around its rotational axis) equal
to 243.03 Earth’s sidereal days. The Venusian sidereal year (time required for a full orbit around the Sun)
amounts to 224.70 Earth sidereal days, which implies that days are longer than years in Venus. Such slow
rotation has far-reaching implications for the dynamics of the planet. To begin with, due to the lack of a
powerful centrifugal force, there is no equatorial bulge as on Earth, which implies that the planetary radius
is the same in the poles and in the equator. This in turn implies that the gravitational field is much more
15
==PAGE:17==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
uniform than on Earth, which prevents orbital configurations for space missions that make use of gravity
anomalies like Sun-synchronous orbits, and the benefits that come with them. In the same line, slow rotation
has implications for observational coverage; full surface coverage can take longer than on Earth because the
equatorial displacement is so low that it is difficult to map regions significantly apart in a short period of
time, and most times Venusian missions present full-coverage cycles that require a full Venusian day (see
missions description in chapter 4). Slow rotation transfers smalls amounts of energy that might mix chemical
components in both the atmosphere and the geological layers, and hence the energy and mass transfers of
the planet are affected. Perhaps one of the most noticeable consequences of the Venusian rotation is the
lack of magnetic field, particularly the main internal component of it, which on Earth is attributed to the
”Geodynamo” (the internal convection of the outer core and mantle). This makes the Venusian magnetic field
and influence virtually non-existent, at least compared to that of the Earth, and the only apparent magnetic
sources are external (ionospheric and magnetospheric contributions due to the interaction of the ionosphere
with the solar wind). The lack of magnetic field has been recently attributed to Venus presenting a different
internal structure than that of the Earth; instead of presenting few layers with sharp, defined boundaries,
the lack of internal convection within the boundaries could be attributed to a stratified disposition in the
core that effectively suppresses the necessary thermal convection [112]. Not having a magnetic field offers 1
observational variable less to extract information about the planet, but at the same time can be beneficial
for some space missions as there are few charged-particle events that can damage their systems.
On another hand, it is important for this work to highlight the surface and atmospheric conditions of Venus,
which are radically different from those of the Earth. The atmospheric pressure, temperature, and composi-
tion are extremely harsh for human life, and pose a significant hindrance to the planet’s direct exploration
of the surface, as man-made crafts with the current technology are also very vulnerable to them. The at-
mospheric conditions of Venus that are of relative importance to this work are summarized in the following
table:
Characteristics

Table 3.1: Venus atmospheric characteristics. Source: NASA [164].
The diurnal temperature range is almost zero, meaning that sun illumination conditions have little effect on
surface conditions. Also, the wind speed is very low, although due to the elevated atmospheric pressure it has
a much higher transport capacity than it would have on Earth [51]. The pressure in the surface is approxi-
mately 92 to 93 times higher to the one on Earth, and is the equivalent of the pressure found 1 km below the
Earth’s mean sea level [140]. The temperature is also very different, being 449 °C above the mean surface
temperature on Earth, which is 15 °C. Such conditions are capable of melting lead, which gives an idea of the
problematics that landers face on the planet. These conditions are fueled by an extremely high atmospheric
density, 54 times the Earth’s one. As seen in 3.1, the composition is mainly CO2, with presence of N2 and
traces of SO2. It is also worth mentioning that the clouds that are present in the atmosphere are most likely
composed by Sulfuric acid (H2SO4) [5], and that their variability can actually affect the interferometric
phase, in case of future interferometric observations made by VISAR [121]. Given the lack of vegetation and
human-related factors that can influence the atmosphere, such high concentration of these gases is mainly
attributed to volcanic activity, as they naturally come from eruptive processes or the combination of their
16
==PAGE:18==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
direct products. This is mainly supported by 2 factors: the observational evidence of a volcanically-shaped
surface [20], and the unimodal2 surface distribution, which argues against a global system of plate tectonics
[98]. If there are minimal or none tectonic dynamics, and a thick, rigid lithosphere 3 which in turn is a
consequence of slow rotation, the planetary capacity to recycle CO2 back into the subsurface is low, which
might explain the elevated molecular density of the gas in the atmosphere. The accumulation of these gases,
particularly CO2, throughout the planet’s eruptive history, bound by gravity, has, steadily over geological
times, achieved the current atmospheric pressure levels. On another hand, CO2 is a greenhouse gas, which
effectively captures infrared/heat energy emanating from a planet’s surface and keeps it and/or reflects it to
the planet’s surface and troposphere. At a certain point in time, the concentration of greenhouse gases in the
atmosphere, unable to be recycled, lead to a global runaway greenhouse effect which has effectively risen the
surface temperature to the nowadays mean value of 464 °C [140]. Pressure and temperature conditions are
expected to significantly alter the possible weathering, erosion and volcanic processes that could be taking
place in the surface, which is of critical importance to characterize the expected volcanism to be detected on
one hand, and to predict the radar observable characteristics that might change in time between acquisition
periods on the other.
The surface morphology and composition is another factor that is necessary to describe to provide a frame-
work for the different topics that are addressed throughout this study. Surface morphology is directly related
to what forces and changes might be currently happening in Venus. Given the uniform distribution of large
and middle-size meteorite impact craters, all the surface appears to possess the same age, being approximately
300 to 600 million years old. This has supported the so-called global resurfacing theory, which states that
the surface remains more or less stable during long periods of time but suffers episodic, catastrophic global
events that completely reshape it due to the accumulated pressure under the lithosphere eventually breaking
through [35]. The surface is divided between lowlands (90 % of the surface) and highlands. Highlands are
the only ”continental” masses of the planet, and they are elevated, highly deformed areas which are thought
to be driven by local plate tectonics [123]. They are referred to as Terrae, of which the biggest ones are
Ishtar Terra and Aphrodite Terra [86]. Nevertheless, the planet is in general dominated by plains and rolling
uplands of basaltic origin, with gentle slopes of a few degrees. Scattered around the surface, majoritarily on
the lowlands, there are a wide array of volcanic features, some of them unique to Venus, which are attributed
to hot-spot class volcanism [98]. These are shield volcanoes and hill domes, which are the most common
feature, and at the same time they often feature lava flows that extend to and through other volcanic features
[39], but there are also other constructs such as Pancake domes, Coronae and Arachnoids [17] (see figure 3.1).
Defined volcanoes are usually referred to as Mons, while small and morphological distinct areas are usually
referred to as Regiones [86] (Regio in singular). Some of the areas that are thought to be volcanically active,
due to the indirect observations of possible activity mentioned in 2.3, are the volcanoes Maat Mons, Ozza
Mons, Sapas Mons and Idunn Mons, with their surrounding rift areas. Nevertheless, clues of present volcanic
activities have been recorded in a great number of locations, particularly in the Themis and Dione regiones,
as well as in Lavignia Planitia. Certain sections of these locations have been used to simulate possible change
detection scenarios in chapter 6.
The morphology of the planet has been extensively studied, but the surface mineral composition remains more
difficult to map, particularly due to the difficulty of directly exploring the surface. Nevertheless, orbital and
in situ data from several soviet Venera [7] missions, the emissivity and radar backscattering maps retrieved by
the Magellan probe [26], and a few surface infrared brightness observations from the Venus Express mission
[81] and the Japanese Akatsuki mission [73] have given enough inside to provide a general picture, and the
conclusion is that rock silicates and metals conform the bulk of the surface composition, much like on Earth.
2The planetary radius has a one-peak distribution, as opposite to the Earth where the tectonic activity has produced a
continental and an oceanic disposition, making the surface distribution bi-modal.
3A rocky planet’s lithosphere encompasses the rocky outermost shell, geologically distinct from deeper layers. For Earth-like
planets this refers to the crust and parts of the upper-mantle.
17
==PAGE:19==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 3.1: Examples of Venusian volcanic constructs. Sapas Mons, an example of shield volcano in the Atla
Regio, with a noticeable cover of different lava flows (1). A concatenation of Pancake domes, Alpha Regio
(2). Aine Coronae formation in the south of Aphrodite Terra (3). An unusual formation Arachnoid, thought
to be not fully formed Coronae, in the Eistla Regio (4). Source: NASA/JPL [162].
.
This is one of the main reasons why Venusian volcanism is thought to present many similarities with that
of the Earth. The chemical composition, judging mainly from the Venera landings, is primarily basaltic,
conformed by Silicon dioxide (SiO2), Aluminium Oxide Alumina (Al2O3), and several other metallic oxides
such as Iron (II) oxide (FeO), Magnesium oxide (MgO) and Calcium Oxide quicklime (CaO) [96]. On top
of that, there is expected to be a significant lack of hydrated products, as no water signatures have been de-
tected. For the majority of the lowlands and basaltic regions, the composition is thought to be constant, but
for certain highland locations the inferred dielectric constant of the soil, detected by Magellan, is abnormally
high and significantly higher than the one found in the lowlands (≈20 −30), which indicates a different
composition, with most hypothesis leading to indications of metallic inclusions and sulphide material [98]
[140]. A more detailed analysis of the composition, particularly when it comes to the consequences it poses
to Venusian volcanism, is found in 3.1.3.2.
As introduced above, several missions have been sent to Venus in the last 70 years that have gathered
important information to understand the planet. The following section provides an overview to contextualize
the new VERITAS and EnVision missions and to understand where does the current available data come
from.
3.1.2
History of Venus exploration
All the current knowledge of Venus, such as the compilation of characteristics and behaviours exposed in
the previous section, has been gathered through centuries of observations, and particularly since the age of
interplanetary missions that initiated at the start of the second half of the twentieth century.
18
==PAGE:20==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
The first space mission to successfully obtain and transmit data from Venus was NASA’s Mariner 2 probe
[158], which performed a flyby of the planet in 1962. It was followed by other Mariner missions, particularly
Mariner 5 and 10, which obtained magnetic data and UV atmospheric images, respectively. Parallelly, the
Soviet Union Venera program delivered a series of successful landings (Venera 3 was the first spacecraft to
reach another planet’s surface), which probed the atmosphere and surface and provided data about their
composition, pressure and temperature. The Venera program had 8 successful landings, and the information
gathered included surface images, which are to date the only up-close view of the surface terrain [7] (providing
information about its morphology and mineralogy).
From the information gathered by the Venera missions, it became increasingly clear that landing missions on
Venus were difficult to be sustained in time due to the harsh surface conditions, and therefore most of the
following selected missions were orbiters that could produce coverage in a global-scale without being exposed
to the atmospheric conditions. Important to mention is NASA’s Pioneer Venus mission [163], which delivered
a suite of results from multiple experiments including radar-derived topography of 75 km resolution, mea-
surements of the gravity field and magnetic signatures, and spectrometer-derived atmospheric data. Pioneer
was followed by the last Venera missions, the orbiters number 15 and 16, which were a great success and
provided the first significant insights of the planet’s surface and geology [171]. These missions provided radar
radio altimetry data of 2.5 km resolution, and the first radar images of another planet (also the first to be
made by a synthetic aperture radar outside of Earth) with a 1-2 km resolution.
The successful Venera observations were followed, several years later on August 10th, 1990, by NASA’s
Magellan probe, to date the most successful mission sent to Venus, at least in terms of radar observations.
The data compiled by Magellan includes SAR imaging of 100-200 m, but also altimetry and stereo-derived
topography (1 km resolution), as well as brightness temperature from radiometry [16]. Since the Magellan
orbiter, there have been multiple flyby observations of the planet, but only 2 additional orbiters have been
delivered to provide science operations for a significant amount of time and coverage.
These are ESA’s
Venus Express mission [81], which included a suite of spectrometer instruments that have provided some of
the most significant evidences of active volcanism (see section 2.3), and the Japan Aerospace Exploration
Agency (JAXA) probe Akatsuki, which is currently orbiting the planet since 2015 (after a 5-year delay due
to a failed orbital inception manoeuvre), and provides atmospheric data.
The history of the planet’s exploration is expected to go through an order of magnitude change in the next
years, with the quasi-simultaneous arrival of the new missions (there are currently 5 approved missions to
be sent to Venus, including orbiters and atmospheric probes). The Magellan mission and its data products,
which are the main data products of this MSc thesis, are addressed in detail in section 4.1.
Figure 3.2: Panorama captured by the rear camera of the soviet Venera 14 lander in 1982, showing a basaltic-
like soil. Source: Russian Academy of Sciences [169].
3.1.3
Venusian surface changes
In the framework of the characteristics and the observational history of Venus, the most important topic to
elaborate for this work is what is the nature of possible surface changes. It is important, particularly from a
19
==PAGE:21==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
radar perspective, to understand the physics and the characteristics of the changes that are looking-forward
to be detected. Knowledge about the composition, morphology, timeliness and extent of the changes is im-
perative to assure that they are capable of being detected on one hand, and on the other, which specific radar
signatures are expected to be recorded from them. This last point is critical to design a change detection
methodology, as is explained in section 5.1. In this section, all pertinent knowledge about the Venusian
surface and its changes is provided.
The Magellan SAR imaging products provided the best to-date information about the Venusian surface.
Since no meaningful direct changes by means of image intercomparison have ever been recorded (except for
the Venera 13 landing cameras, which recorded sediments being removed from the lander during a 40 minutes
period), all possible changes to occur in the surface are hypothetical. Nevertheless, they are all logical ob-
servations that follow the characteristics of the surface imaged by Magellan in conjunction with atmospheric
data gathered by other missions.
There exist multiple studies about the Venusian surface and its possible changes [4] [19] [147]. Because of
the dense atmosphere, micro and small meteorite bombardment in the surface is thought to be prevented
in all detectable scenarios. The lack of water prevents, in turn, a water-cycle erosion of the surface, and
there are also no detected bio-signatures that could come from life-driven changes. Nevertheless, because of
the high surface temperatures combined with the dense and acidic atmosphere, there should exist significant
weathering and erosion cycles on Venus, albeit there is not enough information for their change rates to
be constrained yet. Sediments from weathered craters and highly deformed terrain created by tectonic and
volcanic activity should be present, and the wind should have enough strength for wind transportation to
have a significant role in surface dynamics [119]. On another hand, multiple evidences exist of volcanic and
tectonic activity, and particularly volcanism is widespread present and could produce significant, detectable
changes of the surface. Therefore, there are 2 mechanisms that could a priori be targets for SAR change
detection: Aeolian transportation and Volcanic eruptions.
Since relatively few wind-driven surface features were detected in the Magellan survey[36], and comparatively
the array of observed volcanic features was exuberant [17], with altogether the magnitude difference in the
scale of the changes that volcanism is known to induce compared to aeolian processes, at least on Earth,
makes volcanic changes a much likeable target from a change detection perspective. In the following points,
the possible changes related to these processes are overviewed and described by means of the information
that has been gathered from the data.
3.1.3.1
Sedimentary and weathering processes
Due to their internal composition, surface geology, and either the presence of atmospheric layers or by means
of interaction with space weather and hazards (or both), terrestrial planets exhibit erosion and weathering
processes in their surface [98]. The extent to which these processes shape and influence the surface depends
on the surface conditions, namely the capacity of such conditions to erode and create sediments in one hand,
and the possibilities of transport that they create in the other. In the case of Venus, even if the surface main
features are of volcanic origins, there is evidence of surface weathering. Four Venera landers revealed that the
basaltic plains they had landed upon were dominated by clastic 4 rocks and mobile sediments, and studies of
reprocessed Magellan images have estimated that half of the planet’s surface (mainly the plains) are covered
by sedimentary rocks [148]. The predominant sources of sedimentation appear to be upland erosion and the
mass wasting of steep slopes; thereafter the measured wind speed should be able to allow global transport of
the generated sand-sized granular material, which would be deposited mainly in the plains (which represent
most of the surface) and gradually lithified 5 by the surface pressure as layered and thin, clastic rocks.
4Sedimentary rocks conformed by fragments of pre-existing minerals broken down by physical weathering.
5Process of compaction and solidification of sediments, usually driven by high pressures, to become solid rock.
20
==PAGE:22==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Sedimentation processes are likely to have long timescales, and they probably happen similarly to a marine
environment on Earth due to the elevated surface pressure [114]. Aeolian transportation typical features
such as dune fields and wind streaks 6 around craters have been found in Venus [19]. Nevertheless, only
2 dune fields are currently identified by means of Magellan SAR imagery: Menat Undae (24.8° S 339.4°
E), also called Aglaonice dune field, and Al-Uzza Undae (67.7° N 90.5° E), also known as the Fortuna-
Meshkenet dune field. This can be an observational bias due to the Magellan’s geometry and resolution [36],
but to a greater extent evidences that even if surface weathering happens in a global scale, surface features
characteristically shaped by aeolian transportation processes are of minor importance, and most weathering
acts to erode volcanic features such as lava plains, lava flows and typical Venus volcanic constructs (see 3.1.3).
This last point is of particular interest to this project, because when aiming at the detection of changing
lava flows, and hence the radar signatures that they generate, weathering effects that might alter the surface
properties within the temporal baseline of the observations need to be constrained. In other words, elevated
erosion and weathering of hypothetical new/fresh lava flows, as well as of the old ones and other surrounding
terrain, during the 40-year temporal baseline between Magellan and EnVision/VERITAS, might produce
changes in the radar observations that might hinder the detection of changes. Unfortunately, it is precisely
the observation of surface features at different times that can serve to constrain the rates of such changes;
hence, the ability to detect lava flow changes, without constraining significant weathering activity that might
be acting upon them, can be considerably reduced.
Even though more data is required to constrain the rates and timescales of weathering and erosion [133],
some constraints can already be estimated from the existing multiple observations from Magellan. Specifi-
cally, according to [19], Venusian lava flows, regardless of their physical roughness and how fresh they are
(considering mafic composition), should evolve towards a radar dark smooth signature after approximately
1 meter depth from the surface has been altered/weathered. Independently of all the possible erosion effects
involved and if they have changed through time, and according to observational data, no more than a ∼1
meter of modification has occurred over the last 0.6 billion years, mainly due to mechanical and chemical
erosion rather than aeolian transportation [19]. Therefore, the erosion of lava surfaces (and by extension
other surfaces) leading to radar-detectable changes should be minimal within a 40 year timespan. This is an
assumption that has been taken when designing the algorithm’s change detection method: in the eventuality
of no volcanic-related change (for instance no new or advancing lava flow), the natural surface characteristics
of volcanic landscapes are preserved to a degree that no observable radar changes can be detected.
3.1.3.2
Volcanic processes
Volcanism is the main driving force shaping the Venusian surface [11] [17]. Radar observations revealed an
array of surface features, some unique to Venus, that can be formed by a variety of volcanic processes, and
that if active, might be producing eruptive processes to date. Evidence from Venera [4] and Magellan [20]
radar images suggest that volcanic processes, mostly effusive in eruption type and hot-spot in origin, are
majority, with several local tectonic-induced volcanic features detectable as well. Volcanism can be intrusive
or extrusive; intrusive if magma slowly solidifies under the surface, and extrusive if igneous landforms are
formed in the surface after an eruptive process. Both intrusive and extrusive volcanism are present in Venus,
but from the radar change detection perspective, only extrusive volcanism is detectable within the considered
observational timespan, as it produces visible surface changes. Due to the variety of differences between the
Earth’s and Venus in the surface, atmospheric and planetary stage, Venusian volcanism is more likely to be
effusive [96], producing Hawaiian-style eruptions with outpourings of molten lava. Nevertheless, explosive
volcanism should be possible at high elevations [96], and observations of pyroclastic deposits, which are typical
of explosive eruptions, have been proposed in the literature [99]. Explosive volcanism is more ”destructive”,
in the sense that it is more likely to affect the overall geometry and consistency of the scene around the
6Wind-shaped structures happening when dust interacts with hardened terrain such as hills, mountains or raised crater rims
[140].
21
==PAGE:23==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
particular eruptive volcanic construct. Therefore, from the radar perspective, changes are more localized,
and their evolution quantifiable, if the eruption is an effusive process, because besides advancing lava, usually
the majority of the scene remains unchanged (if there is no lava-domes collapse or seismic activity). Because
of this reason, together with the overwhelming image evidence of effusive processes, lava flows were selected
as the main change detection target (see 2). The next points deepen into characterizing the possible effusive
eruptions that can be detected by Magellan and VERITAS/EnVision image intercomparison.
3.1.3.2.1
Studies and evidence
The Magellan initial volcanic assessment [17] revealed numerous volcanic constructs with characteristics con-
sistent with previous observations of the Venera radars and Pioneer Venus. The analysis of imagery of great
plains and uplands, together with the analysis performed by the Venera landers, are consistent with tholeiitic
and alkali 7 basalt. Molten basaltic lava (previous to the solidification and formation of the basaltic rock)
possesses low viscosity due to its relatively low Silica content (between 45 and 52 %), and hence it tends to
come from effusive volcanic eruptions such as the ones found on Venus. There are also evidences, particularly
from the Venera 8 site, of igneous rocks with an intermediate andesitic and rhyolitic composition (presenting
higher silica content than basalt), which are usually associated with explosive volcanism rather than effusive,
but the bulk of observations are consistent with mafic lava eruptions. Certain locations, usually around large
shield volcanoes that are surrounded by smooth planes, present a higher number and variety of visible lava
flows, which makes them specially interesting to perform detection analysis, particularly if they have been
associated with the indirect evidences of volcanism exposed in 2.3.
Lava flows in Venus cover large areas, sometimes creating fields larger than 50,000 km2. They can also be
extremely long; in Venus there is the longest lava channel observed in the solar system, with a total length
of ∼6800 km and an average thickness of 1-3 km, which suggests that lava in Venus can be extremely fluid
(low-viscosity) [156]. Lavas have been classified into 5 morphological types, but their main morphological
distinction is whether they are sheet-like or digitate-like [39]. Sheet-like flows are larger, thicker, and usu-
ally associated with long laterally-extending fissures. They are thought to be the origin of most of the vast
basaltic plains of the planet, which have been homogenized by erosion throughout geological times. On the
other hand, digitate lava flows are smaller, narrower, presenting multiple shapes, and are usually found close
to prominent volcanic features such as shield volcanoes. From the radar point of view, it is very important
to characterize the physical roughness of the lavas; morphologically, a lava is usually classified as Pahoehoe
(from the Hawaiian, ’Smooth’) or Aa, which presents a rough or rubbly surface composed of broken lava
blocks called clinker 8.
The analysis suggests that both sheet-like and digitate lava flows present similar morphological characteristics
with Pahoehoe lava flows or smoother; nevertheless, digitate flows exhibit more surface variability, sometimes
being closer to terrestrial Aa lava flows [39]. For most of the change detection scenarios, digitate flows have
been selected as the main target, as they are, on one hand, the types found in most of the locations that are
likely to be active, and on the other, the most interesting to be detected from the robustness point of view,
because they are usually smaller and more shape-complex.
The next figure 3.3 is an example of Magellan SAR imagery accompanied by volcanographic and topographic
information, from the Sif Mons area, which is helpful to visualize the different volcanic terrains and the lava
types. It serves as an example of the types of change detection scenarios that have been evaluated in this
work; particularly, this area has also been tested for change detection in 6.2.
7Tholeiitic basalt are rich in iron and poor in alkali metals, and are usually associated on Earth with oceanic basin floors,
while alkali basalt are on the other hand rich in alkali metals and aluminum.
8Hawaiian terminology is used to name a variety of volcanic terms because Hawaii presents some of the best terrestrial
conditions to study volcanism, particularly the effusive eruptions of the Kilauea and Mauna Loa volcanoes. These locations
have been used to tune in EO data analysis in section 5.6.2.

==PAGE:24==

Figure 3.3: Extract of the Sif Mons analysis performed in [17]. (A) shows the Magellan altimetry-derived
(and post processed) topography of the region; the 2 highest peaks are Sif Mons (right) and Gula (left). (B)
shows the cross-section elevation profile of the area, where it can be seen how the lava flows are emplaced in
the relatively gentle slopes that develop right next to the main volcanic cone. (C) is the SAR image product of
the location (see 4.1 for more information), which is the main evidence of the intricate mosaic of digitate flows
that are emplaced around the volcano. The fact that they exhibit radar-bright signatures can be associated
