[86].
3.1.3.2.2
Eruption models and constrains
Important efforts have been done in order to mathematically constrain the volcanic eruption properties in
Venus, and to create a model that might be helpful to describe its processes and that fits observational data
[29] [11]. Volcanic deposits provide information about the relation of volcanism and tectonism, the eruption
style, and the chemistry and volatile content of the eruptions. The model of an eruption from the sub-surface
accumulation of magma until the solidification of the ejected products provides tools to understand present
and future observations of the Venusian surface. Particularly, eruption type and post-eruptive processes are
heavily influenced by gravity, temperature, and atmospheric characteristics.
One of the main products that an eruptive model can provide, if well constructed, is the estimation of the
eruption rate (effusion rate for effusive eruptions). The effusion rate of a lava flow is mainly predicted by
23
==PAGE:25==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
the width of the fissure/conduit of eruption and by the rheology 9 of the lithospheric magma [11], but the
details of the eruption style are determined by sub-surface processes a few meters from the eruption ground
level. The details linked with the particular style of eruptions expected on Venus, and how do they compare
with Earth, are given in the next section, but if considering effusive, expanding, mafic lava flows as the likely
scenario, eruptive models can serve to constrain how much lava is likely to erupt in a specific context, and
therefore how much lava can be emplaced in a certain location, which is the quantifiable measurement that
can be performed with change detection. The precise quantification of emplaced/erupted lava in a planetary
scale gives information about the heat and mass transfer between the planet’s interior and surface, which
ultimately serves to settle the planet’s internal dynamics and geological history.
Therefore, the a priori
modelling or constraining of the effusion rates of Venusian eruptions is an important step towards the science
goals of the planetary science community.
The model by Head and Wilson of 1986 [11] is generally used as the basis model. In case of extrusive, effusive
volcanism, the model links the effusion rate with the length, the surface morphology and the cooling rate
of any detected lava flow, which is in turn a function of the array of factors and variables that conform the
interior and surface conditions of the planet. In general, the thermal state of the lava is the main factor
that determines its length and morphology. The model is not introduced in detail as it has not been used
in this work, neither was it necessary to fulfil the goal of designing a SAR change detection strategy, but
the ability to consider a specific effusion rate to constrain how much lava has been emplaced in a particular
detected change scenario is assured by it. Hence, it is possible to say that, given the detection of a new lava
flow emplacement, Venusian eruptive models (with the aid of observations) can be used to infer the erupted
volumes of lava, which reassures meaningful scientific results from change detection observations.
Figure 3.4: Lava flow cooling mechanisms, as described by the Head & Wilson model (1986). Heat escapes
moving lava by conduction with the ground surface, by radiation of all its surfaces, by natural convection
with the atmospheric air, and by forced-convection in case of wind. The altogether efficiency of these methods
defines the channel’s length and the surface morphology (Pahoehoe, Aa, etc.). It is important to mention
that a lava flow’s core can flow for longer periods of time than its crust (which deforms the crust and creates
different morphologies depending mainly on the effusion rate), and that sometimes creates the so-called lava
tubes (the crust solidifies while the interior keeps flowing, which in the end leaves a void in the middle). Lava
tubes do not necessarily influence the radar signatures at the wavelengths of the considered missions to Venus,
but they can lead to an overestimation of lava volume, and hence their occurrence should be incorporated to
the Venusian eruption model used with EnVision and VERITAS. Source: Head & Wilson [11].
The model finds that, under a series of assumptions, differences and similarities with the Earth’s volcanism
9Rheology is the science of deformation of materials, and in the field of volcanism refers mainly to the relationship between
viscosity, temperature and shear stress of magmas.
24
==PAGE:26==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
(see 3.1.3.2.3), and according to (pre-Magellan) lava flow observations, effusion rates in Venus should be in
general larger than those found on Earth, of the order of 104/105 m3
s . The model results suggest that due
to the Venusian surface conditions, the minimum magma volume necessary to produce surface eruptions in
Venus must be larger than on Earth, and hence for a given effusive rate an eruption can deliver more lava,
which partially explains the long longitudes of the observed lava channels.
With the range of eruption rates assumed by the model (given above), which can be later on corrected
with observations, and a detected change corresponding to a specific area covered with a new lava flow, the
duration and volume of a Venusian eruption can be constrained [11].
3.1.3.2.3
Differences and similarities with Earth’s volcanism
This section exposes and describes the majority of characteristics of the Venusian eruptions, and to contex-
tualize them, compares them with the analogue ones on Earth.
As predicted by models and corroborated by observations, Venus has a majority of effusive volcanic eruptions.
The eruption process and type is heavily affected by the surface conditions and the planetary variables [11].
Venus has a slightly lower gravity than Earth (8.41 m
s ) [164], which lowers the pressure and column momentum
flux when magmas ascend volcanic conduits [96]. The main surface differences with Earth are temperature,
pressure, and atmospheric composition. High temperatures in the surface inhibit volcanic explosivity by re-
ducing the temperature contrast between the ascending column (magma) and the eruption fissure [96]. The
strong surface pressure, on another hand, inhibits magma fragmentation due to vesiculation, which makes
magma more homogeneous and prevents the formation of gas bubbles or deposits that lead to explosivity.
Also, the total lack of water vapor in the atmosphere reduces buoyancy, which further reduces the expansion
of volcanic plumes in case of explosive volcanism. All these factors lead to a dominance of effusive, lava
flow eruptions, compared to Earth. Nevertheless, these factors are strongly attitude-dependent, and there
is argumentation in favor of explosive volcanism on Venus at high altitudes [96], also observationally since
pyroclastic deposits have been identified [99].
On another hand, the similarities between Earth and Venus in a planetary level, particularly in the bulk of
their mineralogical composition, are expected to lead to similarities in magma rheology and bulk-density.
The response of magmas to shearing stresses, which has an important impact on eruption processes, can
be quantified by means of rheological parameters such as the yield strength and the plastic viscosity [11],
which are in turn a function of magma composition and temperature.
For the most part, the magma
rheological properties in Venus are thought to be equivalent to terrestrial magmas of tholeiitic and alkali
basaltic composition. The main compositional difference that might affect the equivalence is the fact that
magmas on Venus are thought to be water depleted, and carbon dioxide enriched. On one hand, both of
these factors lead to magma sources being further away from the surface than on Earth, but the effects that
this poses might be balanced with other rheological aspects related to the very high surface temperature. At
the same time, the lack of liquid water, water-ice or other water-based crustal compounds argues strongly
against explosivity, since such activity is often driven by the decomposition/evaporation of surface and
subsurface volatiles close to the vent where magma rises, and on Earth the main compound driving this
behavior is water [11]. This can be partially compensated by the more dominant volatile role of CO2 in
Venus, which is also a driver of explosivity, but the modelling suggests that in general the balance of these
factors tends towards effusive eruption activity. In conclusion, according to [11] and putting together the
mentioned aspects, Hawaiian/effusive-style eruptions on Venus are dominant, explosivity is minimized at
most surface elevations, and the cocktail of surface and atmospheric factors, together with the characteristics
of the Venusian lithosphere, make eruptions smaller, fountains or plumes narrower and hotter, delivering
punctual pyroclastic and ash material closer, and the flowing of lava more likely on Venus than on Earth.
25
==PAGE:27==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 3.5: Layout comparison of Hawaiian activity on Earth and on Venus. Fire fountain eruptive activity
is typical of this type of volcanism, which coexists or pre-dates the formation of basaltic low-viscosity lava
flows, which are the main trait of this eruption type. In the layout, it can be seen how the already small
explosivity of this stage, which produces almost no ash or pyroclastic material, is significantly reduced on
Venus compared to Earth. Source: Head & Wilson [11].
From the SAR change detection perspective, characterizing the eruption moment is important for 2 reasons:
it indicates until which point the eruptive process itself might disrupt the underlying scene before any lava
flow emplacement, and it allows characterizing the magma and lithospheric properties in the eventuality that
new lava flows are identified and quantified. The conclusions above foresee minimal disruption (explosivity is
usually followed by landfall, pyroclasts, ash and even seismic activity, which might alter or cover up the flow-
ing of lava), and reassure that magma and eruptive properties can be identified if new lava flows are detected.
The full characterization of the eruption process is important, but from the perspective of this work, more
important is the characterization of the flowing lava during and after the eruption itself. As already stated,
both observational data and modelling predict a tholeiitic or alkali (mafic) composition. As stated, thermal
state (for cooling mechanisms, see figure 3.4) controls lava morphological evolution, surface texture and max-
imum flowing length. The modelling conclusions of [11], which are supported by experimentation, indicate
that for approximately the first hour of the flow after leaving the eruption vent, the temperature of the flow
surface is lower in Venus than on Earth, particularly because natural and forced convection are very efficient
in the planet due to high atmospheric density. The crust that forms on the external layers of the flow is also
thicker on Venus, but only during the first 30 minutes; from that moment on, the crust thickness is lower and
stabilizes more rapidly than on Earth. From the outcome of the cooling processes, it follows that, if the lava
travels less than an hour, its length will be shorter than a similar scenario on Earth, while if the travelling
is maintained for longer, lava flows on Venus can flow to much longer distances than their Earth counterparts.
Since for most of the observed effusive eruptions on Earth, eruption times are longer than an hour, and
26
==PAGE:28==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
considering the expected Venusian effusion rates (see section 3.1.3.2.2) and the sizes of the vents observed
in Magellan data, the amounts of lava produced will take most times longer than an hour to cool down and
solidify, the expected new flows to be detected on Venus should be larger and longer, at least compared to
the same conditions on Earth. This implies that there is a high detection chance, from a radar perspective,
of a new flow if an eruption within the Magellan-EnVision/VERITAS 40-year temporal baseline occurs; new
flows should be meaningful enough compared to the underlying scene. When it comes to the physical surface
morphology of the lava, which is strongly related to the radar backscatter coefficient (see 5.1), the change
of viscosity of the flow due to cooling, the topographic obstacles, and the effusion rate of the flow are key
[11]. When considering the relationship between the several variables, if magma rheology is considered equal
on Venus than on Earth (which for the most part it is believed to be), then there shouldn’t be significant
differences between the occurrence and formation processes of Pahoehoe and Aa lava on Venus. In general,
the model concludes that transitions from Pahoehoe flows to Aa flows should occur at times one third ear-
lier on Venus (closer to the vent) than on Earth. For the slope values around most shield volcanoes and
thought-to-be-eruptive areas, the considered effusion rates should generate long (slow-cooling) lavas, with
crustal textures which can be very smooth closer to the vents in general, and further away if the fissures are
wide and the slopes are low (for instance in volcanic plains), and ranging from typical Pahoehoe and Aa lava
flows the further away from the eruption point. Particularly for the Venusian case, the model and the radar
observations suggest that most Pahoehoe flows develop their ropy structure closer to vents, being therefore
larger and better developed than on Earth.
With this, the theory on how eruptions and lava flows happen on Venus is concluded. The particularities of
the eruption processes that might happen within the observation timeline of this work have been defined, as
well as the particularities of lava flows in Venus and how their expected changes might physically look like if
captured by the new missions. Moreover, the importance of this knowledge from the radar and the planetary
science perspective has also been highlighted.
3.2
SAR theory
Synthetic aperture radar is the backbone of this study, and therefore the several main topics that are necessary
for radar change detection are theoretically introduced here. First, the basics of SAR images, particularly their
statistical nature and how they can be mathematically characterized, are introduced. Thereafter, particular
emphasis is put in the description of the radar backscatter coefficient σ0, the main radar observable used in
this work, and the several factors that influence its value. Finally, the 2 main topics that are necessary for
the methodology, SAR image reconstruction and SAR change detection, are addressed.
3.2.1
SAR data background
Synthetic aperture radar was first developed in 1951, and the first spacecraft incorporating one was NASA’s
satellite mission SEASAT in 1978. The first interplanetary mission carrying a SAR was actually the Ven-
era 15 probe in 1983. Spaceborne SAR operates in the microwave spectrum due to power constraints and
because of the need to penetrate through the otherwise optically-thick atmosphere. Both the Earth’s and
Venus atmospheres are partially or totally opaque to frequencies starting from the infrared spectrum and
higher, due to atmospheric absorption and scattering effects. Therefore, SAR is a suitable remote sensing
technique that is able to see into the planet’s surface without significance atmospheric interference.
A SAR, when it acts as a spaceborne imaging sensor (SAR processing can also be applied to altimeters),
is a coherent side-looking radar system which makes use of the orbital flight path of the platform (a satel-
lite or a space probe) to simulate an extremely large antenna/aperture, what is called a synthetic aperture
[173].Because of this, it is able to obtain much finer azimuth resolutions than conventional radar systems,
with the theoretical limit of half of the antenna length. The return time and position of the echoes can be
geometrically associated with distance, which allows SAR (and also conventional radars) to discern scatterers
27
==PAGE:29==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
10 in the range direction (the looking direction).
Throughout the acquisition time, individual transmit/receive cycles, governed by a pulse repetition frequency,
are completed and stored. Each of the received signals possess a magnitude and a phase value, and when
adjacent cycles are combined, while taking into account their discriminating Doppler effect due to their dif-
ferent transmitter-target geometry, high-resolution complex images can be generated [64] [173]. For SAR
signal processing to work, precise knowledge of the orbital position and velocity are required, as well as great
pointing accuracy and a good-enough calibration process. The performance and observational capacities of
a spaceborne SAR system are constrained by the mission goals on one hand, and by the platform’s capabil-
ities (size, weight, power consumption, etc.) on the other. Particularly important for interplanetary SAR
instruments such as the ones in the Venera probes or in the Magellan is the balance between data acquisition
and data transmission rate, as sending data back to Earth can be a challenging operation due to difficult
interplanetary viewing geometry.
From SAR processing, several types of images can be produced from the initial information. Depending on
your scientific goals, certain SAR information can be more useful than another. The next section describes
the several types of information that a SAR system provides, making particular emphasis on the ones that
are important to this MSc thesis.
3.2.1.1
Introduction to SAR images
Turning a SAR 2-dimensional set of measurements, which is a geometrically and radiometrically calibrated
dataset of the scattering properties of a specific scene on a planet’s surface, into information that is mean-
ingful to the goals of the work, is of paramount importance. Each pixel of a SAR image gives, essentially,
information of the electromagnetic interaction between a transmitted wave and the imaged scene [64]. This
information is not direct but rather undergoes intense processing, including the effects of finite bandwidths
in the azimuth (orbit-track) and range dimensions and numerous weightings in order to transform the fun-
damental electromagnetic quantities measured (a pair of voltages corresponding to each pixel’s phase and
quadrature channels) into meaningful geophysical quantities.
Fundamentally, the simplest form of SAR image is a complex-valued image with a real and an imaginary part.
If taking a sufficiently wide sample of pixels, these parts appear Gaussian distributed. Such data distribution
has been proven not to be very useful if trying to infer information from the images in an automatized way,
for instance, to discern between a target and another according to the distribution’s standard deviation [64].
Nevertheless, complex values can also be represented in polar coordinates by an amplitude value, which is
the absolute value of the real and the imaginary parts, and a phase value, which provides information about
the orientation of the amplitude in a 2-D plane. If the SAR image is not used for any technique that requires
information of the phase difference between observations, interferometry for instance, or phase differences
between polarimetric channels if they are available, and if the scene to be imaged possesses distributed targets
(as all natural targets do), then the phase carries no information to be exploited as it is not a function of
a target’s characteristics. The amplitude, on the other hand, can be used to extract information about the
several targets of a scene.
The fact that for distributed targets on homogeneous or quasi-homogeneous areas, SAR images follow math-
ematical distributions, is a critical equivalence that carries significant information, and has to do with the
nature of the image acquisition. The mean value of a distribution is the value that defines the mean behavior
of a scene, which can be then extrapolated in the right context to the retrieval of a geophysical value of
interest. The pixels that are close to the mean value give information about which areas of the scene behave
accordingly, while the ones with values significantly displaced might indicate different behavior. The reason
why SAR image variables are distributed is due to a SAR noise-like particularity called Speckle, which is
10Units of radar targets that scatter the incident power back (backscatter), generating a radar echo/response.
28
==PAGE:30==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
described in the next section.
Amplitude information of a homogeneous-looking area has been observed to follow a Rayleigh distribution,
which has rather a wide spectrum. On another hand, the squared amplitude, which is referred to as the in-
tensity, follows an exponential distribution for homogeneous areas, and the log (with base 10) of the intensity
usually provides the best data visualization because of its reduced dynamic range of values 11.
Therefore, from the starting complex-valued pixels with real and imaginary parts that are proportional to
the voltage recorded in the radar electronics, there are commonly 4 types of images, associated with the
variables amplitude, phase, intensity and log intensity, which can be used to perform analysis. The SAR
intensity (and also its logarithmic version) is usually the main variable of interest when extracting information
from a SAR scene (if phase information is discarded) [64]. Nevertheless, the measured intensity is usually
converted to a proportional geophysical parameter, the so-called radar cross-section (RCS), identified as σ,
which standardizes the intensity/power measurement by taking into account the specific characteristics of the
radar and the scene. The identity that performs this transformation is called the radar equation (assuming
the form taken for monostatic radar12) [144]:
σ =
(4π)3R4Pr
Ptλ2G(ϕ, θ)2
(m2)
(3.1)
where Pt and Pr are power (intensity) transmitted and received respectively, λ is the wavelength associated
with the signal’s carrier frequency, R represents the distance (range) to a specific point on ground, and G(ϕ, θ)
is the 2-dimensional gain pattern of the radar antenna. σ represents the adimensional power backscattered
by a target with a specific area (its units are m2). When considering point targets, RCS is an adequate radar
signature, but when measuring natural targets, which are often distributed, the variable that it usually used
is the backscatter coefficient σ0 (also referred to as sigma naught or normalized RCS), which normalizes the
RCS by its area:
σ0 =
σ
Area
(3.2)
The use of RCS and, by extension, of the backscatter coefficient, allows retrieving the same radar backscat-
tered power from a scene independently of the radar and the acquisition characteristics (if the measurement
process is well calibrated); this is why it is the main suitable variable for SAR image analysis, and it is the
main variable analyzed throughout this work. On another hand, the backscatter coefficient is radiometri-
cally calibrated by the radar and the observation geometry parameters (equation 3.1), but radiometry is also
influenced by the pixel position in itself [88]. In fact, the RCS of a pixel can be normalized to 3 different
backscatter coefficients, not just one, depending on the projection of the area used to perform the normal-
ization: β0, σ0 and γ0. β0 is the nomenclature given to the backscatter coefficient if the RCS normalizing
area of each pixel is given in the radar slant-range plane 13 rather than the ground. This implies that the
backscatter properties are not affected by local topography (and hence, not compensated by it), and it is
usually used if no terrain model for ground projection is available. Generally, the scientific community prefers
σ0 projection when possible, and the models required to obtain it can be from a simple ellipsoid mathemati-
cal model (when data is lacking) to a high-resolution topography map derived from altimetry, for example.
There is also another possible backscatter coefficient projection called γ0, which normalizes the RCS with the
pixel area projected in the plane perpendicular to the slant-range direction. However, this one is not used in
this work. The radar observation and the backscatter coefficient geometry can be visualized in the following
figure 3.6:

3.2.1.2
Speckle
Speckle, the so-called ”salt and pepper” effect, is the fundamental characteristic of SAR images that separates
them different from optical images. Speckle makes the a priori underlying and deterministic RCS values
associated with different targets to be ”blurred”, observed instead with a certain degree of variability that is
mathematically seen as a probability distribution. The reason to be of speckle lies in that, for a finite imaged
resolution cell, the interference of a number of natural scatterers (point targets that to a certain level reflect or
interact with incident electromagnetic energy) within it might add up to different behaviors; this implies that,
for instance for contiguous pixels imaging the same underlying target, their backscattered power might differ.
This fluctuation characterizes the different SAR images, and it is behind many target detection problems
(see section 3.2.3), but for the vast majority of natural clutter, it can be described by a mathematically
quantifiable stochastic process. For single-channel SAR data, speckle is usually modelled as a multiplicative
noise; the SAR image, for instance an RCS image, is seen as the multiplication of a deterministic RCS field of
values with a stationary noise-like process. The outcome is a variable that, under a homogeneous distribution
of targets such as the one usually presented by natural clutter, follows a mathematical distribution like one
of those presented in 3.2.1.1.
30
==PAGE:32==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 3.7: Comparison between a Sentinel-2 high resolution optical image (left) and a Sentinel-1 high-
resolution SAR image (right). The fluctuating/granulated nature of the SAR images due to speckle is visible
in the SAR image in contrast to the optical one. Source: European Space Agency [151].
The fact that SAR σ0 scenes are not deterministic and fluctuate to a certain extent due to speckle is of critical
importance to this work. SAR image reconstruction of the underlying RCS is necessary mainly because of
this fact (see 3.2.2), and the difficulty of delineating changes without introducing a certain degree of error is
also mainly driven by this. These are core challenges of Magellan-VISAR/VENSAR image intercomparison,
particularly because the speckle-driven distributions of their images differ, as they have different characteris-
tics. The mathematical characterization of the backscatter coefficient for practical purposes, considering the
effects of Speckle, is given in the following point.
3.2.1.3
SAR backscatter coefficient: mathematical characterization
As stated before, σ0 is a particular radar observable derived from SAR intensity/power acquisition and ra-
diometrically projected on ground (which is not the same as geocoding, see section 3.2.1.5). For single-looked
images of homogeneous areas, that, is, that don’t present differences in the structural disposition of scatterers
across the scene 14, σ0 follows a negative exponential distribution. Due to the variability of speckle, it is
often required for most applications to perform a form of averaging operation in order to reduce the variance
of the data while preserving the mean value. Averaging an ensemble of observations narrows variability,
although it also reduces the effective resolution of the scene by a similar factor. The most simple averaging
operation is often called Multi-looking, with each look being an observation to be averaged. Multi-looking
is an incoherent averaging (discards phase information) that can be performed on-board by splitting the
SAR Doppler bandwidth during SAR processing [64], a term often referred to as angular Multi-looking in
SAR imaging, and it can also be performed on-ground in the post-processing stage. This second form of
Multi-looking is the most typical, and it averages spatially contiguous pixels, under the assumption that they
possess the same underlying RCS value, as if they would be independent observations of the same pixel. In
general, SAR data processing might use a combination of both, depending on the mission.
A single-look intensity image I such as σ0 is defined as:
P(I) = 1

(3.3)
where the variable I refers to the different σ0 values that a scene can take and σ acts here as the mean
backscatter value of the scene as a whole, which defines the distribution. The incoherent averaging of L
number of looks of an intensity value I is defined as:

they refer unless stated otherwise to σ0 backscatter coefficient, since it is the variable found in the data (see 4).
This mathematical behavior is idealized but, observationally, has proven to work well for most naturally-
distributed scenes. Nevertheless, the mathematical behavior is not accurate and might change in the presence
of fluctuations that are not a consequence of speckle but rather a consequence of the physical variability of the
natural clutter being imaged. These fluctuations, which can also be modelled as an additional multiplicative
noiselike component, are usually referred to as texture. Texture is present in many natural targets such as
forests, fields, and also lava flows, including lava flows on Venus (see 5.1.2). Its modelling adds another stage
of complexity to equation 3.5, and it is not presented in this section as it has not been used throughout
the work. However, the presence of texture can be inferred from multi-looked data by evaluating the so-
called Equivalent Number of Looks (ENL). The ENL is constructed using the first 2 moments of the average
intensity described by 3.4: the mean value σ and the variance σ2

15 is usually larger than the pixel size, meaning that pixels are correlated to a certain degree. If texture
is present, inter-pixel correlation also increases, and hence texture further lowers the ENL with respect to
the theoretical maximum L. Furthermore, several SAR image processing steps and techniques also introduce
smoothing effects, which reduce the ENL even more. If the impact and extent of all these factors is known to
a certain degree, at least except the texture presence, the calculation of the ENL from the mean and variance
of a multi-looked scene is a useful operation to infer the presence of texture.
Finally, it is important to add that simple averaging is one of the basic techniques encompassed in the broad
field of SAR reconstruction techniques, the main goal of which is to retrieve the underlying intensity values
of a scene’s targets from the speckled observation. Several other filtering and advanced techniques fall within
this field, and they are addressed in section 3.2.2, but nevertheless they all introduce a level of averaging and
with it, a level of speckle reduction, of pixel correlation, and of loss of resolution.
3.2.1.4
SAR backscatter coefficient: influences
As explained in the previous point, the generation of backscatter coefficient images is proportional to the
radar’s received power, but the images do not depend on the radar design and observation particularities.
Nevertheless, there are several properties, which depend both on the physical nature of the targets and the
radar parameters, which do affect the value of σ0. This implies that after transforming the received power
to the calibrated geophysical quantity, several radar qualities do still produce differences in the measured
15The SAR effective resolution in range and azimuth can be measured from the -3dB width of the SAR impulse-response
given by a point target such as a corner reflector.
32
==PAGE:34==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
σ0: these are the wavelength of the observation, λ, the radar look angle, and the polarization of the incident
wave, if polarized.
To clarify and quantify the geometry of SAR observation, it is important to properly introduce the notion
of look angle, incidence angle, and local incidence angle. The look angle is the angle between the observa-
tion direction of a side-looking radar and the nadir direction, comprised in the plane perpendicular to the
platform’s flight direction. The angle complementary to the look angle, i.e. the angle between the surface
normal and the look direction, is often referred to as the incidence angle, which throughout the work is
referred to as θ0. This angle is not exactly complementary for spaceborne radars, as the curved surface of
the planet usually increases the angle between the look direction and the normal to the spherical/ellipsoidal
model used to characterize it. Furthermore, the incidence angle takes into account a basic curvature model
for the observation geometry, but it does not take into account local topography. If knowledge of topography
is provided, then the incidence angle associated to each pixel can be corrected by the local slope, in which
case the local incidence angle is obtained. This angle is written throughout the work as θ. The relationships
between these angles can be visualized in the following figure:
Figure 3.8: Layout of the observational geometry of a side-looking radar. The platform can either be an EO
satellite or an orbiter in another planet. The curvature of the planetary body and the look direction define
an incidence angle for every point on the ground. At the same time, local topography might displace the
local normal (purple line) to the surface from the initial spherical/ellipsoidal model, generating the so-called
local incidence angle.
The local incidence angle is an important parameter that, together with the wavelength and the physical
33
==PAGE:35==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
properties of the surface, defines the surface roughness from a radar perspective. That is why, the extraction
of a meaningful local incidence angle for each pixel of an image is an important step in the methodology of
this work (see 5.3). The next 3 points deepen into the characteristics and effects of the different variables
that are related with the observed σ0 values.
3.2.1.4.1
Roughness
Surface roughness is the main parameter that drives the radar brightness of a target, particularly at short
wavelengths, when the dominant scattering mechanism is surface scattering. For a given surface physical
roughness, the backscattered power is a function of wavelength and local incidence angle θ, as can be visualized
in the following layout of figure 3.9. Roughness dominates the nature of the returned power, and because it
depends on the mentioned parameters, roughness it also a relative concept; a surface is radar ”rough” if it
possesses a strong backscattered component, which is often associated to large local incidence angles for a
given wavelength. If the incidence angle is very small, the power is reflected back by the surface mainly due
to geometry, by means of reflection. In this scenario, the roughness of the surface relative to the incident
wavelength does not affect much the received power. Nevertheless, as the local incidence angle increases, if
the surface is very smooth (contrary to rough, also in radar terms), most of the power is reflected away by
the surface and never reaches the radar, meaning that the received power is very low. It is in this scenario,
when incidence angles are large, that surface roughness acts to increase the received power by increasing the
backscattered power in all directions. On another hand, for a given local incidence angle, the roughness of a
surface usually increases if the wavelength decreases, because the roughness is always relative to the size of
the incident wave; smooth (and hence usually radar dark) surfaces seen by a relatively large wavelength are
usually brighter if seen by a shorter one, as their roughness relative to the wavelength increases and hence,
the power scattered back to the sensor is greater.
Figure 3.9: Layout of how the roughness of a surface, relative to the wavelength size, affects the measured
backscattered intensity (proportional to the received power) for different local incidence angles. For smooth
surfaces, specular returns produce large backscattered intensities for small incidence angles, but low returns
as the angle increases. This changes for rougher surfaces. In most cases, radar intensity returns can actually
increase with large incidence angles. Source: NASA [153].
Roughness is mathematically described by the Root Mean Square (RMS) variance and the surface correlation
length [172]. The RMS provides a measure of the statistical deviation of the surface height with respect to
34
==PAGE:36==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
the mean level of the surface: high RMS indicate more deviation and hence, more roughness. On another
hand, the correlation length is the displacement distance between surface height points from which their
values can be considered statistically independent: If heights are significantly stable, attributed to relatively
smooth surfaces, then the correlation length is large because significant height variations are spatially apart.
Therefore, rough surfaces are linked with high RMS and short correlation lengths [172].
In the context of this work, the physical roughness of the different terrains relative to the wavelength and
observation geometry is the main difference that affects radar intensity returns. This is assessed in detail in
section 5.1.1.
3.2.1.4.2
Dielectric constant
The dielectric constant is a complex-valued measure of a material’s permittivity and conductivity [172]. In the
context of SAR imaging, the dielectric constant, when assessed together with the characteristics of the SAR
observation, can well be a major factor affecting the brightness of the backscatter returns. Physically, when an
incident electromagnetic energy (such as a SAR pulse) interacts with a material, loose dipolar molecules can
align themselves with the generated electric field, storing energy. This energy is then released and scattered
back, which usually produces stronger radar returns (higher σ0). Such molecules are usually identified with
water on Earth, and hence soil moisture is a driving factor that can lead to an increase in radar brightness.
An incident electromagnetic wave can penetrate the ground to a certain level, called the penetration depth,
which depends mainly on the wavelength, the incidence angle, and the dielectric constant. The larger the
wavelength and the dielectric constant are, the deeper the waves penetrate, and consequently the extent and
location of the different energy scattered back to the radar change. This can be defined from the scattering
mechanism point of view: significant penetration is linked with volume scattering mechanisms, in which
the dielectric constant can play an important role, while low penetration usually implies a predominance of
surface scattering. The dielectric constant is mathematically defined as:
ϵ = ϵ′ −jϵ′′
(3.7)
where ϵ′ is the conductivity (real part) and ϵ′′ the permittivity (imaginary). In general, conductivity values
on Earth can range from a value of 3 for very dry materials to a value of 80 for water. Rocky terrains on
Earth usually exhibit a range between 4 and 9 [8], while in certain locations, particularly at high latitudes,
high dielectric constants of around ≈27 have been inferred in Venus. This has been associated with metallic
inclusions, particularly iron sulphides such as pyrites, since the water content in the soil is thought to be
negligible [31].
The implications (or not) of the dielectric constant of the Venusian surface on the SAR σ0 of Magellan and
VISAR/VENSAR is explored in section 5.1.3.
3.2.1.4.3
Polarization
The electromagnetic waves sent in each SAR pulse can be polarized. Polarization applies to transverse waves
and, given that the direction of the wave’s oscillation is perpendicular to the direction of motion, it indicates
its geometrical orientation in the perpendicular plane. In the radar context, the polarizations that are usu-
ally considered for spaceborne imaging systems are the linear polarizations HH, VV and HV. The effects of
polarization in the radar return are important to be briefly highlighted in this section in order to understand
the interaction of the radar observations of the future missions to Venus and the planet’s terrain.
Polarizations interact differently depending on the orientation of the physical structure of the terrain. Signifi-
cant work has been performed in the field of SAR crop monitoring [172] [61], where different surface structures
are available due to vegetation. In general, VV polarized waves are more attenuated by vertically-aligned
structures, while HH signatures interact directly with the underlying soil (in case of vegetation) [172]. In the
35
==PAGE:37==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
case of Venusian terrain, polarization-induced backscatter changes are more linked with surface roughness,
surface texture and local slopes, as vegetation is non-existent. HH polarized waves are more affected by
surface roughness and local slopes, while cross polarization signals are less affected by them. Comparison of
HH and HV polarized backscatter signatures, which can be performed by VenSAR [121], can be used to infer
properties of the texture of the Venusian terrain [133]. In general, polarization signatures of Venusian lava
flows appear to behave similar to their terrestrial analogs in terms of small-scale roughness information [21].
3.2.1.5
Image co-registration
Image co-registration is a necessary step in SAR image intercomparison, as it assures that the 2 images are
effectively showing the same scene. Good co-registration is a critical step when comparing a series of images
with the goal of change detection, and it consists in ensuring that the images are spatially aligned, meaning
that the features in the 2 footprints, at least the unchanged ones, overlap.
Co-registration is a step that is performed at the beginning of any change detection algorithm chain, once the
different images have been obtained and are ready to be processed. Usually, the initial image is considered
the ”master” image and the subsequent set of images obtained at other times are named ”slaves”. Typi-
cally, co-registration algorithms use first a coarse co-registration method for pixel accuracy, which consists
mainly in finding offsets between images and shifting the slaves in order to fit them with the master. If
further precision is required (usually in case phase information is required like in SAR interferometry), a
finer co-registration step searches for so-called tie points at sublevel accuracy (resampling the slave images)
and applies polynomial fits that match the points between images [78]. Methodologies need to take into
account the different geometries and samplings of the images, but this has been performed, even with images
originating from different sensors (Optical and SAR) [138].
Image co-registration between products imaging the same locations has been assumed in this work. This is
mainly because the time series involves the use of images that have not yet been obtained (VISAR/VENSAR
images), and the images to be compared have been generated from the original Magellan images, ensuring
co-registration. Nevertheless, understanding the concept as a necessary step for accurate change detection is
important, as co-registration is present in the proposed change detection strategy in 5.2.
3.2.1.6
Radiometric calibration
Radiometric calibration is a very fundamental operation in SAR processing that allows to retrieve mean-
ingful, comparable information from the acquired images. Formally, it refers to the ability to convert the
digital numbers recorded by the SAR sensor into geophysical properties; convert the measured voltages to
backscatter coefficient values, for the cases considered in this work. The values of σ0, for example, can only be
obtained after a proper calibration process. Such process mainly follows the radar equation in 3.1 to convert
the detected, digital numbers (DN) provided in the raw data of most SAR sensors to the specific type of
backscatter coefficient desired. DN values are usually larger than the scaled backscatter values in order to
optimize the use of the data dynamic range (usually 16 bits) [76]. On top of that, different sources of error
and noise need to be estimated and incorporated to the process. The outcome of the calibration process is
usually simplified in data calibration manuals by means of a calibration constant Ks and an estimation of
the total noise, to be subtracted to the image, pixel by pixel.
In general, SAR radiometric calibration needs to compensate the following factors [76], when information is
available:
• The elevation antenna pattern on the local Digital Elevation Model (DEM) and range spread loss.
• The azimuth antenna pattern.
• The effects of different azimuth and range bandwidths.
36
==PAGE:38==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
• The sensor settings (receiver gain, transmitted power, duty cycle, etc.).
• Instrument temperature-dependent gain variations, if any.
• Thermal noise sources, mainly receiver and antenna fluctuations.
The pixel-by-pixel thermal noise estimate reflects the antenna gain pattern compensation during the process-
ing. Thermal noise is usually computed by the sensor at several azimuthal positions during the acquisition
time, and different methods are used to fit the estimates to each of the pixels of the image. Much in line with
the projection of the backscattered power that is desired (σ0, β0 or γ0), the noise estimate needs to have the
same projection, which explains the distinction between the Noise Equivalent Beta Naught (NEBN) term
and the Noise Equivalent Sigma Naught (NESZ) term that are usually employed in calibration processes.
Even though calibration processes target the same corrections in most SAR observations, each SAR product
has its own characterization of the calibration process. In this project, which involves data processing of
4 different SAR missions, the specific calibration processes are explained in the respective data processing
sections of chapter 4.
Large calibration errors pose a significant problem for SAR change detection routines, as radiometric distor-
tion between acquisitions might mask or obscure real changes in backscatter. Further complications appear
particularly when the images to be compared are not obtained with the same sensors, as the calibration pro-
cess, but particularly the calibration accuracy, differ. Differences in radiometric accuracy directly translate
into radiometric distortion, and this cannot be avoided if the sensors capacities are different in this sense.
Such distortion needs to be quantified and subtracted from the change detection process in order to minimize
detection errors.
3.2.2
Reconstruction of SAR images
As explained in 3.2.1.1, the most employed SAR image model postulates that the power images are charac-
terized by an underlying true intensity (RCS or most commonly σ0) multiplied by a noise-like component
named Speckle, which is the main responsible behind their probabilistic nature [64]. The term reconstruction
is concerned with the inverse problem of retrieving the true σ0 of the scene from the noisy observed intensity.
In practice, this consists in reducing or eliminating the variability of the image (speckle reduction), without
distorting the retrieved underlying intensity values (to a certain extent), and without amplification of any
other source of noise.
Reconstruction should achieve good speckle reduction while conserving the image
structure and radiometric qualities and without introducing artifacts.
Estimation of the underlying RCS can be done by means of reconstruction filters and techniques, often referred
to as despeckling techniques. On another hand, instead of just estimating the RCS values, reconstruction
can go one step further and attempt to classify the image in areas/targets of constant RCS. This is usually
referred to as segmentation. Despeckling and segmentation can be employed for image reconstruction, but to
choose one or another depends on factors that apply to the specific study case on one hand, and to the nature
of the goals pursued on the other. Despeckling removes speckle, albeit sacrificing spatial resolution, but the
variability of the several targets of the scene does not disappear completely, usually because the outcome of
the process requires for certain spatial resolution to be preserved. On the other hand, segmentation effec-
tively suppresses the spatial resolution, as the outcome of the process is the classification of pixels into their
most likely target-associated RCS value, which transforms the image into a mosaic of intensity-homogeneous
patches, also called classes. Because of this, despeckling is usually performed as a middle step leading to a
pixel level analysis, such as pixel-based change detection. On the other hand, segmentation might be used
for change detection, but in general it requires of the definition of area-based metrics to perform.
There is a wide array of studied techniques for despeckling and segmentation purposes [64] [33] [146]. In the
following points, the theoretical framework is briefly described, and the techniques used in the project are
addressed.
37
==PAGE:39==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
3.2.2.1
Despeckling techniques
A total of 3 different filters, with certain sub-variants, have been used in this project to test despeckling
performance. Precisely, the analysis of the outcome of the reconstruction is the only way to assess whether the
despeckling operation matches the reconstruction criteria exposed in the previous point. Feature preservation
and absence of artifacts are usually assessed visually, by directly comparing the images. Nevertheless, 2 simple
metrics can be used to assess radiometric preservation on one hand and speckle reduction on the other. A
good trade-off between significant speckle reduction and a small radiometric distortion is the objective of
any despeckling technique. Radiometric preservation can be visualized by computing the mean of the ratio
r between the original image and the despeckled/filtered one [64]:
¯r = 1
N
N

(3.8)
If this value significantly deviates from one (either gets larger or smaller), the radiometric accuracy is not
fully preserved. It is important to be noted that this value just provides a measure of the mean radiometric
distortion; it is possible that this distortion is not homogeneous throughout the image, depending on the
despeckling filter used. This can be analyzed by computing consecutive smaller patches of the scene and
evaluating the same metric on them; if radiometric preservation is significantly uneven throughout the im-
age, the compensation of the distortion is less straightforward (cannot be compensated by a simple offset).
On another hand, speckle reduction can be visualized by computing the standard deviation of the ratio r:
¯

(3.9)
The larger the SD value is, independently of which image goes to the numerator or the denominator, the more
successful the filter is reducing speckle. This happens because the SD is a measure of the mean deviation of
one image with respect to the other: if variability (speckle) has been successfully reduced, the mean difference
between the 2 images will be large, and hence so will the SD.
These 2 metrics have been used in section 5.4 to evaluate the filters’ performance. In the following lines, the
several despeckling filters are described, particularly focusing on their pros and cons.
3.2.2.1.1
Multi-looking
Multi-looking is the most straightforward method for speckle reduction. As defined in section 3.2.1.3, spatial
multi-looking is an image post-processing technique that is used to reduce variability under the assumption
of each contiguous pixel being an independent realization of the same underlying radar return. This is an
assumable condition in most parts of a given SAR image, although it can clearly lead to errors in the borders
between targets, as the contiguous pixels would then not possess the same underlying RCS. Spatial multi-
looking can be seen as the result of the low-pass filtering of the image with a simple 2-D averaging filter,
typically referred to as a boxcar filter. The intensity output of each pixel after filtering is the equal-weighted
average of the values of all pixels within the filter dimensions; mathematically, this is the same as the equation
of incoherent averaging of intensity values (equation 3.4).
Nevertheless, the question of edge preservation arises when the extent of the averaging operation starts to
be problematic. If speckle reduction is performed by a large-enough averaging, because the loss of spatial
resolution is within the desired limits, the location of the edges between the observed underlying targets of
the image can be smeared-out to the point where target extent and location becomes difficult to discern.
This also applies to the possible texture information of the scene. Because of this, additional filters that can
incorporate information of the local statistics of the pixel they are centered around have been designed [117]:
38
==PAGE:40==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
these are the so-called adaptive filters, which might adapt their filtering weight and extent to the statistical
particularities of the scene.
3.2.2.1.2
Adaptive filters
2 different adaptive filters have been considered in this work for despeckling purposes: the Lee filter and the
Frost filter. The design of the filters and their mathematical theory, described here, mainly follows the work
performed at DTU by Sorensen, Lupemba and Lippert [117]. Modified and enhanced version of these filters
are also included.
Several adaptive features are included in these filters. One of the features is to modify the weighting of the
filter, i.e. not all the pixels within the filter’s box are equally important (such as in the boxcar filter). This
can depend on the homogeneity of the area of the scene, for example, which in turn depends on its statistical
properties. Geometrical information regarding the edges of targets in the scene is also important to construct
adaptive filters. Edges can be step discontinuities, in which the mean intensity significantly changes from one
area to another area, and also line discontinuities, where there is a line of high intensity but relatively low
intensities in both sides [117]. For natural distributed targets such as the expected landscapes on Venus, step
discontinuities dominate and are an important feature that distinguishes terrains and different lava flows.
Therefore, speckle reduction with edge preservation is definitely an important feature to take into account if
selecting a despeckling routine. The following points theoretically describe the adaptive filters considered.
Frost filter
The Frost filter is an adaptive filter that works similarly as the boxcar filter, but presents a non-homogeneous
and variable weighting of the different pixels values within its range. The weight of each pixel in the filter
box is defined as:
wi = e−αCcri,c
(3.10)
where α is a sensitivity constant, ri,c is the distance between a pixel of the box and the center pixel for a
given position of the filter, and Cc is the speckle index of the center pixel, which is usually defined as:
Cc =
1
ENL
(3.11)
where ENL is the equivalent number of looks of the current area within the filter’s box extent. From this
definition, the adaptive nature of the Frost filter can be inferred. The larger the Variance to Mean Ratio of an
area (the inverse of the ENL), the more heterogeneous an area is and the larger Cc becomes, which translates
into a sharp difference in pixel weights. Comparatively, high ENLs will yield low Cc values, which are found
in homogeneous areas, and in these situations the weighting of the pixels will be similar, resembling a boxcar
[117].The choice of sensitivity constant decides the level of filtering, which balances out the brightness of the
edges and the noisiness of their detection in one hand with the level of noise in homogeneous areas on the
other. Experimentally, a choice of α = 1 is seen as a good compromise [117].
Modifications can be introduced to the Frost filter in order to refine its performance.
The modification
considered in this work is an adaptation of equation 3.11; the Cc value in this case will only be the inverse
of the ENL when the ENL is below a defined threshold T0. For the cases where the ENL is larger, meaning
that the area to be filtered is very homogeneous according to the criteria, the filter will behave as a simple
boxcar:
Cc =

1
ENL −
1
T0 ,

0,
ENL > T0
(3.12)

==PAGE:41==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
The method to select this threshold is based on a Constant False Alarm Rate (CFAR) for the homogeneous
areas, usually set at 1 % (for more information on CFAR see 3.2.3). Taking the upper 1% of the pixels’
histogram of a homogeneous area and calculating the ENL will yield an ENL value, lower than the real ENL
for the whole homogeneous region, which can be used as a threshold.
The 2 versions of the Frost filter (with and without thresholding the definition of Cc) are used in chapter 5.
The optimal choices of Threshold T0 and sensitivity α are chosen by experimentation with the images.
Lee filter
The Lee filter is the other adaptive filter considered in this work. It is slightly more advanced than the Frost
filter because, on top of weighing the pixels with local statistics, the statistics are restricted according to the
edge geometry of the area within the filter box, at least in the modified version considered in this work. The
simplest Lee filter calculates the mean µ, the variance V and the ENL of the local box position and assigns
a weighting k to the center pixel as follows:
k = V · ENL −µ2
V (ENL + 1)
(3.13)
The consequent value of the pixel after filtering is:
PLee = µ + k(Poriginal −µ)
(3.14)
These expressions are modified if adding the geometrical observation of how the center-pixel of the box is
located with respect to the edges of the scene. Equation 3.13 is used in the first place to assess whether a
pixel belongs to a homogeneous region or to a textured/edgy position. If k is under a threshold value set the
same way as with the Frost filter, then the pixel is considered to belong to a homogeneous area and no mask
direction needs to be calculated (mask value is assigned to -1). For the pixels that have not been associated
to a no-mask value, a mask orientation operation is performed in order to assign the pixel to a mask number
that ranges from 0 to 7. There are 8 possible orientations of the mask then, for the Lee filter considered in
this work, which are the ones shown in the next figure:
Figure 3.10: The possible orientations of the Modified Lee filter masks, for a 7 x 7 window example. Values
that are associated with each of the dispositions are shown below each mask. Source: DTU [117].
The mask assignation operation effectively selects which part of the box/window will be used to compute
local statistics (mean, variance, ENL) for the calculation of the Lee filter weighting and which will be dis-
carded, considering it belongs to a different target than the central pixel. Mask assignation works by dividing
40
==PAGE:42==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
the box into a subset of areas and by calculating their mean values; the disposition of these mean vales ev-
idences the presence (or not) of 2 different areas separated by an edge, and what direction does this edge take.
After this operation, each pixel is associated with a value between -1 and 7. For the pixels that this time
have mask values between 0 and 7, the weighting is calculated following equation 3.13, but in this case only
the pixels within the positive (dark) part of the mask are taken into account to compute the mean value
µmask and the variance Vmask;
kmask = VmaskENL −µ2
mask
Vmask(ENL + 1)
(3.15)
Consequently, the pixels values are updated with these masked weightings:
PLee = µmask + kmask(Poriginal −µmask)
(3.16)
3.2.2.1.3
Segmentation techniques
Another form of intensity image reconstruction is segmentation, which classifies pixels into classes/regions
with single intensity values [64]. Segmentation techniques are based in the assumption that images are built
up of regions, separated by edges, and that regions are identifiable and discernible by a certain radar ob-
servable. The radar observable is usually radar intensity or, in some cases, radar texture. The number and
position of these segments and their mean values are not known a priori, and this information is obtained
solely from the data and, in some cases, prior knowledge. Segmentation does not despeckle in order to locate
the true RCS value in a pixel level; it builds up an algorithm to locate constant RCS/texture patches. If
the objective is image interpretation, for instance to discern the structural properties of the different targets,
segmentation is an appropriate technique because it introduces no radiometric distortion, as opposed to the
despeckling filters described in the previous sections, which always bring a certain degree of distortion. Seg-
mentation techniques are furthermore, and ideally, automatic and not tuned to specific SAR images, aside
of certain general parameter modification. When it comes to change detection, segmentation might be the
correct tool in most scenarios, but significant errors might occur in image comparison with different sensors
involved. The choice of despeckling or segmentation techniques for the change detection algorithm work is
argued in 5.2.1.
Segmentation is a reconstruction (and classification) technique in itself, and should not be preceded by
despeckling, as speckle reduction and degraded resolution can introduce classification errors between regions.
In simple terms, segmentation techniques can be classified as Edge Detection, Region Merging and Region
Fitting. The following list offers insight in their qualities and differences:
• Edge Detection: Initially, the SAR intensity image is regarded as a single class. Based on speckle
statistics alone, a mask process in line with the Lee filter previously described detects the location of
the edges. The correct method for edge detection follows the ratio edge detection principle [15], which
considers different edge orientations and different window sizes and performs a test statistic on whether
the mean value of one side of the edge is equal enough to the one on the other as to regard the pixel as
belonging to a homogeneous region. Bright edges are detected with the smallest window sizes, as their
discontinuities are sharper. These detected edges are retained, and the pixels excluded from successive
calculations with larger windows, where weaker edges are subsequently detected. The regions enclosed
by edges eventually become discernible, and are associated with their calculated mean RCS value.
Multiple edge configuration exists for performing the edge detection test statistic, for example fixed
window, scanned edge (FWSE) configuration or scanned window, center edge (SWCE) configuration
[64]. Moreover, several edge detection techniques exist [33] [44], including where the information-bearing
parameter is texture [42] and where the data possess multiple polarimetric channels [62]. Overall, edge-
detection segmentation is a powerful framework for reconstruction and classification of SAR images.
41
==PAGE:43==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
• Region Merging: This family of techniques assumes the same speckle model and statistic framework
of edge detection, but instead of locating edges, it attempts to create large uniform regions by merging
small neighboring units, starting from the pixel level. The most employed region merging technique
is known as Merge Using Moments (MUM), and it works by performing a Student t-test that decides
whether to perform a merging. The test is a test statistic of two-sample locations which asks whether
the means of two populations are equal under the assumption of comparable variances, in this case the
means of 2 regions to be merged. The MUM merging process is iterated until the mean differences
between regions is smaller than a preset threshold value that provides a CFAR in the image (see 3.2.3
for theory on CFAR). Its performance is similar to that of Edge detection.
• Region fitting: This method is an alternative approach to Edge Detection and Region Merging,
which are methods that iterate from small to large features in order to identify segments.
Region
fitting is concerned with the global optimization problem of the scene as a whole; identifying the
Maximum Likelihood Estimate (MLE) of each region as a function of their position, extent and RCS
value, where the RCS is either gamma-distributed (equation 3.5), in the fairly homogeneous case, or
distributed with a more complex distribution in the presence of texture [64]. Region fitting is often
one of the best-performing family of techniques, particularly when employing a specific method called
simulated annealing [41] [42], which can be exploited to locate segments in cases where texture is present
and where it is not. Nevertheless, even though simulated annealing is the best-performing technique
studied in this work (according to the reconstruction criteria exposed in 3.2.2), it is often also the most
computationally costly.
Figure 3.11: Results of a simulated annealing segmentation process using a MLE from a textured-data dis-
tribution, which allows to segment this forested area in the Amazon in forest segments and cleared segments.
Notice that both types of segments exhibit actually similar RCS, meaning that segmentation according to
RCS does not perform great in this case. Instead, the texture of the different regions is the information-
bearing parameter that allows segmentation. The MLE of textured intensity SAR images is a log likelihood
based on an analytic approximation of a K-distribution 16when the number of looks is large enough [64].
Source: Oliver et al., 1994 [34].

==PAGE:44==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
3.2.3
SAR change detection
After characterizing the nature of SAR observations from several points of view, and addressing the problem
of reconstruction and classification of the images, this section focuses on describing the process of SAR change
detection, i.e. the technique that detects and spatially locates changes between 2 acquisitions.
3.2.3.1
Initial considerations
SAR CD techniques are performed after achieving a suitable scenario for comparison, which includes proper
image co-registration, comparable radiometric calibrations between the images, and the identification of the
information-bearer parameter/radar observable that effectively contains the change information. To begin
with, and as stated in 2, there are several methodologies for change detection. Certain techniques allow
detecting the location of the changes, but also the magnitude and the direction. When selecting a CD algo-
rithm, it is therefore critical to have in mind the requirements from the user perspective. In the case of lava
flows, the location of the change is important, and so can be the magnitude, if the objective is to quantify
how physical changes of terrain relate to the changes in radar observables, but the direction can actually be
inferred from the scene disposition and from other sources of data such as topography. In this study, for
a majority of applications, including the performance assessment of change detection itself, it is enough for
the CD goal to be the generation of a binary map of pixels or areas that classifies the scene as change (1) or
no-change (0), at least when no phase information is exploited.
From this point of view, 2 different classes of change detection can be described: coherent and incoherent.
Coherent change detection requires a certain degree of coherence between observations, and the information-
bearer parameter can either be the coherence estimate itself [43] [67] or the phase difference between images
[74]. Coherence is a measure of similarity; if the different sources that can lead to decorrelation (geometric,
volumetric, thermal and other observational-induced changes between the 2 images to be compared) are
low, then information from the phase difference can be associated to changes [145], particularly if the phase
difference associated with topography and atmospheric variations is known and can be removed. This tech-
nique is termed D-InSAR, which has been named in the introduction, and allows the full characterization
of the changes as displacements, i.e. mass and feature movements from one area to another. Rather than
allowing the classification of a pixel as change or no-change, phase information provides a vector field, giving
the magnitude and direction (at least in the radar line of sight) of the changes and allowing to relate them
with physical displacements of the order of millimeters. On another hand, if a scene preserves its coherence
overall, specific areas of low-coherence that might be located in the image can also be associated directly with
change scenarios. In this case, the loss of coherence beyond a certain value is the discriminator that allows
to classify the pixels of an image as change or no-change, but even if talking about a coherent CD technique,
the result is not a vector field of change but rather a change/no-change classification.
The same applies to fully incoherent CD techniques. Coherence is sometimes not preserved, even if the images
are co-registered and well calibrated, because the rates of change are too large between the 2 acquisitions or
because the different factors that incise in coherence preservation are too different between the acquisitions.
In the case of radar images that are obtained by different sensors, wavelength changes immediately lead to
full decorrelation, and hence incoherent techniques become the only ones exploitable.
Incoherent CD techniques in most cases exploit the difference in the statistical properties of the images in
order to locate changes (an exception is offset-tracking [60], which uses the correlation between displaced
features in the images to quantify displacement). If the same group of pixels follows a different statistical
distribution in one image than in another, at least different enough from the point of view of a designed change
criteria, changes can be located at pixel level. As introduced in 5.1.1, there are several radar observables,
and each of them can be modelled according to different probability distributions under certain assumptions.
16A K distribution is a three-parameter continuous probability density function that arises when compounding two gamma
distributions, and it has been observed to be the closest fit to SAR intensity data that is significantly textured.
43
==PAGE:45==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
In most cases, the main radar observable that is used for incoherent change detection is the radar intensity
(usually in the σ0 form); because it contains discriminating information in most cases, because it is well
characterized mathematically (even in the case of texture), and because it is also the parameter that is
despeckled/segmented before applying change detection (as it is the physically correct radar observable to
be reconstructed [154]). The change in statistics of σ0 between images can be evaluated by a so-called test
statistic, which evaluates the change and decides whether it is large enough to assign a pixel into the change
category.
Figure 3.12: Examples of the different CD techniques. Starting from the left: D-InSAR interferogram that
shows phase changes associated to the ground deformation produced by a volcanic eruption in the Gal´apagos
Islands [142]; new lava flow emplacement in the Kilauea area (Hawaii) associated with loss of radar coherence
with respect to the rest of the scene [89]; and change detection binary map following a test statistic on radar
intensity changes in a crop area [64].
3.2.3.2
Incoherent change detection: the ratio image
Incoherent change detection techniques based on test statistics need to evaluate a specific relationship be-
tween the 2 images. This specific relationship needs to be statistically definable by a probability density
(PDF) function of some sort, if the changes are to be mathematically discernible and hence, not dependable
on human observation. In the case this is achievable, the distribution of the pixels in the scene (histogram)
will fit this PDF (or PDFs), and a threshold value of this particular metric can therefore be selected in
order to decide which pixels are considered to belong to a class associated with change and which ones to a
no-change one.
There are several relationships between images that can be statistically modelled. Initially, the most obvi-
ous operation to detect changes in intensity is to subtract one image to the other, evaluating the difference
image, also called the gradient image. Nevertheless, one of the most important and necessary conditions
for change detection is that the ability to detect changes does not depend on the value of the metric being
modelled. This is equivalent as to state that one of the main conditions of change detection in a statistical
context is that the rate of false alarms (miss-classifications) is the same independently of the images’ values.
A false alarm corresponds to a situation when a pixel is assigned to a change class when it actually does
not experience any change, and vice versa. Therefore, test statistics in SAR change detection should always
have a Constant False Alarm Rate (CFAR) for a given image because, this way, the ability to detect changes
remains unchanged throughout the scene.
If the scene can be considered homogeneous as a whole, and texture is not significant, radar intensity after a
certain degree of multi-looking (can be any despeckling technique) can be described by a gamma distribution.
Therefore, the gradient image follows the statistics resulting from the mathematical subtraction of 2 gamma
distributions. It has been shown [15] [64] that in this case, the gradient value depends on the intensity of the
images, meaning that the probability of false alarm cannot be homogeneous. This is in general not desirable,
44
==PAGE:46==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
and therefore most incoherent change detection is usually based in the ratio of the 2 images, the ratio image,
which exhibits a probability of false alarm that is constant. Note that evaluating the difference of the log
intensity in decibels is actually equivalent to evaluating the ratio.
In order to visualize the rationale behind the change detection statistics of the ratio image, the following figure
illustrates the different mathematical distributions that characterize the histograms formed when dividing
2 SAR intensity images that have been averaged to a certain level (and hence can be assumed gamma-
distributed). It is important to state here that in the classical theory of test statistic change detection, the
despeckling techniques used in both images are equivalent, meaning that their expected ENL is equal. This
might be not the case of when there is an intercomparison of different SAR sensors, such as there is in this
work, and an adaptation of this mathematical framework might be needed (see 5.7).
Figure 3.13: Examples of the PDFs of different SAR intensity ratio images, with both images presenting
different number of looks/averages. Source: Touzi et al., 1988 [15].
The more number of looks the images possess, the more narrow the ratio distribution becomes. On another
hand, the horizontal axis determines the ratio value. If the images do not experience any change, then the
ratio distribution will be centered around 1. If there is a significant mean change of the image as a whole,
for instance due to a change in a system parameter that might affect all intensity values equally, then the
distribution is displaced from 1. In this case, if the numerator image has a larger mean value than the
denominator, then the mean ratio value is displaced to the right, while if it is smaller the mean ratio moves
to the left. Also, if a part of the image experiences a significant change (in radiometric and spatial terms), a
second distribution associated with this area arises, also to the left or to the right of the unity value depending
on the numerator/denominator disposition. The capacity to discern between changed and unchanged pixels
according to the different distributions visualized when fitting the histograms of the ratio of intensity images
is the core of a test statistic change detection. The strategies on how to locate the threshold values that
select the pixels’ classes are given in the point 3.2.3.4, while the mathematical characterization of the ratio
PDF, the probability of detecting change, and the probability of error/false alarm is given in the next point
3.2.3.3.
45
==PAGE:47==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
3.2.3.3
Ratio test statistic theory
In the following section, the framework for a change detection strategy based on a test statistic of the ratio
SAR intensity image has been defined.
Considering that the 2 SAR intensity images to be divided are
gamma distributed (see equation 3.5), the probability density function that describes the histogram obtained
by performing the ratio is [64]:
p(R) = Γ(2L)
Γ2(L)
γLRL−1
(γ + R)2L
(3.17)
where R is the ratio value, L is the number of looks applied in the multi-looking/despeckling of the images
and γ = σ2
σ1 is the ratio of the mean intensities of the image. Intensity in this case can be any of the calibrated
values introduced in 3.2.1.3; in this work, test statistics are performed with σ0 images. Equation 3.17 is the
one that defines the ratio distributions seen in figure 3.13.
Once the PDF is defined, setting a ratio value threshold for a specific probability can separate the pixels
that are considered changed from the ones that remain the same (no-change). The probability of detection
is, consequently, defined as the value of the cumulative distribution function (CDF) associated to the PDF
at infinity minus the CDF value at the selected threshold level. This can be mathematically defined as [64]:
Pd(T, L) = P{R > T} =
Z ∞
T
p(R)dR = Γ(2L)
Γ2(L)
