k−2L+1
(3.18)
It is important to make the point here that, according to the above definition of detection probability, the
threshold value T always needs to be less than the mean ratio to achieve meaningful detection probabili-
ties. In CD theory, the desired detection probability is always of 0.9 or above. The detection probability
is associated with the CDF, which represents the area under the PDF. Therefore, the area to the right of
the threshold ratio value (because Pd is defined from the threshold to infinity) should be 90% of the total area.
This definition is motivated because the theory considers that the mean ratio value γ carries the bulk of
the pixels that have changed; in other words, it considers that the whole ratio distribution is in fact com-
posed by a majority of pixels that change. Selecting as many pixels of the PDF as possible (low threshold)
implies a high detection probability. However, there is a limit for lowering the threshold in this case, and
this limit is given by the probability of false alarm, Pfa. The probability of false alarm is the probabil-
ity of classifying a pixel as change (meaning that exceeds the threshold), when that pixel has not actually
changed. Pfa, which is only a function of the L used in the PDF definition and the selected T, can be
found by calculating the detection probability if the γ of the distribution is set to γ = 1 [64]. For a given
threshold, the detection probability might be very high, but the probability of false alarm can be too high
to obtain the desired results. In practice, the correct way to proceed is to define an assumable CFAR (an
assumable Pfa throughout the scene), usually between 0 and 5 %, which defines a threshold, and then the
threshold value is used with equation 3.18 (with the γ used in the PDF) to calculate the detection probability.
But what if the bulk of the distribution, the majority of the pixels, represent no-change? This can happen
when the change is only localized for a given scene dimensions, which is very often the case for lava flow
emplacement. Selecting a threshold ratio value that is lower than the mean ratio value will detect a majority
of no-change pixels, and hence, the threshold should be set to a ratio value much larger than the mean (if the
change is seen to have displaced the histogram mean to the right, meaning that the changed pixels have in
average higher radar brightness than they had before). In this case, nevertheless, the detection probability
as defined in 3.18 would be very low (the area under the curve would be very small). On another hand, pixel
changes could be overall towards a lower radar brightness, which would mean that the pixels changed are
found between a ratio value 0 until the threshold value T, which contradicts the Pd definition.
46
==PAGE:48==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
As it can be seen, there are different scenarios to be considered, which require for a broader approach and
a more plastic definition of the Pfa and the threshold selection method. In practice, what happens is that
localized changes create a second PDF that follows the definition in 3.17 and is displaced (to a brighter or
darker mean ratio value) from the main distribution, which represents the pixels that have not changed and
that is centered at a mean ratio value 1 (if there is no radiometric distortion between images). Therefore,
the histogram of a ratio of intensity images that has undergone change usually presents a bimodal behav-
ior. The Pd is defined then as either the probability to detect change pixels, or the probability to detect
no-change pixels, depending if it is computed on the distribution that represents the localized change or on
the distribution that represents unchanged pixels. Either way, the pixels that are not selected as change
correspond to no-change and vice versa, which implies that either way of calculating Pd globally defines the
behavior of all pixels in the histogram. If the distribution representing change has a mean value brighter than
the no-change one, the detection probability is used in the change distribution, and so it is the probability
of detecting changed pixels. On another hand, if the mean value for the change distribution is lower, the
detection probability is used in the no-change distribution, and hence it becomes the probability of detecting
no-change pixels. The motivation behind these criteria is not to change the formal definition of the detection
probability, which computes the area from a threshold to infinity, as shown in 3.18. Figure 3.14 is helpful to
visualize the different scenarios.
One consequence of these different scenarios of change detection is that the thresholding operation ceases to
be just a limit for false alarm detection of a single distribution, and becomes the ratio value that separates 2
classes (at least), change and no-change, each of them distributed as in 3.17 but with different mean values
and with different number of pixels [79]. If the distributions do not overlap to a certain degree, all change
pixels can be easily separated from the no-change ones. Nevertheless, this is seldom the case. If they do, the
probability of false alarm set by the threshold evolves to a more general definition, and is called the error
probability Pe (which in this work is equivalently named Pfa as well). The error probability is the sum of
the total in-between classes error after setting a threshold, which equals to the sum of the misclassification
of pixels as class change when they belong to class no-change and the misclassification of pixels as class
no-change when they have actually changed [30]. Mathematically, this is:
Pe = PchangeEno−change+Pno−changeEchange = Pchange

Figure 3.14: Summary of the 2 envisioned scenarios for change detection from the point of view of the
computation of Pd and Pe, when 2 classes are considered (only 1 meaningful change occurs in the considered
scene). The 2 factors of Pe in equation 3.20 correspond to the green and blue areas under the curve highlighted
in the plot on the left, while in the plot on the right they correspond to the 2 factors in equation 3.19. Notice
how in the first case (left), all pixels classified as no-change are the ones above the threshold, and hence the
detection probability computed is the probability of detecting no-change pixels. In the opposite case (to the
right), the detection probability is defined analogously (following equation 5.42), but in this case it is the
probability of detecting class change pixels. In both cases, error/false alarm probabilities arise due to the
overlapping of the 2 classes. Displayed with Geogebra [161].
The ratio PDF, the probability of detection, the probability of false alarm/error, and the ratio threshold value
have been defined, and how they are computed or behave according to the different CD scenarios has been
assessed. One conclusion is that, depending on how much probability of error can be assumed for a given
scenario, the threshold value T plays a central role in deciding which pixels are assumed to have changed and
which pixels not. Nevertheless, the methodology to choose this threshold value has not been introduced, and
several approaches exist. This is addressed in the following point.
3.2.3.4
Thresholding methodologies
Selecting a threshold for change detection, or thresholding, is the operation that ultimately decides the
detection and error probabilities in case of change given a test statistic framework. In this work, 2 different
thresholding methodologies have been used, although a large variety of them exist in the literature [64] [79].
The first one is the most common threshold selection method, known as the minimum error thresholding
method. Given the definition of the 2-class probability of error (equations 3.19 and 3.20), which depends on
the threshold selection, there exists a threshold that minimizes it:
P ′

3.3
Image processing techniques
This last section of theory encompasses all image processing theory that has been used throughout the re-
port, but it is independent of SAR image processing. All the theoretical topics here have been used in other
parts of the work; particularly, most of them have been employed in the processing of the altimetry-derived
topography product of Magellan (see the product in 4.1.5 and the post-processing chain in 5.3), but also in
several steps of the basic data processing of chapter 4.
3.3.1
2-D interpolation
2-D interpolation is a basic sampling operation that is often required when dealing with different datasets.
1-D interpolation have been also used in this work, but the 2-D version generalizes the problem to a higher
dimensional degree. Interpolation allows the resampling of a given image/scene into a finer or coarser pixel
size. It is important to mention that interpolation resizes the image in terms of number of pixels, but it does
not alter neither its physical extent nor its effective resolution; these both depend on the radar capabilities
and cannot be altered by means of resampling operations (resolution can be enhanced to a certain extent
with certain image reconstruction algorithms).
Interpolation is, therefore, the operation of finding middle-vales in between the already given image pixel
values, with the aim to increase the sampling. This is useful if 2 co-registered images have a different number
of pixels: 2-D interpolation allows pixel-to-pixel intercomparison by resampling at least one of the images. 2
interpolation methods have been used in this work: linear and spline.
Linear interpolation creates a straight line between any given adjacent points in a data set. If one point
is (x0, y0) and the other is (x1, y1), then the interpolated points (y, x) can be any values x ∈[x0, x1] and
y ∈[y0, y1] that are found in the line:
y = y0 + y1 −y0
x1 −x0
(x −x0)
(3.33)
The number of the desired equally-spaced points in the lines is to be decided when performing the inter-
polation, usually to match one dataset size with another. On another hand, spline interpolation constructs
polynomial interpolations between points (degree 3 or higher) that minimize the curve’s bending (curvature).
The basic algorithm to compute a curve qi(x) between the 2 points introduced before is:
q(x0) = y0
(3.34)

(3.38)

Solving this system of equations between 2 points (x0, y0) and (x1, y1) allows to construct the spline qi(x)
between them, where values can be interpolated upon analogously as with the linear interpolation. The
choice of linear and spline for each of the steps of the algorithm’s code where they are required depends on
the trade-off between the observed performance and the observed computation time.
3.3.2
Kriging
Another more advanced tool for interpolation purposes is the commonly called Kriging operation [3], for-
merly known as the Wiener–Kolmogorov prediction. Under suitable prior conditions, which make the problem
sufficiently well-conditioned, kriging provides the best linear unbiased prediction of unknown values at non-
sampled locations. Because of this, kriging has a different application than the 2-D interpolation methods
stated before, at least in the framework of this work. 2-D interpolation is used to increase the sampling of
fully-sampled images. Kriging, on another hand, is employed to predict values of image pixels that have not
been sampled for some reason; they might be empty, or have been corrupted in the image-forming process.
In any case, the goal is not to change the image sampling, but rather to fill the ”holes” with the most accu-
rate prediction according to the pixel value surrounding them. A relatively simple version of kriging, called
ordinary kriging, has been used in the code of this work. Ordinary kriging takes into account the statistics
of the whole image to generate the unsampled locations’ values; it does not attempt to calculate the local
neighborhood of each hole that is meaningful for modelling the area’s statistical behavior (for instance, by
means of the range of the autocorrelation function).
The kriging system of equations has not been included here, as it is only used once throughout the work and
as a part of a coded routine to compute missing/corrupted values in the Magellan topography processing (see
5.3); it has been deemed too large and complex relatively to its importance in the framework of this work.
3.3.3
2-D image filtering
Image filtering has also been performed outside the framework of SAR images. The main difference between
the images considered here and the SAR ones is their probabilistic nature: SAR images have been shown
to have a stochastic nature due to the phenomena known as speckle, while the images considered here are
deterministic (targets do not exhibit variability; in other words, the histogram of a specific target is uniform).
2 different filters have been used in the framework of this work: the Hamming filter and the Taylor filter.
3.3.3.1
Hamming filter
The Hamming filter, also known as the Hamming window, is a signal processing window technique that
operates to smooth the transition between pixel values, in order to reduce the spectral leakage 17 of the data
acquired because of the sampling operation and characteristics. When performing a Fast Fourier Transform
(FFT) of a signal, which is a standard procedure for processing data in different operational levels, the
finite sampling framework of a computer makes the theoretical delta function shape of the frequency domain
17The generation of new frequency components in a signal’s spectrum that do not belong to the signal, produced by the
multiplication of the signal with a time-variant function (for instance a window function).
51
==PAGE:53==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
be instead a sync function shape, which is in fact the same operation as to applying a simple rectangular
filter/window to the data. The delta function would be the theoretical perfect response of a simple sinusoidal
wave, but the signal to be analyzed is usually much more complex and containing a variety of frequencies.
Therefore, FFT operations produce sync spectrums that in some cases present errors, for instance, strong
frequency responses away from the sync function peak. Spatially, this can correspond to patchy pixel behavior,
or spikes in pixel values. The Hamming window applies an additional filter in the data spectrum that smooths
out its edges, eliminating the so-called side lobe components that are represented by frequencies displaced
from the main lobe. Particularly, the hamming window effectively suppresses the first side-lobes of the initial
sync-like spectrum, which is achieved when the constant α is set to 0.5435 in the following mathematical
definition of the (more general) zero-phase Hann-shaped window [6] (the properly called Hann window sets
α = 0.5):
h(n) = α −(1 −α)cos(
2π
N

n)
n = 0, 1, 2, ...N −1
(3.45)
where n is the sample index and N the number of samples. In the framework of this work, the Hamming
window is applied as a 2-D kernel in order to spatially smooth the signal observed in an image.
3.3.3.2
Taylor filter
The Taylor filter is the other 2-D window that has been considered in this work, together with the Hamming.
Similarly, it represents another shape of filter that is applied in the 2-D domain in order to smooth the data
sampled signal. The Taylor window is a variation of the more general Dolph–Chebyshev window, which
minimizes the Chebyshev norm 18 of the signal’s spectrum side-lobes for a given width of the main lobe. In
its zero-phase lagged form, the Dolph-Chebyshev window w0[n] can be defined as:
w0

The complete theoretical description of the Dolph-Chebyshev window following this equation can be found
in [47]. The Taylor window is a particular variation of the Dolph-Chebyshev window that uses a Taylor
expansion approximation in the edges of the time-domain window to avoid edge discontinuities. It is also
commonly used in the field of SAR processing.
3.3.4
Geographic Information Systems
Geographic information systems is a range of settings to operate on data that represents geographical phe-
nomena, and includes data capture and reparation, data management, data manipulation and analysis and
data presentation [80]. Particularly, in the framework of this work, GIS has been used to transform, with
a set of tools, the Magellan mission accessible topographic information with the aim of achieving pixel-level
values of the local radar incidence angle (see 3.2.1.4).
3.3.4.1
Slopes: angle and aspect
Topography information, understood as a 2-D image whose pixel values provide height information in a given
reference framework, can be transformed by means of GIS to derive pixel-level values of slope [50]. Slope
information is synthesized by 2 values in a 2-D frame: the slope angle α and the slope aspect ϕ. The angle α,
also called the slope gradient, is the angle between the horizontal plane and the slope path p. The adequate
slope angle is chosen as the maximum value for all the possible paths p that can be created between a pixel
position and that of its neighbors [80]. In a 2-D image that corresponds to 8 possible options. The slope
18The largest distance between two points in 2 sets of points or lines.
52
==PAGE:54==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
angle can be given in angular measures or in percentage; in this work, angular values (°) have been used.
Mathematically, α is defined as:
α = arctan

where δf is the change in topography/height from one pixel to another and deltap is the horizontal distance
between pixels. On another hand, to define the direction of the path p that gives the maximum α value, a
polar framework is used. The angle of the path, starting from the local northern direction of a pixel and
included between 0 and 360°, is called the azimuth or aspect angle and provides the path orientation. By
calculating and assigning a slope angle and aspect to each pixel, a slope field/map can be derived from an
initial topographic image. The aspect angle is calculated as [80]:
ϕ = arctan

3.3.4.2
Local incidence angle mask
If the slope angle and aspect are known, together with certain characteristics of the SAR orbital path and
observation regime, it is possible to extract values of local incidence angles at a pixel level.
First, the azimuthal orientation of the sensor’s flight track with respect to the scene north direction needs to
be computed. In the case of spaceborne SAR acquisition, due to the global extent of the observations in a
quasi-spherical planetary body, the orbit’s inclination only provides this information if the scene is placed in
the equator. The adequate measure to provide this 2-D information in the form of an azimuth angle is the
so-called apparent angle of the ground track with the meridian. This angle, mathematically defined as j′, is
found in any point of a spherical planetary surface with the following equation [59]:
j′ = arctan


case of Venus).
This equation allows the calculation of an azimuth angle between the flight track and the north (y) direction
for each pixel on a given image. Once this angle is known, together with the pixel’s local slope aspect ϕ
and angle α and the SAR incidence θ0 (not local) angle, the local incidence angle θ for each pixel can be
calculated as:
θ = θ0 −α · cos(ϕ −j′)
(3.52)
It is important to remark that this equation only works if the references of each of the angles match. In the
case of this work, the east direction is the reference (0° angle) for the azimuthal values of the aspect ψ and
apparent meridian angle j′. In order to visualize better the possible local incidence angle outcomes of this
equation, a layout of the particular observation geometry of Magellan is shown inf figure 3.16 for clarification
purposes. Essentially, the slope aspect angle acts as weighting/modulation (following a cosine function) that
regulates the impact of the slope angle α on the incidence angle θ0. If the sensor’s flight direction and the
aspect angle are the same (or the opposite), the impact of the slope angle on the local incidence angle θ0 is
effectively zero, and hence θ = θ0 (planet curvature corrected). For more insights on this calculation and its
results on the particular case of Magellan topography, consult section 5.3.

for a given apparent meridian angle j′ and slope angle α.

==PAGE:56==
Data & missions description
This section presents the several missions and the datasets that have been used throughout the study. It
documents the missions and data characteristics, the sources of the datasets, and the different processing
steps applied to them in order to be able to use them for the purposes of this project. The data has been
divided and presented according to the mission with which it was acquired.
4.1
The Magellan mission
The Magellan Spacecraft’s data constitutes the primary dataset used in the project, as it is the most recent
and accurate data, in terms of radiometric and spatial resolution, of the surface of Venus. Radar products
include SAR imaging products and altimetry products, and the sensor also acquired radiometry products.
From these, the imaging products have been the ones widely used for the SAR change detection strategy
described in chapter 5, supported by altimetry products.
Furthermore, Magellan also produced a set of
gravity measurements using radio tracking techniques.
4.1.1
Overview and objectives
The Magellan Venus Radar Mapper spacecraft [16], a space probe funded, designed and operated by NASA/JPL,
was a mission launched into an interplanetary transfer trajectory to Venus on May 4th, 1989. It entered Venus
gravity field on August 10, 1990 and after the orbital insertion maneuver and several calibration oeprations,
the first mapping cycle of the mission started on September 15, 1990. The mission performed a total of 6
mapping cycles as well as an aerobraking experiment and a so-called ”windmill” experiment prior to the end
of the mission, which was oficially stated to be on October 13, 1994. Each of the mapping cycles lasted 1
Venus day, equivalent to 243 Eath days. The primary payload of the mission was a radar system with Syn-
thetic Aperture Radar (SAR) imaging capabilities together with altimetry and radiometry modes. Moreover,
the spacecraft’s coherent X- and S-band radio subsystem was used to acquire gravity field measurements by
precision tracking of the spacecraft’s orbit. The mission’s primary objectives were:
• To produce near-global radar images of the Venusian surface with resolution equivalent to optical
imaging of 1 km per line pair.
• To obtain a near-global topographic map with at least 50 km spatial resolution and 100 m vertical
resolution.
• To map in a near-global scale the Venusian gravity field with 700km resolution and 2-3 milligals accuracy.
• To develop, by means of the radar and gravity products, an understanding of the geological structure
of the planet, including its density distribution and dynamics.
4.1.2
Orbital characteristics
The Magellan orbit is closely connected with the observational capabilities of the mission’s radar and therefore,
the characteristics of its products. It had similar altitude range as most Low Earth Orbit (LEO) satellites
55
==PAGE:57==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
during its observations, but its elevated eccentricity induced significant height variations between the periapsis
and apoapsis orbital positions. The reason behind the choice of eccentricity was to allow the data to be
transmitted (tape playback) back to Earth in a long radar-standby period throughout the apoapsis region
every orbit (except in times of superior conjunction line-of-sight interruption [16]), thus allowing an increase
in radar swath length that could ensure the coverage objectives of the mission [40]. The following table
summarizes the Magellan orbital information:
Semimajor axis (a)
10190 km
Excentricity (e)
0.382
Inclination (i)
85.3 °
Periapsis height
275 km
Apoapsis height
8029 km
Longitude of the ascending node (Ω)
291.1°
Argument of periapsis (ω)
170°
Period (P)
189 min
Venus rotation rate x orbit
-0.1944°/orbit
Equatorial ground track shift
20.54 km
Table 4.1: Magellan orbital characteristics. Source: Saunders et al. (1990) [16].
The mission follows an almost polar orbit (the choice of 85° was in part motivated by the fact that the mission
could map the poles directly with the side-looking radar [40]). This allows global coverage (as long as the
sensors are active) as the planet rotates. The orbital plane orientation was very stable (very minor rate of
change of Ω), and that allowed for each successive ground track to be separated by a stable 20.54 km in
the equator, achieved by the Venus slow rotation (LEO satellites ground track separations are much higher
on Earth due to the planet’s much faster rotation). The high eccentricity also induced a very large orbital
period, which allowed for almost 2 hours of data transmission while the radar was not active (see figure
4.1), and sufficient mapping time at the periapsis side. Furthermore, because of the height variation induced
throughout the orbit, mapping in the periapsis also suffered from a significant height variation: height was
around 2100 km at the beginning and end of the mapping swath, while it was just 275 km in the middle
(periapsis position) [16]. The atmosphere en Venus is much thicker than the Earth’s, and due to its conditions
and the planet’s gravity, its layers couple with the surface much closer than on Earth, reducing the total
atmospheric height to around 250 km. Because of this, radars in orbit around Venus can map the surface
from a closer distance than they would on Earth.
Important to mention is also the mapping geometry: the periapsis is not found in the equator but rather
displaced to the northern hemisphere by a factor of 10° (because of ω being 170° instead of 180°); the location
of the periapsis on ground is actually to a 9.5° N latitude [26]. This implies that the potential coverage was
not symmetric between the 2 hemispheres: in fact, the coverage achieved was (for the first nominal mission
plan), from 90° N to 68° S. Successive cycles of the extended mission also allowed to cover the south polar
regions. The mapping geometry can be visualized in the next figure:
56
==PAGE:58==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 4.1: Lateral view of a Magellan orbit around Venus. Notice how the eccentricity allows separating
mapping and transmitting times, giving data downlink enough time to process and send the data back to
Earth. Mapping always occurs from north to south. In order to extend the in-orbit length of the mapping
time, the successive observation times were offset approximately 5 minutes, which minimized overlapping in
the polar regions and allowed to extend the observations to latitudes further south (successive swaths are
shown as north swath and south swath in the figure). Source: Saunders et al. (1990) [16].
The impact of the orbital conditions on the radar system, particularly the height variation, the ground track
separation and the mapping time/swath time, are addressed in the following point.
4.1.3
Radar system characteristics
The Magellan spacecraft possessed a multi-purpose radar that had an observation regime that included
consecutive SAR imaging, altimetric and radiometric measurements.
A typical radar burst involved the
transmission of 150 to 800 SAR pulses, 17 altimeter pulses, and a 50-ms radiometer listening period [26].
The SAR operated a side-looking high-gain antenna, while the altimetry and radiometry modes operated a
nadir-looking antenna. The radar design was constrained mainly by the necessary orbital particularities of
the mission introduced previously, by a smaller antenna than those used on Earth-based SARs, and by a
demanding downlink data rate in order to fulfill the mission goals [16]. The main SAR characteristics that
are necessary for the purposes of this work are given in the following table:
Radar frequency / wavelength
2.385 GHz /12.579 cm
Slant-range spatial resolution
120-360 m
Azimuth spatial resolution
120-150 m
Number of (angular) looks
5-15
Swath width
20-25 km
Incidence angle on surface
18-50 °
Polarization
HH
Nominal operating altitudes
275 - 2100 km
Pixel size (full resolution)
75 x 75 m
Dynamic range
50 dB
Table 4.2: Summary of Magellan SAR characteristics. Source: Saunders et al. (1990) [16].
The radar frequency corresponds to an S-band observation, and the swath width roughly corresponds to the
57
==PAGE:59==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
separation of the ground track in the equator (see table 4.1), which allowed to eliminate orbit-to-orbit gaps
and reduce the overlapping at the edges of the swath in most latitudes. The dynamic range, together with
a significantly constrained number of bits per sample, allowed to obtain σ0 images with enough radiometric
accuracy by means of the Muhleman law [37]. The Magellan radiometric resolution was approximately 1.75
dB [129].
The quality and usefulness of SAR images is a function of the number of looks, the spatial resolution (in
slant-range and azimuth), the radiometric resolution, the signal-to-noise (SNR) ratio and the local incidence
angle θ. In the Magellan mission, all of these characteristics were variable to a certain degree due to the
orbital characteristics. The minimum total system SNR from the mission design perspective was 5 dB [16].
The height reduction during the in-orbit radar operation time had the effect of reducing this value, degrading
the image quality, and to compensate this effect the spacecraft was steered to adapt its look angle (manip-
ulating the acquisition incidence angle). High altitudes provide lower SNR than the low ones; therefore, the
incidence angle of the observations at orbital positions with high altitudes (corresponding to high latitudes)
was decreased, because lower incidence angles provide more powerful returns (due to a more suitable geom-
etry), which in turn are able to compensate the loss of SNR [18].
The manipulation of the incidence angle to maintain a stable SNR had the effect of varying the range
resolution with altitude/latitude; lower incidence angles produced coarser resolutions of the order of 360
meters at the beginning and the end of each orbit mapping time, while the resolution was maximum at
the periapsis position (120 m). At the same time, changes in altitude also affect the azimuth resolution
by increasing the time duration between SAR bursts, which affects the radar capacity to form a synthetic
aperture. In the end, the azimuth resolution throughout most cycles of the mission was controlled to be kept
at around 120 m by increasing the duration of the bursts with increasing altitude [18]. For the same reason, the
number of azimuth-angular looks performed by the on-board system was also adapted; to keep the resolution
constant, higher averaging values to a maximum of 15 were required in the higher altitudes/latitudes. It is
also important to highlight the looking geometry of the SAR: for the majority of imaging products obtained,
the radar operated in left-looking mode (cycle 1 and cycle 3), although a little more than half of the locations
were also imaged with a right-looking geometry by reorienting the spacecraft in cycle 2. All imaged locations
for CD purposes and the vast majority of SAR products used in this work belong to cycle 1, which follows the
left-looking incidence angle varying geometry described above; nevertheless, 3 cycle 2 and 1 cycle 3 imaging
products have been used in section 5.5 as data points.
On another hand, the altimeter was operated as a nadir-looking radar and provided estimates of height
(calculating the echo return times) and of RMS slope and reflectivity from the measured returned intensity
and time dispersion [27]. The extent of the footprints (spatial resolution) it produced on the ground varied
with latitude as another consequence of height variation: 12 x 8 km (along and across track) in the periapsis
position and up to 27 x 15 km in the orbital mapping times with the highest altitudes. The altimeter had
the capacity to distinguish height variations as low as 30 meters (the scientific requirement of the mission
was 80 m) [26].
4.1.4
Data sources
Magellan data is entirely available to the general public, free of charge, and has been distributed by NASA.
The Planetary Data System (PDS), a NASA-supported long-term archive of digital data products from the
several planetary missions of NASA, hosts the totality of the products acquired during the mission’s life [27].
The PDS is actively managed by planetary scientists around the US, including from JPL and from several
US universities, in order to ensure the usability and correction of the stored data.
The PDS Geosciences Node Venus Orbital Data Explorer (ODE) tool, managed by the Washington University
in St. Louis, has been used to sort, select and download the data.
58
==PAGE:60==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
4.1.5
Data products
The SAR imaging products selected are Mosaicked Image Data Records (MIDR) derived from the Magellan
radar system raw data. The mosaicked records correspond to selected areas of stacks of the original pole-
to-pole strips corresponding to the Full Resolution Basic Image Data Records (F-BIDR). Since F-BIDRs are
raw data in form of long, narrow image swaths or ”strips” 20-25 km wide (depending on the latitude), for
the interest of studying specific locations wider than a single swath the strips are pieced together to form the
mosaics (F-MIDRs). 2 types of MIDR products have been considered for the purposes of this work, although
there is a total of 4 types:
• Full resolution Mosaicked Image Data Records (F-MIDR): 7168 azimuth lines x 8192 range samples
with a pixel size of 75 x 75 meters.
• Compressed resolution Mosaicked Image Data Records (C1-MIDR): 7168 azimuth lines x 8192 range
samples with a pixel size of 225 x 225 meters.
• Twice-times-compressed resolution Mosaicked Image Data Records (C2-MIDR): 7168 azimuth lines x
8192 range samples with a pixel size of 675 x 675 meters.
• Three-times-compressed resolution Mosaicked Image Data Records (C3-MIDR): 7168 azimuth lines x
8192 range samples with a pixel size of 2025 x 2025 meters.
Only F-MIDR and C1-MIDR products have been used. They are provided with header information which
allows retrieving the latitude/longitude reference of each pixel and the corresponding incidence angle (not
corrected by topography). Magellan SAR MIDR products are tagged with a specific format. To describe it,
the example of the C1-MIDR product c100n197 can be used as a reference: C1-MIDR product tags follow the
format of c1 (compressed once), followed by 2 digits of latitude (00), by the latitude hemisphere (indicated
with an n for north and s for south), and finally 3 digits of longitude (up to 360°). The same tag applies to
F-MIDR products, which have the initial letter f instead of a c1.
On another hand, the reading and processing of 2 altimetry-derived products has been performed as well:
the Global Topographic Data Record (GTDR) and the Global Slope Data Record (GSDR), although in the
end only GTDR has been used. The GTDR provides values of planetary radius, with an origin mean value
of 6040 km. The product is a 4096 x 8192 pixel image that covers all planetary longitudes and the latitudes
between 66.487° N and 66.522° S, with a pixel size of 4.641 x 4.641 km in the equator [165].
4.1.6
Data processing
Each product is read following the format and the specifications provided in the PDS [165]. Each MIDR
is structured in 56 framelets of 1024 x 1024 pixels each. The latitude and longitude data from the MIDR
images’ corners is read and, by means of interpolation and projection, each pixel in the 7168 x 8192 image
is assigned a geographic reference. The code allows for 2 different projections: sinusoidal (see Appendix
10.1), following the relations provided in the data readme files [165] [27], or linear, which is a simple 2-D
linear interpolation of values from the corner values. For fairly equatorial areas, and for the product size of
an F-MIDR image, little difference has been observed between the 2 methods, and in most of these cases
linear interpolation speeds up the computation process significantly. Nevertheless, to follow the sinusoidal
projection is the physically correct procedure.
When it comes to associate each SAR image pixel with an incidence angle, the 1995 data manual [37]
provides a series of tables for each Magellan mapping cycle that provide values of incidence angle linked with
corresponding latitude values. From the tables, further work performed in [66] provides a best-fit polynomial
model that enables the direct computation of the incidence angle for any given latitude (for any given pixel):
θ = 0.00008λ3 −0.0127λ2 + 0.1825λ + 45.665
(4.1)
59
==PAGE:61==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Once each pixel longitude, latitude and incidence angle is obtained, the values of the backscatter coefficient
σ0 can be derived from the pixels’ DN by means of the Muhleman scattering law [2]. The Muhleman law is
an empirically-derived model based on experimentally distributed height and length variations of a planetary
surface’ scattering facets, which fits existing radar measurements. In this case, the law was adapted to fit
the expected Venusian surface behavior when observed with a 12.5 cm wavelength. The importance of this
law in Magellan processing is that it allows the scaling of the dynamic range of the data to be compressed
into the 8-bit format of the pixels, regardless of the incidence angle of the observations. The equation used,
tailored to the Magellan mission, is [37] [66]:
σ0 = 100.1(
−20+(DN−1)
5
)
0.0118cos(θ + 0.5)
(sin(θ + 0.5) + 0.111cos(θ + 0.5))3
(4.2)
with the dB version of σ0 reducing to
σ(dB) = −20 + (DN −1)
5
(4.3)
With these equations and the previous processing, the SAR image data is radiometrically calibrated and
holds sufficient information for the different methodology steps and the results of chapters 5 and 6.
On another hand, the GTDR product also requires of a specialized reading and pre-processing step. The full
global map consists of a total of 32 framelets of 1024 x 1024 pixels each. In this case, a Mercator projection
(see Appendix 10.1) is used to find the latitude and longitude value associated to each of the pixels, although
sinusoidal and also 2 polar stereographic projections are available.
It is important to highlight that the pixel sizes of MIDR products do not correspond to the ones in the
GTDR product. In order to reference each SAR image with corresponding topographic values in a pixel
level, special care is needed to properly process the topographic data. The final goal is not to associate
simple height values to each MIDR pixel, but rather to calculate a local incidence angle for each of them.
The series of post-processing steps to achieve that are not considered basic data reading and processing and
hence, they are properly addressed in section 5.3.
The following figure 4.2 provides an example of an F-MIDR product backscatter image (dB) of the Sif Mons
area, together with the full GTDR product after reading and pre-processing.

Right: GTDR product with latitude-longitude references using a Mercator projection. The definitions of
both projections and the formulas used in this work can be found in the Appendix 10.1.

4.2
The VERITAS mission
The Venus Emissivity, Radio science, InSAR, Topography, And Spectroscopy mission [107], will be the
next NASA discovery-class mission to perform observations of a planet of the solar system, together with
DAVINCI. VERITAS launch date is still to be scheduled as of 2022, but it is expected between the 2028-2030
launch window to Venus. VERITAS 7 science objectives are closely related with quantifying the similarities
and the differences between the Earth and Venus, in line with the geological and climatological questions
stated in chapter 2. To achieve its goals, VERITAS will carry, on one hand, the Venus Emissivity Mapper
(VEM) that will map surface emissivity using infrared bands that operate in 5 identified atmospheric win-
dows, and on the other, a SAR instrument named VISAR, with SAR imaging and interferometric capabilities.
VERITAS, in its initial configuration, will possess 2 phases of science operations: the first one will feature
an elliptical orbit with characteristics similar to Venus Express, during approximately 100-150 days, in order
for the VEM instrument to acquire comparable spectrometry results [107]. After an aerobraking maneuvre,
VERITAS will acquire a circular orbit configuration and perform the science phase 2 which will provide up to
3 cycles of observations (3 Venusian days) for VISAR to provide full coverage of the planetary surface. The
orbit of this science operation is expected to be polar, with i = 88.5° and an almost constant height between
175 and 250 km (close to the Magellan observation height at its periapsis). The ground track spacing in the
equator is calculated to be of less than 10 km, due to the planet’s slow rotation
Taking into account the orbital characteristics, VISAR will provide a side-looking 14.4 swath image, not
only providing SAR imaging products but also interferometric phase and coherence due to a dual antenna
configuration, which will allow single-pass interferometry in order to extract a global topographic map (DEM)
of the planetary surface. The DEM is expected to have a 250 m spatial resolution and a height accuracy of
just 5 meters, improving Magellan topographic information by an order of magnitude [100]. The main radar
characteristics, that are required to follow several studies of this work, are given in the following table:
Radar frequency / wavelength
7.9 GHz / 3.8 cm
Slant-range spatial resolution
7.5 m
Azimuth spatial resolution
2.1 m
Number of (angular) looks
7-25
Swath width
14.4 km
Incidence angle on surface
32 °
Polarization
VV
Nominal operating altitudes
180-250 km
Table 4.3: Summary of VISAR characteristics. Source: Hensley et al. (2020) [129].
The fixed interferometric baseline between the antennas for single-pass interferometry is 3.1 m. Since inter-
ferometric performance is not assessed in this work, only the imaging products produced by VISAR are of
interest, in order to compare them with Magellan. It is nevertheless remarkable to state that VERITAS will
also produce repeat pass interferometry of 12-18 designated sites of 200 x 200 km, which, if the atmospheric
contribution to the interferometric phase is well modelled, should allow retrieving phase differences associated
with possible surface changes [168] [129].
VISAR has similarities and differences with respect to the Magellan SAR observations. The radar wavelength
is significantly smaller: from 12.6 cm (S-band) to 3.8 cm (X-band). The spatial resolution of the sensor is
significantly better, up to 2 orders of magnitude in certain locations. The effective azimuth resolution will
be globally downgraded to 30 meters to allow for 25 angular looks, which will reduce the images’ speckle; in
certain locations (20 % of the surface) the averaging will be less, with 7 looks producing effective azimuth
resolutions of 15 m [129]. Swath widths are similar (approximately 75 % of the Magellan value), but more
61
==PAGE:63==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
importantly, look angles are constant throughout the SAR observations, as well as the orbit altitude (at least
comparing to the Magellan variations). This implies that only a limited array of locations will be imaged by
roughly the same incidence angle by the 2 missions. Particularly useful for comparison purposes in this case
will be the Magellan observations of cycle 2, which were fixed to incidence angle values around 25 °, allowing
a constant relationship in terms of observation geometry between sites.
VISAR data is not available. Nevertheless, by means of EO X-band SAR missions (TerraSAR-X, SRTM),
simulated images have been created. The VISAR images are Magellan images tuned to match EO data
points, and they do not require a particular reading and pre-processing routine, as they are generated by
transforming the already processed Magellan images.
4.3
The EnVision mission
EnVision is the new ESA medium-class science mission to Venus. It is expected to be launched in 2032
under the current mission plan [133]. Its science goals compliment those of VERITAS, particularly when it
comes to the investigation of the planet’s surface, as EnVision carries a synthetic aperture radar VENSAR,
to be produced in conjunction with VISAR at JPL. The mission also carries a subsurface radar sounder, a
radio science experiment, and a suite of spectrometers that will target atmospheric and surface emissivity
characteristics, which altogether are expected to provide the necessary knowledge to constrain the Venusian
surface and climate dynamics.
In orbital terms, EnVision is significantly similar to VERITAS. After arrival and an aerobraking manoeuvre
1, it will be placed into an orbital inclination of i = 87.7 −88.6°. The eccentricity will be slightly larger than
for VERITAS, but the height variation will be constrained to a periapsis of 220-290 km and an apoapsis of
355-527 km [133]. Therefore, each cycle of the mission should be comparable, in terms of coverage, to that
of VERITAS. EnVision is expected to have 6 science operation cycles of a Venusian day each.
On another hand, VENSAR will be operated to provide high-resolution SAR imaging data (25-30 % of the
surface) of 7.5 m range resolution (30 m effective ground resolution) by using a 15.5 MHz bandwidth on
one hand, and 2.5 m range resolution (10 m ground resolution) of selected sites (2-5 % of the surface) by
using a 60 MHz bandwidth on the other. These modes are referred to as reconnaissance and high-resolution,
respectively. The instrument will be able to operate also in altimetry mode, providing a footprint of 3 km
after Doppler beam sharpening and an altitude accuracy of 20 meters (also about an order of magnitude
improvement from Magellan in terms of spatial resolution), and in radiometry and SAR stereo modes. The
VENSAR characteristics that are critical for this thesis work are provided in the following table 4.4:
Radar frequency / wavelength
3.15 GHz / 9.5 cm
Slant-range spatial resolution
7.5 & 2.5 m
Azimuth spatial resolution
3 m
Number of (angular) looks
9-16
Swath width
57 km & 20 km
Incidence angle on surface
20-40°
Polarization
HH, HV
Nominal operating altitudes
220 - 530 km
Table 4.4: Summary of VENSAR characteristics. Source: Hensley et al. (2020) [129].
The two SAR imaging operation modes described above, reconnaissance and high-resolution, produce dif-
ferent effective azimuth resolutions by means of different number of angular looks (16 and 9, respectively).
1Spaceflight orbital procedure that reduces the orbit’s apoapsis by flying the vehicle through the atmosphere at the periapsis.
62
==PAGE:64==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
They also possess different swath widths, as described in the table. VENSAR will have a radiometric reso-
lution of 1.1/1.2 dB (depending on the cycle), which is an improvement of 0.5 dB with respect to Magellan.
On another hand, VENSAR carrier frequency is S-band, which implies that the scattering properties of the
ground should be significantly close to those of Magellan, at least for a suitable range of incidence angles. In
that sense, VENSAR look angle is adapted (like Magellan) to produce incidence angles of 20 to 40° because
of the in-orbit height variations, which affect the stability of the system’s SNR.
One important characteristic of VENSAR is its ability to produce dual polarimetric observations; VENSAR
will emit horizontally polarized waves and receive both in vertical and horizontal, producing HH and HV
signatures. HH image data from locations with shared mean incidence angle with Magellan data should
provide the closest observational match between the missions in terms of the scattering properties of the
ground.
VENSAR data, likewise VISAR, has been simulated by modifying Magellan processed images with a certain
factor obtained by radar signatures comparison with EO data from the NovaSAR-1 satellite (see 5.6).
4.4
The Shuttle Radar Topography Mission
The SRTM mission was NASA and DLR collaborative shuttle mission of 11 days, launched on February
11, 2000, from the Kennedy Space Center in Florida. The mission, which included an astronaut crew of 6
members, lasted 11 days and included a large interferometric SAR (dual antenna) for single-pass interfer-
ometry, with the main objective of producing a global DEM with the best resolution of the moment [58].
The SAR systems operated with both C-band and X-band observation capabilities. This work is concerned
only with the X-SAR instrument, and particularly SAR imaging only (without the slave image that allows
interferometric processing).
4.4.1
Mission characteristics
The NASA shuttle Endeavour was placed upside down and tail forward, following an exact-repeat circular
orbit of altitude h = 233 km. The orbit inclination was 57°, which implied no coverage of the poles. The
X-SAR system on board the shuttle produced partial coverage of the Earth’s surface between 60° N and 58°
S. The main characteristics of the system were:
Radar frequency / wavelength
9.6 GHz / 3.1 cm
Slant-range spatial resolution
15 m
Azimuth spatial resolution
20 m
Number of (angular) looks
2
Swath width
50 km
Incidence angle on surface
54°
Polarization
VV
Nominal operating altitudes
233 km
Table 4.5: Summary of VENSAR characteristics. Sources: ESA, Hensley et al. (2020) [152] [129].
The significantly lower bandwidth of the SRTM pulse modulation (9.5 Hz) compared to the other missions
considered in this work provided a coarser single-look slant-range resolution of 15 m. At the same time, the
steep look angle of 52° that the mission featured (mean incidence angle of 54°) was chosen to avoid layover
in mountainous areas [152], but the look angle could be steered between 17° and 60°, providing a wider array
of incidence angles.
63
==PAGE:65==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
This mission was chosen to provide simulated scenarios of the VISAR mission particularly because its radar
wavelength is almost equal (3.1 cm for SRTM and 3.8 cm for VISAR), which could provide the closest matches
in terms of scattering mechanisms of the imaged surfaces. The operating altitude and the incidence angle
are also equivalent in certain Magellan products, which provides suitable scenarios to empirically constrain
the future observations of the VERITAS mission.
4.4.2
Data products processing
The X-SAR SRTM image data provided to this project was kindly compiled and delivered by DLR’s Earth
Observation Center Geoservice Team, under no license issue. The processing steps to read the data were
delivered by SAR specialist Helko Breit [154]. The SAR image provided is a Single Look Slant-range Complex
(SSC) image 2 of the Hawaii island (the largest island in the Hawaii archipelago), which includes coverage of
the west and south-west flanks of the Mauna Loa volcano. The acquisition date was February 18, 2000.
The reading operation performed considered a Sun-raster file type 3. The number of pixels of the image is
of 39784 records in azimuth with 3092 samples in range each. Special care was taken to design a reading
function that could read the pertinent information within the file. The data header of the SRTM file is 32
byte long, and the data depth of each pixel is 4 bytes (32 bits). Every 32-bit SSC pixel is divided into a
16-bit integer real part and a 16-bit integer imaginary part. For reading purposes, the byte-order is Most
Significant Byte (MSB), and the integers are signed according to two’s complement coding.
After reading the Sun-raster file, a complex image with the specified dimensions was obtained, and sub-
sequently the absolute value (amplitude) was retrieved. The intensity image associated was calculated by
squaring the result. Nevertheless, the absolute calibration of the intensity image into an equivalent σ0 was
not achieved due to the lack of information regarding the calibration constant Ks and the NEBN estimates.
Therefore, the image obtained works itself as an indicative of the relative brightness of the targets within it,
but cannot be directly used to provide empirically comparable σ0 relationships with VISAR. This inconve-
nience is briefly addressed in 5.6, where the processed images obtained are presented. Figure 4.4, at the end
of this chapter, shows a modified amplitude image of the SRTM scene for visualization purposes.
4.5
The TerraSAR-X mission
The TerraSAR-X EO satellite mission is a German Aerospace Center (DLR, in german: Deutsches Zentrum
f¨ur Luft- und Raumfahrt) satellite with an X-band SAR imaging system, heir of the X-SAR and SRTM
missions [82]. It was launched into orbit on June 15, 2007, and it possesses a variety of imaging modes and
capabilities, including 3 acquisition modes (Stripmap, ScanSAR and Spotlight), fully-polarimetric observa-
tions, and along-track interferometry. It continues to operate to this date, and together with the secondary
twin satellite Tandem-X launched on July 21, 2010, it has produced one of the most accurate DEMs of the
Earth’s surface by means of variable-baseline across-track interferometry.
4.5.1
Mission characteristics
The mission follows a polar LEO Sun-synchronuous orbital configuration with a repeat period of 11 days,
producing global coverage with the radar operated with different modes. The eccentricity of the orbit is very
low (0.0011-0.0012) and the inclination is 97.44°[82]. The mean orbital height (very low variability) is of
514.8 km. A priori, the orbit characteristics are similar to those of VERITAS (and also EnVision).
2Complex image in slant-range geometry produced by a SAR sensor. It is often referred to with the acronym SLC.
3The Sun Raster image file format was the native bitmap format of the Sun Microsystems UNIX platforms using the SunOS
operating system.

6-looks averaging reduces these values to approximately 1.5 dB [76].
4.5.2
Data products and processing
A subset of TerraSAR-X Stripmap data products was requested and granted to the author by the Earth
Observation center of DLR. The data was provided via the EOWEB Geoportal [149] and contracted under
a user license for the utilization of TerraSAR-X / TanDEM-X data and products for scientific use (proposal
number GEO3848). 3 study areas imaged by Stripmap products were selected:
• Mt. Kilauea area (Hawaii). UL: 155.913° W, 19.474° N; LR: 155.090° W, 18.878° N.
• Mt Etna area (Sicily). UL: 14.890° E, 37.839° N; LR: 15.086° E, 37.654° N.
• Mt Anak Krakatoa islands area (Indonesia). UL: 105.349° E, 6.061° S; LR: 105.490° E, 6.179° S.
UL stands for Upper-Left corner, while LR refers to the Lower-Right corner. Due to time constraints, only
the products of the Kilauea area were used to produce results, particularly, the part of the products that
actually corresponds to another volcano; the Mauna Loa Volcano (Northeast flank). The acquisition date of
this particular scene is August 30, 2018.
The processing of the data, in preparation of the studies presented in 5.6 and the results of chapter 6,
consisted in the following steps [95] [76]:
1. Reading of the TerraSAR-X SSC product in Tagged Image File Format (TIFF). A subset of 16000 x
16000 pixels (azimuth x slant-range) is selected from the original 48218 x 30253 SSC image.
2. The non-calibrated power image is obtained by squaring the absolute value of the SSC image.
3. The calibration constant Ks [95] is obtained from the product’s metadata, which comes in an ancillary
file in XML format.
4. A comprehensive calculation of a noise estimate for each pixel is computed. The noise factor is the so-
called NEBN introduced in chapter 3. The TerraSAR-X system computes estimates of system noise in
6 azimuthal positions during the duration of the acquisition of the product. For each of these positions,
the metadata provides 8 coefficients to fit a 7th grade polynomial in the slant-range direction. To do so,
65
==PAGE:67==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
the data provides the range-time validity with a maximum time τmax and a minimum time τmin. Once
the NEBN of each slant-range pixel for the given azimuth NEBN-measurement positions is obtained
with the polynomials, 2-D interpolatioin has been used to expand the measurements to all the pixels
of the image. The following figure 4.3 provides visual insight on the NEBN calculation process:
Figure 4.3: Layout of the pixel-level calculation of the NEBN. Source: derivated from AIRBUS TerraSAR-X
manual [95].
5. Estimation of the incidence angle θ0 of each pixel by 2-D linear interpolation of the metadata values.
6. After computing NEBN and θ for each pixel, the calculation of the estimate of the backscatter coefficient
σ0 for each pixel can be performed, following the next equation [95]:
σ0 =

4.6
The NovaSAR-1 mission
The NovaSAR-1 EO satellite mission is a joint technology demonstration initiative of SSTL (Surrey Satellite
Technology Ltd.), the UK government, and Airbus Defense & Space, funded by the UK Government via
the UKSA (UK Space Agency) [125]. It was built with the main objective to demonstrate that an effective,
calibrated, medium resolution SAR system can be incorporated in a low cost satellite of 430 kg (which is a
rather small mass if compared, for instance, with the 1230 kg of TerraSAR-X), but it provides global SAR
coverage with S-band, an underutilized observation band in the field of spaceborne radars [150]. This is
particularly interesting as it makes NovaSAR-1 a suitable EO reference for VENSAR, which also uses an
S-band (and for Magellan).
4.6.1
Mission characteristics
The NovaSAR-1 mission features a circular (e = 0.00165) LEO polar orbit with an inclination of i = 97.5°
and a constant altitude of approximately 580 km. The orbit is sun-synchronuous with a mean local solar time
on the ascending note of 10:30 h. It is similar to the future VERITAS orbit, although with slightly higher
inclination (on Earth, inclinations have to be retrograde to allow orbital precession in the same direction as
the Earth’s precession around the Sun), and with the double of altitude because of the differences between
the two planets’ atmospheres.
When it comes to the SAR design, the radar has a versatile operations array, including Stripmap, Stripmap
wide and ScanSAR, each of them with several polarization capabilities [150]. The main characteristics are
summarized in the following table 4.7. They correspond to the Stripmap operational mode (VV polarization),
as it is the only one used in this report:
Radar frequency / wavelength
3.1 - 3.3 GHz / 10 cm
Slant-range spatial resolution
6 m
Azimuth spatial resolution
6 m
Number of (angular) looks
3
Swath width
20 km
Incidence angle on surface
16-25.38°
Polarization
VV
Nominal operating altitudes
580 km
Table 4.7: Summary of NovaSAR-1 radar characteristics in Stripmap mode. Source: CSIRO [166].
The azimuth resolution is similar to that of the range direction after an azimuthal averaging to reduce speckle.
The radiometric standard deviation of the system is 0.41 dB. The radar can be operated as right-looking or
left-looking, but in the case of the data obtained for this work it is operated as left-looking.
4.6.2
Data products and processing
The NovaSAR-1 data products used in this work have been obtained via the CSIRO NovaSAR-1 National
Facility Datahub of Australia. CSIRO stands for Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organi-
zation, and the access to the hub is open and free of charge to researchers. Within the limited coverage of
the mission in the Indo-Pacific area, 1 suitable study area has been selected. The study area and the product
are:
• Mt. Anak Krakatoa islands area (Indonesia). UL: 105.349° E, 6.061° S; LR: 105.490° E, 6.179° S.
• Single Look Complex Stripmap product (VV), ascending left-looking geometry, obtained April 25, 2020.
67
==PAGE:69==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
A TIFF format reading routine is used to load the full product. The complex image is obtained and con-
verted to magnitude detected, and later on squared to obtain the non-calibrated power image. A calibrated
β0 image is obtained by multiplying the obtained power image by a calibration constant Ks and subtracting
the noise estimate analogously as in section 4.5.2. The pixel-level estimates of incidence angle θ0 to convert
β0 to σ0 are provided by a slant range fitting of a 5-th degree polynomial, with coefficients provided in the
metadata file associated to the product.
The product dimensions are 27680 azimuth lines with 4870 slant-range pixels each. Since most of the product
corresponds to ocean, and the interest of the study area is the lava flows and terrain from the volcanic con-
struction of the Anak Krakatoa island, a subset image has been selected. The selected scene has dimensions
900 x 1200 (azimuth x slant-range) in number of pixels.
The particular interest of the data for simulation purposes, as well as the different conclusions of its analysis,
can be found in section 5.6.
Finally, to end the presentation of the different missions, data products and processing, the following figure
4.4 provides an example/overview of the different EO scenes considered in the work. The figure is a mosaic
of the SRTM, TerraSAR-X and NovaSAR-1 scenes for visualization purposes; the scenes are not scaled and
are not the same image type.
Figure 4.4: Mosaic of the 3 different EO products selected to perform analysis on the future VISAR/VENSAR
radar signatures of volcanic terrain. Left: Amplitude SRTM image of the west side of the island of Hawaii,
Hawaii, processed as a SRTM quick-look image according to [154]. Middle: Section of a TerraSAR-X detected
product of the northeast flank of the Mauna Loa volcano, Hawaii, calibrated to a σ0 intensity image. Right:
Anak Krakatoa island (2020) as observed by NovaSAR-1 Stripmap mode, calibrated to a σ0 image.
