==PAGE:70==
Methodology and studies
This chapter is intended as a compilation of the different steps, tasks and studies performed that, altogether,
have provided the framework for the end-to-end change detection results that are given in chapter 6. The
chapter is organized as: first, an analysis on the main factors that influence the choice of a change detection
strategy, second, a choice, definition and argumentation of the strategy itself, and third, a series of studies
focusing on the several parts that the algorithm needs to perform.
5.1
Radar signatures of Venusian lava flows
The definitions of a suitable and efficient SAR change detection strategy requires, first and foremost, knowl-
edge on how the expected changes to be detected will look like, from the radar perspective. As carefully
described in 3.1, and as argued as target in 2, the expected changes are lava flows. The lava flows should have
radar behaviors similar to terrestrial Aa and Pahoehoe lava flows, should appear or develop close to rifts or
shield volcanoes, and should in general possess a basaltic composition, with the Venusian conditions allowing
for significant flow lengths. This scenario has been taken as the prototype case for identifying suitable regions
in Venus.
Many Magellan MIDR products (if not all) have been used for the analysis of this section at one point or
another. Nevertheless, there are specific products that are particularly interesting from the change detection
perspective because: first, they are samples of the prototypic scenario introduced above, and second, they
are regions that correlate with the indirect signs of volcanism summarized in 2.3. These regions have been
used in chapter 6 to provide possible change detection examples in Venus, and they are often used as test
areas for the majority of studies presented in this section. They are:
• Maat & Ozza Mons: C1-MIDR product number c100n197 from disk 0033 of the Magellan PDS
archive [165]. Center coordinates: 0° N 163° W.
• Idunn Mons: F-MIDR product number f45s218 from disk 0029 of the Magellan PDS archive. Center
coordinates: 45° S 142° W.
• Sif Mons: F-MIDR product number f25n351 from disk 0004 of the Magellan PDS archive. Center
coordinates: 25° N 9° W. It should be noted that Sif Mons has been chosen due to the interesting
geometry and disposition of the lava flows of its landscapes, rather than because of studies leading to
considering it volcanically active today.
From the radar perspective, the degree on how detectable lava flow changes are depends on 2 factors: which
radar observable is the information-bearer parameter, and how discernible are the lava flows according to
it. This can only be assessed from the available data (and hence from the available data resolution), in this
case Magellan SAR images. Nevertheless, a wide assessment of Magellan images has been deemed sufficient,
according to the observations of this work, to characterize these 2 factors.
The Magellan wavelength (see 4.1.3) is low enough to ensure that the dominant scattering mechanism of
the images is surface scattering. Therefore, radar brightness should be dominated by the surface roughness
69
==PAGE:71==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
and the observation geometry (see 3.2.1.4). The first radar signature to be assessed is the radar normalized
backscatter power σ0, which should correlate with these 2 factors. After σ0, the presence of texture in the
lava flows and the images in general is assessed in order to see if it correlates with terrain types. Furthermore,
remarks are also given about possible changes associated to dielectric constant variability.
5.1.1
Backscatter coefficient
The backscatter coefficient σ0 is the most important radar observable for the purposes of this work. This is
mainly because of 3 factors:
• It is a normalized parameter that usually allows differentiating targets in a SAR image, when all phase
information has been discarded and speckle has been reduced.
• It makes the observations independent of the radar acquisition (see radar equation 3.1), which is
necessary in cases of image comparison between different sensors.
• It is well-characterized, mathematically, under certain assumptions (see 3.2.1.3).
SAR MIDR products from Magellan were processed as indicated in 4.1.6 and visually analyzed. The first
important step of the visual inspection is to corroborate that σ0 carries information to discern different types
of terrains, particularly lava flows from other background terrains and also from other flows. This implies
that new lava flows can subsequently be identified by a particular mean σ0 signature, different from the
surroundings. Visual inspection corroborates that mean σ0 values are clearly associated to different types of
terrain, which means that σ0 is the information-bearing parameter that can discern lava flows (at least it is
one of them). The following figure 5.1 provides a variety of Magellan scenes from different location, without
a speckle reduction routine, and the association of different terrain characteristics and morphologies with
different σ0 values is clearly evident.
Figure 5.1: Mosaic of 6 σ0 1024 x 1024 framelet images (dB) of different Venusian terrains.
The tags
identifying each product are seen on top of each scene. Notice how the bottom-right image, corresponding
to an Idunn Mons framelet of higher latitude (south) than the others, is larger than the rest: the pixel size
increases as the latitude gets away from ≈10° (the periapsis of observations).
70
==PAGE:72==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
In the 2 left scenes of figure 5.1 (top and bottom), digitate flows with different lengths and shapes are seen
on the slopes of Sif Mons shield volcano as they develop and intertwine towards the surrounding plains and
rolling uplands. Their σ0 values are clearly higher than the surroundings, allowing for clear delineation of
the flows. The smallest contrast between identifiable flows and surrounding terrain is of ≈2.5 dB, which is
higher than the 1.75 dB radiometric accuracy of the Magellan. The middle column scenes and the top-right
scene show bright lava flows, coexisting with other bright signatures such as ridges, rifts and cracks in the
surface, and craters. In this case, flows are also easily identifiable from surrounding backgrounds, and even if
σ0 signatures of cracks are similar to those of the flows, these are assumed to be stable in relation to moving
lava and are not expected to introduce uncertainties if constructing an intensity ratio image (see 3.2.3.2).
Finally, the bottom-right scene corresponds to a more complex scenario, an array of interacting lava flows
in Idunn Mons that show smoother transitions between them, with speckle in some cases effectively blurring
the borders between them. This type of scenario is less common than the ones of the other scenes in the
figure, but is nevertheless present in a variety of locations of interest, including another of the volcanoes that
is thought to be active (Sapas Mons).
The differences seen in this scene in particular have been chosen to assess the limiting scenario, i.e. the most
difficult case in terms of discerning lava flows by their σ0. These difficulties arise due to the radiometric sim-
ilarity between flows, aggravated by speckle, and due to their spatial disposition, which leads to overlapping
of flows with similar characteristics. Nevertheless, after close inspection, the conclusion is that radiometric
changes down to the theoretical radiometric resolution of Magellan should be enough to identify and delin-
eate flows even in this complexity, if proper image reconstruction techniques are used in the change detection
algorithm. Since VISAR and VENSAR both will have better radiometric resolution, lava flow detection to
the same level as Magellan or better is expected for these systems as well. Therefore, σ0 can be assumed to
be a suitable radar observable to identify lava flows from other types of terrain in Venus. This, therefore,
strengthens the option of using a test statistic of the intensity ratio image as a change detection method.
5.1.2
Texture
Texture is propriety of SAR images that has been introduced in section 3.2.1.3. It is often the case where
SAR targets present an excess RCS variability that is not associated with speckle fluctuations but rather
to the variability of the scattering pattern (because of the heterogeneous surface characteristics) [64]. The
analysis of this section has 2 objectives:
• Assess if Magellan SAR intensity images possess a significant degree of texture.
• Identify if texture is an information-bearer parameter in order to discern lava flows from other types
of terrain; i.e. texture effects on the RCS differ significantly from one type of flow to other types of
terrains, but also to other types of flows.
To infer the presence of texture, the estimation of the ENL metric as described in section 3.2.1.3 is an ad-
equate method. Estimating the ENL of a homogeneous patch of a given σ0 and comparing it to the total
number of looks L applied to the data provides information on pixel correlation, radiometric smoothing and
texture. Since no speckle filtering has been applied to the images, only inter-pixel correlation and texture
can be the causes of an ENL lower than L. Magellan data is expected to have significant pixel correla-
tion (the pixel average size in the closest observation point is 75 x 75 meters and the spatial resolution is
120 x 120 m in the best-case scenario), hence ENL should be lower than L even if no significant texture is
present. Nevertheless, if the proportions between L and ENL vary according to the location and to the type of
terrain of the patch, then texture is present, and this is what has been observed in the analysis presented here.
An array of 18 1024 x 1024 framelet locations of F-MIDR products encompassing different types of terrains,
and including multiple lava flows, has been analyzed. For each product, 2 patches have been compared, one
targeting a homogeneous area a priori not visually identifiable with a lava flow, and a second one targeting an
identifiable lava flow. The following figures present the results, with the captions pointing out their general
71
==PAGE:73==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
remarks. It is important to mention that the scenes presented in this section’s analysis suffer from a flip on
the scale, which has not been possible to correct due to the project schedule. The scale is reverted: large
dB values correspond in fact to low dB values, which implies low σ0 linear values and therefore corresponds
to darker regions, meaning that the bright/dark contrast in the scenes is flipped. Nevertheless, this does
not affect the consistency of the contrasts in the scene and has no impact on the ENL values; it is just a
visualization error.
Figure 5.2: Compilation of 6 framelet scenes from 6 F-MIDR products. The rectangles in blue represent
the homogeneous patches, used to calculate the theoretically higher ENL, while the rectangular areas in red
are associated with patches of lava flows (and have been used to calculate their associated ENLs). The first
important point to highlight is that the ENL of the homogenous areas varies throughout the scenes. This
is not due to changes in pixel-correlation but to changes in the number of angular looks applied; as it was
pointed out in section 4.1.3, the number of azimuth looks is adapted depending on the latitude. On another
hand, all the ENL of the flows are lower than those of the homogeneous areas, meaning that lava flows are
textured. Finally, the proportions between ENL of the blue and red patches are not equivalent between
scenes, which implies that the degree of texture is not the same for all flows.
Figure 5.2 illustrates how flows are seen to present a varying degree of texture in relation to more homogeneous
surrounding terrain. Further analysis was required to establish if this is a global trend, and the results of
figure 5.3 do not corroborate it; in fact, an array of very different behavior and situations was observed:
72
==PAGE:74==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 5.3: Compilation of 12 framelet scenes from different F-MIDR products, analogously to figure 5.2.
The behaviors of the 12 scenes analyzed exhibit different traits. The top 6 scenes (analysis 1) correspond
to fairly equatorial latitudes, while the bottom 6 scenes (analysis 2) are from latitudes around 45° south.
Regarding analysis 1: the flow patches in the 3 scenes of the top row, as well as the bottom-mid one, present
comparatively higher ENL (lower texture) than the patches identified as ”homogeneous”. These generally
refer to background terrain in this case, as their lower ENL highlights that they are in fact less homogeneous
than the flows. The scenes in the bottom-right and bottom-left have very similar ENL values for the 2 patches,
indicating that texture levels can be significantly similar independently of the terrain. Regarding analysis
2: the 3 scenes of the top row exhibit flows that are less textured than the surrounding ”homogeneous”
areas. On another hand, the 3 bottom scenes present the opposite behavior, with flow patches slightly more
textured, comparatively, than the surroundings.
73
==PAGE:75==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
The conclusion from figure 5.2 and 5.3 altogether is that texture is present, to a certain degree, in all the
types of terrain observed. The texture level varies depending on the flow, and this can be physically induced
by a variety of factors, including the local slopes and the lava formation process. Different radar texture
in different flows indicates different surface morphology, which indicates different effusion rates, composition
and eruption durations (see 3.1). In any case, what it is clear is that, even though texture is present in
most terrains, particularly in lava flows, it does not seem to possess the necessary information that allows
to separate one lava flow from another or from a certain background type. This is because similar texture
characteristics appear to induce similar ENL values in different types of terrain; that is, a single lava flow
texture does not appear unique to that flow and hence, the detection of the signature can lead to terrain
misclassification.
Therefore, the Venusian surface possesses a certain degree of texture, from a radar perspective, and this
degree is variable from one location to the other, but texture itself does not seem to be an information-bearer
parameter that allows to delineate lava flows from other features.
5.1.3
Dielectric properties
Lava flow backscattering changes in the case of VISAR/VENSAR and Magellan, due to dielectric changes
(see 3.2.1.4.2) of the first layers of the surface, are a priori not important due to the small amount of volume
scattering/penetration achieved by their wavelength size. Studies on Earth have found that wavelengths need
to be at least 70 cm for σ0 to possess a significant volume scattered power fraction in lava flows; the majority
of σ0 comes from surface scattering due to surface roughness [31].
The dielectric constant ϵ of the surface is obtained by estimations of the Fresnel reflectivity and the surface
RMS slope, found by fitting a model, the so-called Hagfors scattering model [23], to each group of returned
measurements of the Magellan altimeter [37]. Very high ϵ values are observed in highlands, from ≈8 to
27, while lava flows posses lower values, generally from 1 to 9. High values are associated to 2 possible
mechanisms: internal volume scattering, which requires a low internal propagation loss coupled with efficient
internal reflection in the first surface layers, or scattering from a single layer close to the surface possessing a
very high effective dielectric permittivity [24]. Due to the short wavelength of the missions, and the models
obtained by comparing reflectivity estimates and emissivity from Magellan radiometry [31], the second mech-
anism has been generally accepted, with a layer of pyrite material seen as the responsible for high ϵ values.
Putting together the evidence and the studies, the dielectric behavior of the lava flows has been assumed
and characterized, but the dielectric constant has been discarded as a useful information-bearer parameter
for terrain classification. This is because:
• The dielectric constant cannot be directly estimated with a SAR imaging sensor, but rather needs a
model fitting of different altimetry-derived quantities, and for instance VERITAS does not include an
altimeter that can provide dielectric estimations of the surfaces it observes.
• Lava flows that are physically discernible in the Magellan SAR images, for instance because of different
mean σ0, present similar dielectric properties. Only in several cases, for instance in the flows southeast of
Maat Mons, lava flows present abnormal dielectric constants compared to their surroundings (probably
due to a chemical composition including titanium or iron) [31].
• In the global picture of the Venusian surface, only high altitude locations in the continental Terrae
exhibit markedly different dielectric constants, and these areas do not usually contain many volcanic
constructs.
74
==PAGE:76==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
5.2
Change detection strategy
With the background and the objectives described in chapter 2, the knowledge of the different mission
capabilities in chapter 4, and the study of the radar signatures of the Venusian lava flows and surface in
general, a suitable change detection strategy is formulated in this section. This strategy is then constructed
and tested, step by step, in the rest of the sections of this chapter, and is put in practice as a whole in the
results of chapter 6.
5.2.1
Despeckling vs segmentation
In chapter 2, incoherent change detection by means of a test statistic was established as the core procedure
for the CD algorithm. As explained in 3.2.3, the test statistic usually works as an image rationing operation,
which is done at pixel level. Because SAR images possess speckle noise (see 3.2.1.2), image reconstruction
techniques are necessary to identify mean radar signatures (see 3.2.2). One discussion within the framework
of the algorithm is whether to use a despeckling technique, which simply reduces variability, or a segmentation
technique, which assigns pixels to classes with the same intensity value. After evaluating the different pros and
cons of the 2 strategies, despeckling techniques were chosen as the image reconstruction step of the algorithm.
If the detection of changes between images is sought to be detected at pixel level, as the smallest unit where
change could be identified, then preserving the variability of nearby pixels to a certain extent might preserve
information that is otherwise lost if all pixels considered belonging to the same target are associated to a
class value, as algorithms of segmentation do (see 3.2.2.1.3). Additionally, in the case of image comparison
between 2 different SAR systems, a transformation of the mean intensity values occurs even if no physical
change happens. This transformation can be predicted by means of theoretical or empirical models (see 5.6),
but the accuracy of it might not be equal throughout the considered images. Therefore, a heterogeneous
bias is introduced in the scene to a certain degree. Magellan VISAR/VENSAR image intercomparison can
be classified as presenting 2 different types of change: the system-change, which describes the changes in the
image when no physical change actually occurs, and the total-change, which accounts for system change and
the real/true physical changes. In this scenario, when constructing the image ratio histogram of a CD scene
(that includes system change and real change), segmentation presents 2 characteristics: reduces the spectral
resolution on one hand, and precludes the use of the probabilistic framework to select a threshold, on the
other. Both effects are closely related. Spectral resolution refers to the number of distinguishable histogram
bins; since segmentation equalizes the intensity values that are considered to be part of a target, it is not
possible then to distinguish pixels that belong to the same target by extracting information solely from the
histogram. At the same time, because the histogram values do not follow a probability distribution such
as the one in 3.2.3.2, the threshold value can therefore only be selected as an edge of spectrally-contiguous
bins/targets. By doing this, sensibility to changes is reduced to the accuracy of the segmentation process
itself; i.e. the error introduced in the segmentation process in delineating areas in both images is the error
that the thresholding operation possesses. This ”reduction” of the choice of threshold between ratio values,
aside of carrying the segmentation process error which might be unevenly amplified by the different accuracy
bias when calculating system changes, is not supported by a probability framework and hence, the detection
and error probabilities of pixel classification cannot be calculated accordingly.
In the end, the detection and error probabilities, when if not directly calculated by the mathematical frame-
work in 3.2.3.2, directly relate to the segmentation accuracy: if the error of classification in the segmentation
process in both images would be zero everywhere (and there would be no source of radiometric distortion
due to the change of the SAR instrument), the detection probability of true change would be 1 and the error
probability would be 0. Such an outcome is indeed desirable; however, it is improbable, and moreover, costly.
This is the last argument in this work to disregard segmentation algorithms in favor of speckle reduction.
Relatively simple despeckling techniques exist, which could be studied in the time frame of this work, but
segmentation algorithms require a more in-depth study and implementation, which would have impeded the
implementation of the full end-to-end change detection chain. To really confirm that thresholding by means
75
==PAGE:77==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
of the probabilistic framework of change detection is more sensible to delineate changes in the pixel level and
might be more robust in the case of bias, a segmentation strategy should be implemented and the results
compared. This is because the detection and error probabilities of segmentation cannot be a priori quanti-
fied, as explained.
To illustrate the particularities of despeckling and segmentation, the following figure offers a layout of a
simple change detection example between the missions, including the 2 types of change, the histogram shape
differences between the methods, and how these affect the choice of a change threshold.
Figure 5.4: Layout depicting an example of change (system change and real change) between a Magellan
and a VISAR/VENSAR image. The 2 rows illustrate the Magellan image, followed by a VISAR/VENSAR
image including real change, and a subsequent example histogram of the ratio between the 2 images, in
the case of segmentation (above) and despeckling (below). The slight change of colors between the images
represent the different backscatter values expected to be retrieved from different terrains due to changes
in the system (wavelength, observation geometry, etc.). The area enclosed by the red curve represents the
extension of a target identified in the Magellan image only due to the unequal system-induced changes in the
backscatter. On top of that, a real change (identified in purple) occurs in the same area. To the right of the
images, the histograms (not to scale) reveal their different shapes according to the reconstruction technique,
and particularly, the different shapes of the region containing system and real changes (red and purple lines).
Next to them, zooming in the histogram curve of this region, the source of possible misclassification differences
is depicted. When despeckling, the choice of threshold can be optimized to reduce classification errors by
using the theoretical framework described in 3.2.3.2; on the other hand, the segmentation operation itself sets
the threshold as the boundary ratio value between regions/bins. If the accuracy of this process, considering
also system-change-induced radiometric distortion, is lower than the margin for threshold choice optimization
given by the probability theory, then segmentation introduces larger errors.
In conclusion, simple filtering techniques to reduce speckle (see 3.2.2.1) have been selected as the SAR image
reconstruction step of the CD algorithm (described in full in the next section). The reasons behind this
choice are: the timeliness of the project itself, the inability to characterize a CD threshold from a probability
point of view, and the possible misclassification errors that come from the segmentation method accuracy.
Nevertheless, some robust segmentation method in case of varying accuracy could be proven to be effective
as well (see chapter 8).

DTU Space
5.2.2
Algorithm definition
Taking into account the objectives of the project, the theoretical framework of SAR images and change de-
tection described in 3.2.1 and 3.2.3, the radar signatures of the Venusian terrain described in 5.1, particularly
of lava flows, and the argumentation in support of simple despeckling techniques of the previous point, the
following end-to-end algorithmic chain for Magellan-VISAR/VENSAR change detection is proposed:
Figure 5.5: Magellan-VISAR/VENSAR change detection algorithm layout.
77
==PAGE:79==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
5.2.2.1
Description
Figure 5.5 represents the algorithm step by step. The 2 main inputs of the algorithm, highlighted in red, are
the 2 SAR intensity images, σ0 images in this case, of Magellan on one hand and future VISAR/VENSAR
on the other. Parallelly, an additional set of inputs, considered ancillary information, is also required. The
full list of necessary inputs and their description is the following:
• VISAR/VENSAR image: SAR σ0 image of a predetermined scene of the surface of Venus.
If
VISAR/VENSAR images are not available yet, Magellan-simulated images are used instead.
• Magellan image: SAR σ0 image of a predetermined scene of the surface of Venus, mapped by Mag-
ellan.
• Mgn GTDR: Magellan topographic information of the selected SAR scene.
• VERITAS/EnVision mission configuration parameters: necessary information from the mis-
sions’ orbits and the radars’ operation (see 4.2 and 4.3).
• Empirical σ0 model: Mathematical relationship predicting σ0 values of the Venusian surface as a
function of local incidence angles and radar wavelength, derived using Magellan and EO SAR data
products (see 5.5 and 5.6).
• Error probability: The a priori assumable or desired change detection error probability for a given
selected scene.
Following the ingestion of the different inputs, the algorithm steps until obtaining the output are:
1. Co-registration of the 2 SAR images: in the case of using Magellan images to simulate VIS-
AR/VENSAR (as it is the case in this work), no co-registration step is needed, but it will be critical
when the VISAR/VENSAR products become available.
2. Resolution homogenization and decimation: the VISAR/VENSAR images, as seen in chapter
4, will possess much more resolution than Magellan (and consequently a smaller pixel size).
Since
the Magellan resolution is coarser, VISAR/VENSAR images require an increased number of looks to
balance out the resolution and a decimation of the pixel size in order to allow pixel-to-pixel comparison
between the 2 images. This step is not necessary while VISAR/VENSAR images are not available, as
the Magellan-simulated images of VISAR/VENSAR have the same resolution.
3. Despeckling routine: a suitable despeckling routine is selected to reduce speckle in both images (see
5.4 for the optimum choice).
4. GTDR post-processing chain: the three yellow boxes in figure 5.5 from GTDR post-processing to
local incidence angle mask, represent, parallelly to the SAR images processing, the calculation of a local
incidence angle θ value for each pixel of the images. The Magellan topography refinement is the same
regardless of the mission, but each mission’s observational geometry creates a different θ mask.
5. Generation of a No-change simulated VISAR/VENSAR image: once the pixel values of the
θ angle for either VISAR or VENSAR are obtained, these can be used by the empirical model in order
to estimate the images σ0 values if no true change has occurred.
6. Generation of the σ0 ratio image: the VISAR/VENSAR image (or currently simulated versions)
are divided by the empirically-tuned Magellan image to create the ratio image described in 3.2.3.2.
7. Threshold selection: given the histogram of the ratio scene and the desired error level, a suitable
threshold can be calculated to classify pixels as change or no-change (considering only 1 class of change).
The mathematical description of the histogram requires of a new framework that considers images with
different number of looks (see section 5.7).
8. Detected change binary map: the output of the algorithm, if successful, is a map that identifies
the pixels that have been classified as change.
78
==PAGE:80==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
5.2.2.2
Argumentation
Several points need to be stated regarding the choice of the algorithm’s steps and the particular differences
between the algorithm’s current implementation and its performance when VISAR/VENSAR images will be
available.
Co-registration is always performed first, because any subsequent comparison is not possible without it. When
it comes to resolution homogenization, decimation and despeckling, all 3 tasks can be performed at the same
time when future VISAR/VENSAR images are available; the same despeckling routine with more number
of looks can be applied to them, and subsequently a simple decimation to equalize pixel sizes can be performed.
It has been considered more optimal to tune the Magellan image with the empirical model after image recon-
struction than before it. This is because the model itself provides an estimation of the mean σ0 values and
does not take into account the variability introduced by speckle: if the variability is still high when applying
the model’s conversion, possible errors in the model might lead to spikes in local σ0 pixel values. The model is
introduced here to convert a despeckled Magellan image into a VISAR/VENSAR equivalent; but while these
images are not yet available, it can be used to generate the input VISAR/VENSAR images as well. In this
case, since the model is used twice (to generate the VISAR/VENSAR image and to transform the Magellan
image to a VISAR/VENSAR-like acquisition), the errors that it might introduce cannot be discerned.
The only way to figure out the accuracy of the model is to compare a real VISAR/VENSAR scene, with a
priori no change detected, with the one created by tuning a Magellan image. In the case when the images
are available; the comparison between the model results and the real results from different scenes with no
changes can be used to quantify and constraint the extent of the system-induced changes. Once this will
be done, the transformation of the Magellan images will be possible with the least radiometric distortion,
optimizing the separation between pixels that exhibit real change and those which do not.
79
==PAGE:81==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
5.3
Local incidence angle
Surface scattering due to surface roughness is likely the majoritarian component of the power recorded
by any of the past or planned SAR missions observing the Venusian surface (see 5.1). As seen in section
3.2.1.4.1, for a given observation wavelength, the local incidence angle θ plays a central role in determining the
backscattered power component for any surface with a specific morphology. Therefore, in order to produce an
empirical model that recreates the recorded backscatter of any of the SAR missions to Venus considered, the
relationship between recorded backscatter values and their corresponding θ angles needs to be extracted from
data. Calculating θ requires knowledge of the missions’ orbital and radar characteristics, but, ultimately, a
good estimate of the planetary topography is required (see theory of section 3.3.4.2). The Magellan mission
provided altimetry estimates that were used to generate the GTDR product. Under a first inspection, the
product itself is seen to present a series of noisy features and artifacts that require a dedicated processing
chain to be identified and smoothed out. Once the smooth topography estimate is obtained, the slopes angle
and aspect can be calculated, taking special care in reducing the so-called Venetian-blind effect [23], and
finally, adding each mission’s parameters, an estimate of the local incidence angle can be obtained for a given
scene. This section exemplifies the specifically-designed processing from the initial GTDR product to a local
incidence angle mask that has been automatically implemented to any of the Magellan scenes considered for
change detection. The area used as example throughout the analysis corresponds to the area of the SAR
C1-MIDR product c100n197, which includes Maat and Ozza Mons.
5.3.1
Venus topography processing
The original GTDR product of the Maat and Ozza Mons area, interpolated to the SAR C1-MIDR product
pixel size, can be seen in the following figure:
Figure 5.6: Magellan GTDR product of the Maat and Ozza Mons area. The color bar represents elevation
in meters with respect to the Venus mean radius (see 3.1.1). The largest red spot to the center-left is Maat
Mons, while the smoother elevation pattern with the red spot to the upper-right part is Ozza Mons.
80
==PAGE:82==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
From figure 5.6, 2 effects were identified as errors or as undesired behavior, coming from the nature of the
acquisition process: the presence of spuriously low elevation values, which distribution a priori does not seem
correlated with their surrounding terrain trends, and the rather patchy appearance, particularly following
the mosaic pattern of the satellite tracks stacked one next to the other.
The first effect is related with an error in identifying the main peak of the recorded return signal by the
altimeter, and it is well documented in [25].
For a given spacecraft altitude, in an altimeter footprint,
multiple echo peaks (associated with different time delays) might appear in the altimetry waveform for
specific combinations of feature heights and feature positions in the footprint with respect to nadir. In this
sense, when high-elevation constructs sit at a distance of 20-25 km from nadir, it is possible that a secondary
power peak appears after the first one, and the processing of the radar might select this secondary one as
the main peak. Since the associated time delay with this peak is larger than that of the real point of closest
approach, the range/height associated to it is lower, generating a low elevation value such as the ones that
can be seen in figure 5.6. This effect is illustrated in the next figure:
Figure 5.7: Magellan altimeter waveform of the Sapas Mons summit area. A dome of ≈0.5 km sitting 25 km
off nadir might be responsible for the secondary power peak (≈0.0567 s time delay). The radar processor
selected in this case the secondary peak, generating a spurious low elevation value. Source: Jeffrey J. Plaut,
1992 [25].
On another hand, the second effect, sometimes referred to as the ”Venetian blind” effect, manifests itself as a
clear elevation differences following the spacecraft orbital tracks; a sharp contrast on both sides of the border
separating 2 Magellan swaths. This effect was also documented in the early stages of the mission [23], and
it is particularly visible also in SAR images, not only in altimetry. The Venetian-blind pattern is en effect
that, if not mitigated, causes severe distortion of the slope aspect, calculated from the topographic data. The
effect is shown in figure 5.8 (the slope aspect calculations are analyzed in the next point). The cause behind
this pattern effect lies in the poor knowledge of the topography of the surface before Magellan.
81
==PAGE:83==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 5.8: Example of ”Venetian blind” effect in a subregion of the Maat and Ozza Mons (including the
peaks). The effect is visible in the SAR pattern (left), but if the topographic processing does not mitigate it
to a certain degree, the obtained aspect angle (right) presents a striking artificial effect that effectively blocks
the underlying information.
When computing SAR backscatter or the elevation height associated to a time delay in altimetry, knowledge
of the exact location of the antenna beam provides accurate attenuation to each return in the swath/footprint.
If this knowledge deviates from reality, for instance due to the use of an incorrect or a coarse resolution DEM
(Magellan used data of Pioneer Venus with a resolution of ≈75km), sharp differences in attenuation might
arise in the borders between passes, where the gain patterns’ miscalculation translates into a larger power
difference. The following layout provides a geometric view of the problem:
Figure 5.9: The source of the ”Venetian blind” pattern effect is the incorrect attenuation of the antenna
pattern, which achieves maximum contrast in the borders between SAR or altimetry observation passes. If
the Pioneer-derived topographic model 4 used in Magellan significantly deviates from the ground truth, the
assumed antenna boresight directions (yellow), do not correspond with the real boresight positions (red) and
therefore, their attenuation values differ accordingly, generating the pattern.
82
==PAGE:84==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
The presence of these 2 effects/errors requires a specific post-processing routine for them not to propagate
to the calculation of the slopes angles and aspects. A combination of filters have been found, tailored to the
strength and nature of the errors, that is able to minimize their impact on the following phase. The filters
smooth the topography, partially eliminating the spurious low values and reducing the blind pattern effect.
Firstly, a 2-D moving window with dimensions of 7 x 8 pixels (following the SAR image dimensions geometry
of 7168 x 8192, which after interpolation corresponds to the local GTDR product as well) has been designed,
which calculates the standard deviation of each pixel within the window. After trial and error, 2 suitable
thresholds have been used as the minimum and maximum standard deviations that a spurious low value is
seen to possess. These values are a trade-off: values higher than the maximum threshold can be confused with
real sharp elevation changes, for instance the edge of a ridge. Lower standard deviations than the minimum
threshold, on the other hand, might select pixels that are simply moderate topography changes in rolling
uplands. Within these limits, nevertheless, a significant portion of pixels containing spurious low values can
be identified. Then, their values are marked as voided, and a kriging routine (see 3.3.2) is used to fill the gaps.
After removing the low topography errors, the ”Venetian blind” pattern is smoothed out in a first stage here
by applying a Hamming window 2-D filter. The use of a Hamming filter was decided experimentally: a suite
of filter routines was tried, and the smoothed-out results were compared with the original topography; the
results using a Hamming window showed one of the best performances in reducing the pattern. The results of
both processing steps can be visualized in the next figure 5.10. It is important to keep in mind that applying
a Hamming filter introduces a small bias to the topography results; in the example scene the mean bias is
0.15 meters, which is negligible, but at some points can be up to 200 m. since the final objective of the
processing is to generate values of local incidence angle of each pixel, and such calculation is very sensible
to the presence of the errors in the topography, the errors introduced are accepted as a ”necessary evil” to
obtain meaningful incidence estimates.
Figure 5.10: Sequence of the Magellan topography post-processing chain that addresses the 2 effects com-
mented above in the Maat and Ozza Mons example area. Left: Initial portion of the GTDR product of
the area. Middle: reduction of the spurious low values by means of a 2-D standard deviation filter and a
threshold. Right: mitigation of the ”Venetian blind” pattern effect by means of Hamming filtering.
5.3.2
Venus slope calculation
Once the topography has undergone the appropriate post-processing, GIS can be used on it in order to
calculate the 2 values that define a slope at any given pixel of an image: the angle and the aspect (as defined
in section 3.3.4.1). The first necessary step is to calculate the gradient in the x and y image directions (first
derivative), which corresponds to the height variation in each direction. After obtaining this and together
with the pixel size measures, the mathematical calculation of the slope angle and aspect for each pixel is
found using equations 3.47 and 3.48. Since the results of both variables still suffer from a residual patchy
83
==PAGE:85==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
appearance from the ”Venetian blind” effect, an additional filtering operation is applied to them with a
2-D Taylor window (see 3.3.3.2), which additionally smoothes the results. As it happened in the Hamming
filtering, this introduces a bias, which is seen to be globally negligible (mean error of 0.01°), although in a
few local values in the case of the slope angle, the bias can be as high as 5°. In order to filter the aspect
values without a discontinuity between 180° and -180°, the filtering is applied in the complex plane. After
filtering, a rotation is applied to all aspect values in order to obtain a reference of 0 to the East (x positive);
aspect values from 0° to 360°, instead of the original calculation of the arctan operation, which provides
values between 180° and -180° from the North (y positive) direction.
Figure 5.11: Results of the slopes angle and aspect values calculation, in the Maat and Ozza Mons area,
using the GIS framework described in 3.3.4 and the dedicated processing described in the lines above. The
left scene shows slope angles and the right their aspect values. In general, the topography is relatively gentle,
with only a few slope angles of around 10°, although this is slightly biased by the processing and influenced
by the Magellan’s altimeter resolution.
In order to corroborate that these results of figure 5.11 are in fact accurately describing the terrain, their
behavior relative to the topographic features is key.
Having the slope magnitude (angle) and direction
(aspect), the full slope field can actually be visualized by constructing contour maps. If these maps correlate
with topography, the GIS derivation of the slope values, even if limited to the topographic information
retrieved, are in good agreement with the data and hence, can be regarded as successful for the purposes
of this work. 2 specific areas of the example scene of this section have been chosen as tests to visualize
the derived slope fields, mainly because they present clear topographic features that are easily relatable to
possible slopes: these are the summits of the Maat and Ozza volcanoes, and they can be visualized in the
following figure 5.12.
84
==PAGE:86==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 5.12: Figure showing the Ozza Mons (top) and Maat Mons (bottom) summit areas topography,
together with the juxtaposition of slope angle and aspect fields, creating a slope field mapped with arrows.
The arrows’ directions correspond to the slopes’ azimuthal directions (aspect values), while their length
corresponds to the steepness of the slopes (slope angles).
Moreover, contour lines of equal slope angle
(steepness) have been added to the slope field maps.
Both areas exhibit very good agreement between
topographic features (height) and the surrounding slopes; they point away from summits (isolated height
points, which correspond to the ”sources” of the field) and die in local low grounds (sinks).
5.3.3
Computation of a local incidence angle mask
The last point of the processing chain that starts with topography information is the derivation of local
incidence angle maps, for any given scene, which take into account the GIS-derived slope field but also the
observation geometry of a specific SAR sensor at a specific location. This includes the sensor’s pass direction
(ascending or descending), look direction (right or left), incidence angle (for some missions depending on
the latitude) and direction of flight track with respect to the image local coordinates (summarized by the
apparent meridian angle).
Once the slope map is obtained for a specific scene, after all the processing described in this section, each
mission SAR sensor produces a unique local incidence angle mask, since at least their incidence angle (prior
to slope correction) is in general different. After using the theory in 3.3.4.2 and taking special care in defining
the observation characteristics for each mission and scene, the local incidence angle maps that correspond to
85
==PAGE:87==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
any Magellan SAR scene, as well as the projected future ones for VISAR and VENSAR for the same scene,
have been successfully obtained, at least according to the theory. The following figure illustrates the different
θ maps of the same Maat and Ozza Mons example that has been used throughout the section:
Figure 5.13: Comparison of local incidence angle masks (θ) for the different missions to Venus considered in
this work. The upper-left image depicts the Magellan SAR scene of the Maat and Ozza Mons area in dB
scale. Subsequently, the other 3 images show the obtained θ pixel values after topography post-processing,
the GIS derivation of the slope field of the scene and the final generation of the angle according to each
mission’s viewing geometry (see figure 3.16 for more insight). All masks are in good agreement with each
other and with the slope values computed.

==PAGE:88==

DTU Space
5.4
Despeckling analysis
An efficient speckling reduction is a critical step in SAR image intercomparison. As stated in 3.2.2.1, this
translates into reducing intensity images’ variability as much as possible without introducing radiometric
distortion. Ideally, such distortion can be corrected as a global offset of the whole scene after despeckling,
but this is seldom the case as the distortion is usually unevenly distributed. In this analysis, the several
considered filters introduced in 3.2.2.1.2 were evaluated, and their performance compared qualitatively and
quantitatively to choose the more appropriate filter to reconstruct Magellan, VISAR and VENSAR σ0 images.
For this purpose, an example area of a F-MIDR SAR product of Magellan was chosen; specifically, the
framelet number 30 of the f25n351 product of disk 0004, which corresponds to a particularly distinguishable
digitate lava flow in the north flank of Sif Mons. The following figure illustrates the area and its corresponding
trials of the different filters, with a 7 x 7 window size:
Figure 5.14: Sif Mons example area, with the original σ0 dB image in the upper-left position and the
corresponding despeckled results of all the filters introduced in 3.2.2.1.2. The choice of a 7 x 7 window
size was chosen after trial and error as a good first compromise between speckle reduction and resolution
preservation for testing purposes (see below for this topic development). The Frost filters’ sensitivity value
was also chosen by experimentation.
Qualitatively, the modified Lee and Frost filters (the more complex filters) appear to obtain the highest
degree of speckle reduction: the underlying weathered lava plain (dark) and the digitate lava flow (bright)
are visually more homogeneous.
However, to solidify the choice of filter, the metrics introduced in 3.2.2.1 are useful to establish which filter
behaves better, at least in terms of speckle reduction and radiometric preservation. Moreover, several window
sizes should be explored together with the metrics to conclude the optimal window size for the images. Due
to the similarities between Magellan and the new VISAR/VENSAR radars, in the sense that they are all SAR
87
==PAGE:89==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
systems that image or will image the same terrain with a predominance of surface scattering, it is assumed
that a correct filter choice for Magellan is also a well-balanced choice for VISAR and VENSAR. Nevertheless,
because of their different spatial resolutions, VISAR and VENSAR despeckling routines will present larger
window sizes, in order to balance out the sensor’s resolutions when comparing images.
The radiometric distortion is evaluated by how much the ratio image mean (see equation 3.8) deviates from
the ideal value 1; on the other hand, the level of speckle reduction is assessed by the ratio image standard
deviation; the higher it is, the more speckle is reduced. These 2 metrics were computed for each of the 5
despeckling filters, considering window sizes from 3 x 3 to 13 x 13, in the framelet area selected as a test (see
lines above). The results are summarized in the following figure:
Figure 5.15: Analysis of ratio mean (y axis on the left) vs ratio standard deviation (y axis on the right)
for each of the 5 filters considered and for each of the window sizes considered. The x axis in all the plots
represents the window sizes in pixels at each side of the window’s central pixel, in both x and y directions.
Observing figure 5.15, it becomes clear that the general trend is that speckle reduction is higher the largest
the window size is. This is natural, since higher window sizes imply more averaging and hence less statistical
variability, but at the same time comes with the setback of loss of spatial resolution. Furthermore, in general,
the radiometric distortion introduced by the filters does not follow this pattern; the best behavior in this sense
is obtained with window sizes of 7 x 7. In this specific case of study, the discussion revolves around what is
more important when detecting future physical changes in vISAR/VENSAR images with respect to Magellan.
Very large window sizes are not desirable due to significant loss of resolution, which particularly in Magellan
SAR images would be accentuated with respect to VISAR/VENSAR. Moreover, since in general the mean
backscatter differences between lava flows and with surrounding terrains have been seen to be substantial (see
section 5.1), a very large degree of speckle reduction is not a priori necessary to accurately delimit different
terrains beyond statistical variations. On another hand, radiometric distortion is an important parameter to
take into account in the scenario of Magellan and VISAR/VENSAR image interomparison, particularly due
to the reasons discussed in section 5.2.2. An important degree of radiometric distortion, distributed unevenly
throughout a SAR image, risks masking the real changes occurring between images, as the comparison is
88
==PAGE:90==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
already sensible to the prediction errors of the system changes introduced by the considered empirical model
between missions. In order to assess this possible behavior, the example framelet has been divided into 64
sub-frames of 128 x 128 pixels each. The ratio image mean for each of the sub-frames has been computed
using a 7 x 7 window size (for each of the filters), and the results are shown in the following figure 5.16:
Figure 5.16: Analysis of the ratio image mean for each of the 128 x 128 sub-frames of the example Sif Mons
scene. All filters exhibit a degree of distortion, as they deviate from the ideal behavior, and there exist certain
trends among the sub-frames which indicate that distortion is not homogeneous throughout the image. The
Frost filter exhibits the worst radiometric preservation behavior, while the Boxcar filter the best.
Analyzing figure 5.16, it becomes clear that even though the degree of radiometric distortion by all the
different filters is relatively small (maximum 2.75 % of deviation), there are significant variations between
sub-frames, ranging between 0 dB to 0.1 dB. The heterogeneity between sub-frames is partially explained by
the fact that all filters except the Boxcar adapt their filtering operation, in terms of internal weightings but
also spatial extent (depending on the filter, see section 3.2.2.1.2), according to the window’s local statistics.
On another hand, differences in texture across the image are responsible as well in modifying the filtering
output even in the case of the Boxcar filter; texture implies higher pixel correlation, which in turn means
lower effective resolution and different effective number of looks for the same spatial averaging.
An unbalanced radiometric preservation implies that, in practice, if the radiometric distortion of the process-
ing chain of the algorithm needs to be precisely known, the ratio mean deviation after despeckling cannot be
corrected by simply adding an offset to the image as a whole. This has motivated a conservative approach
when deciding the filter, at least in terms of distortion; since a window size of 7 x 7 appears to perform best,
this size has been selected as the appropriate size for the Magellan SAR images, modified to a 7 x 8 size to
match the SAR product dimensions of 7168 x 8192 pixels. Nevertheless, VISAR and VENSAR images are
expected to be averaged by larger window sizes, in order for the images to be compared to present comparable
spatial resolutions. The effects of this towards radiometric distortion should be assessed, however, when the
images become available.
89
==PAGE:91==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Once the optimal window size has been decided, a comparison between the quantitative behaviors of each of
the filters using this size is presented in the following figure 5.17. The best choice corresponds to that which
appears to better preserve the image while keeping a good degree of speckle reduction (high ratio standard
deviation): the results show that the modified Lee filter is the most suitable choice, which matches the initial
qualitative inspection of the scenes in figure 5.14.
Figure 5.17: Filter analysis comparison with 7 x 7 window size, considering all the Sif Mons example framelet.
Both versions of the Lee filter and the modified Frost filter reach good performances in speckle reduction,
but since the modified Lee filter is the one in general exhibiting a higher degree of radiometric preservation,
it is the most suitable choice for despeckling Magellan and future VISAR/VENSAR images. The level of
radiometric distortion homogeneity is equivalent for both Lee filters and the modified Frost one, as can be
seen in figure 5.16.

==PAGE:92==

DTU Space
5.5
The Magellan backscatter coefficient empirical model
Following the definition of the change detection algorithmic change in section 5.2.2.1, a critical step for future
Magellan/VISAR/VENSAR intercomparison is the adaptation or simulation of one of the images to be com-
pared, considering no physical change between images, to the system of the other one. In order to do that, the
characterization of the scattering level of the different Venusian terrain units is necessary. Such scattering,
predominantly from the surface level, depends majoritarily on the observation geometry, characterized by
the local incidence angle θ, and on the SAR wavelength λ. Therefore, ideally and with enough data, the
backscattered power of any Venusian surface unit can be characterized by means of these 2 parameters.
The finest resolution σ0 estimates of the Venusian surface come from Magellan products. Magellan, as stated
in section 4.1.3, featured a single-frequency SAR with a wavelength of roughly 12.6 cm. Nevertheless, due
to SNR stability issues, a wide array of incidence angles were used to map the planet, depending on the
latitude. Such differences in θ present, in fact, a considerable opportunity to assess the backscatter of the
different terrain types under different viewing geometries, for the same wavelength.
Of particular interest is the σ0 behavior of the common radar-bright lava flows that are usually found in the
vicinity of shield volcanoes or flowing towards plains, as these are the morphological units that are expected
to vary or expand in the case of ongoing eruptions.
In order to characterize this behavior for all the θ angles seen by Magellan, a survey of all the F-MIDR
SAR products of the mission was conducted.
A subgroup of 33 framelets, belonging to 11 different F-
MIDR products, was selected as a good sample of the prototypical lava flows observed by the mission. The
methodology followed can be summarized as follows:
1. Selection of a suitable scene that contains a lava flow, usually close to a volcanic construct or flowing
over a radar-dark plain. The fact that the incidence angle variation affects all terrains of a given scene
implies that regardless of the latitude, the contrast between lava flows and their underlying background
is enough for them to be discernible.
2. Generation of a local incidence angle mask for the Magellan scene, following the methodology explained
in 5.3.
3. Selection of a patch which is representative of the lava terrain and averaging of the σ0 values within in
order to obtain a mean σ0 estimate.
4. Analogously, averaging of the local incidence angle values of each pixel within the patch in order to
obtain an estimate of its mean θ value.
5. Fitting of all data points with an appropriate polynomial function.
This process is conducted not only for lava flow patches, but also, parallelly, with bigger patches which are
assumed to represent a heterogeneous balance of different terrains that are characteristic of the Venusian
surface as a whole. The objective behind this is to produce a Magellan σ0 vs θ0 model not only for the
prototypical lava flow terrain but also for an average of all the possible terrains and then compare them.
Ideally, a model should be generated for any a priori distinguishable and characterizable surface unit, but
if the mean behavior of all surface terrains is close enough to any of them separately, at least to the level
required to not introduce significant radiometric distortions when the model is used to transform a despeckled
SAR image, then the mean model might be enough to ensure successful change detection. An example of
the patches used to generate the model is given in the next figure 5.18:
91
==PAGE:93==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 5.18: Examples of patches for lava flow terrain units (left) and generic terrain units (right) used to
extract mean estimates of σ0 and θ values to use in the characterization of the empirical model. The ones in
this figure are examples and do not necessarily relate to the ones used to generate the model.
The selection of the framelets was carefully conducted as to be representative of all the different incidence
angles mapped by Magellan, and the final span achieved in terms of local incidence angles ranges from merely
3°, with a suitable combination of low incidence angle and slopes’ angle and aspect, to a maximum of 52°.
The samples are nevertheless not equally spaced, as due to the average slope field’s steepness and disposition
on one hand, and on the unevenly distributed coverage available for each incidence angle θ0 on the other,
most of the imaged terrains are found to have a local incidence angle between 25° and 50°. Nevertheless,
effort has been made to cover the gaps around low incidence values by looking for the appropriate geometries
in the SAR F-MIDR products. The model resulting from the analysis of lava flow patches, following all
the considerations expressed above, can be visualized in the following figure 5.19, while the one obtained
by the general terrain patches is presented in figure 5.20. The lava flow model is constructed with 33 data
points, while the all-terrains model only uses 30. This difference comes from the availability of meaningful
areas in the products to perform the averaging; while lava flow patches are smaller (a fixed size of 250 x 250
pixels) in order to fit within any homogeneous flow disposition, the all-terrain patches are much bigger (2389
x 6144 pixels), precisely to cover an area that is representative of the different terrains in the Venusian surface.
Both models exhibit similar curves, with a good agreement particularly in the trailing part of angles between
20° and 50°. From the physics of surface scattering, the results are in line with the expected scenario: when
the local incidence angles are very small, power returns are almost specular; geometry purely dominates the
echoes and hence most of the power emitted is scattered back to the radar. At angles around 20°, the lowest
backscatter values are obtained because the returns are still specular but the geometry does not favor the
collection of echoes within the radar antenna gain pattern. From that point, as the angle increases, so does
surface roughness, which in turn increases the power that is scattered back in all directions (without having
a purely specular/reflection component) and hence, the power received by the Magellan SAR.
92
==PAGE:94==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 5.19: Magellan σ0 lava flow empirical model. The y axis represents the backscatter coefficient, while
the x axis the different values of local incidence angle θ. The legend to the right shows the product from
which each data point has been calculated, and each data point is the mean value of a patch belonging to
a prototypical lava flow morphological unit with equal dimensions. The model can be clearly divided into
2 parts: the specular returns in the low angle values, which can be individually well-fitted by a 5th degree
polynomial, and the roughness-dominated return in the high angle values, approximately fitted by a linear
regression. The model as a whole can be represented by a 9th degree polynomial (see equation 5.1).
The polynomial expression for the full lava flow model, defined as a 9th degree curve, is the following:

(5.1)
Parallelly, the expression for the full all-terrain model, defined as a 9th degree curve as well, is the following:
σall
0
= 4.1649 · 10−12 · θ9 −9.3494 · 10−10 · θ8 + 8.6500 · 10−8 · θ7 −4.2062 · 10−6 · θ61.1156 · 10−4 · θ5

Although they appear to differ significantly if only looking at the expressions, the reality, corroborated by
figures 5.19 and 5.20, is that for the majority of angles θ the σ0 are significantly similar, or at least linearly
proportional, particularly around the area dominated by surface roughness (backscatter-dominant). In that
span of angles, where simple linear fits appear to work good for characterizing the backscatter, the slope
difference is only 0.002 1/°, which is relatively small compared to the slope values of 0.007 1/° for the lava
flow model and 0.005 1/° for the all-terrain model. Moreover, the fictional ordinates of origin (if extending
the lines until crossing the σ0 axis) for both linear fits are almost the same: -0.1339 for the lava model and
-0.1270 for the all-terrain one. These results are encouraging as they show partial agreement between the
93
==PAGE:95==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
models, which might imply that in average, the empirical correction of the model could be applied to all
types of terrain without distinction; knowledge of the wavelength and the local incidence angle might be
enough to transform an intensity pixel of Magellan to VISAR/VENSAR, and vice versa, without concerns
of very different responses provided by physically different surface units. Nevertheless, more data points
are required to fully confirm this behavior, particularly for the all-terrain model, which also suffers from
oversampling in the area of 45-50° while lacking information in the 25-40° span. Also, on another hand, the
behavior of the models differs considerably in the reflection-dominated part (low θ angles). It is possible that
reflections of only one type of terrain such as lava flows are more straightly directed back to the radar, and
hence achieve higher σ0 returns, while if accounting for different types of terrain at once the reflected power
suffers more interferences. However, not sufficient data points have been obtained in this area, and this is
probably hindering a meaningful characterization in both models, particularly in the all-terrain one. The
lack of data points is due to the lack of very low θ values; only very high latitudes are able to achieve the
adequate observation geometry, and the GTDR product use to generate local incidence angle masks for the
product is limited to 66.487° N and 66.522° S.
Figure 5.20: Magellan σ0 all-terrain empirical model. The y axis represents the backscatter coefficient, while
the x axis the different values of local incidence angle θ. The legend to the right shows the product from
which each data point has been calculated, and each data point is the mean value of a large patch including
a sample of different terrain types. The model can be clearly divided into 2 parts, analogously as with the
lava flow model: the specular returns in the low angle values, which can be individually well-fitted by a 5th
degree polynomial, and the roughness-dominated return in the high angle values, approximately fitted by a
linear regression. The model as a whole can be represented by a 9th degree polynomial (see equation 5.2).
94
==PAGE:96==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
5.6
The VISAR/VENSAR backscatter coefficient empirical model
In the previous section, Magellan data has been used to obtain characteristic curves and expressions linking
σ0 surface scattering from lava flows and averages of different terrains with a wide array of θ angles. However,
these models are only representative of the scattering achieved by Magellan’s radar wavelength; as surface
roughness is only relative to the wavelength, it is anticipated that sensors with different wavelength, in this
case VISAR (λ = 3.8 cm) and VENSAR (λ = 9.5 cm), will behave differently, particularly when surface-
roughness dominates the backscatter returns (angles from 20-25° up). In order to try to empirically tune
or compensate the models derived from Magellan, for them to try to mimic the future radar responses of
VISAR and VENSAR, Earth Observation data is the only useful and reliable source of information. This
section presents a simplified methodology, within this work time framework, that uses specifically designated
EO scenarios in order to generate the so-called VISAR and VENSAR σ0 empirical models.
5.6.1
Why to use Earth observation data?
The most prominent obstacle when trying to construct an empirical model for the SAR sensors in the new
missions to Venus is the lack of SAR data from the planet that uses their same radar wavelengths. If the
model to be constructed is purely empirical, to be used to simulate the system-driven changes in the observed
σ0 between missions, then a certain volume of test data is required.
One of the great advantages of Venus as a target is its many similarities with the Earth. As stated in 3.1.1,
Venus has roughly the same size as the Earth and, as a terrestrial analog, its bulk composition is inferred
to be similar to the Earth as well. The many studies about its surface conditions and volcanism have con-
cluded that, a side from certain differences in the possible eruption mechanisms and the lack of water erosion
cycle and vegetation, most surface terrain types in Venus have similar counterparts on the Earth’s surface
(see section 3.1.3.2.3). Particularly, Venusian lavas, which are in the spotlight of change detection in this
study, are seen to have very similar radar and radiometric signatures as to Earth basaltic flows with Aa and
Pahoehoe morphologies. Given that the Earth possesses an array of locations presenting such types of lava
flows, a good amount of them of easy delineation and without vegetation cover, it appears possible to locate
adequate scenarios which present the same physical composition on Earth than on Venus.
On another hand, given Earth locations that fulfill this analogy with Venusian surface morphologies, the
other half of the simulation procedure is to identify, within the existing catalog of EO SAR sensors, image
products of these locations that present a radar wavelength equivalent (or very close) to that of the new
missions and that additionally, present a viewing geometry that corresponds to Magellan scenes with the
same characteristics.
There are two layers of limitations in this aspect: the actual availability of missions that fulfill these criteria,
and which of the missions can in fact provide useful, calibrated products with a delivery scheme that is
acceptable and assumable by the author. After a mission survey and a literature review, 3 spaceborne SAR
EO missions were selected, with products over certain areas of interest (see the next points). These have
been introduced, together with their sources, study areas and processing, in chapter 4.
5.6.2
Adjusting the Magellan empirical model
The adjustment methodology to construct the VISAR and VENSAR empirical models, in the level reached
in this MSc thesis, is based on a correction of the values obtained in the Magellan models by substituting the
sample points that have EO-equivalent points. These points, which represent the simulated VISAR/VEN-
SAR σ0 values obtained, can be used as reference points to scale the full polynomial expression obtained with
Magellan. The points are obtained from EO data following the same patch methodology used in the previous
section 5.5, with the appropriate corresponding topographic information. The difficulty of the process lies
in 2 important factors, which cannot be a priori corroborated due to the lack of field work in the Venusian
95
==PAGE:97==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
surface locations, but that are assumed in the adjustment process: the physical equivalence between a terrain
unit observed on Venus and that of a selected Earth location, and the assumption that, to a significant de-
gree, the Magellan model possess the same level of scaling difference in all the angles θ that are not directly
observed in the EO test scenarios.This second factor, however, is not very robust because the fact that more
than one data point (at different angle θ) present different scaling already introduces a change of the overall
shape of the model, i.e. all angles do not equally scale with wavelength. To mitigate possible errors, one way
of proceeding is to secure a vast pool of Venus-equivalent locations with different mean θ angles, in a way
that reference points can be evenly spaced throughout the model span.
The accuracy of the adjustment process is critically linked with a good knowledge of the surface morphologies
of the terrestrial test locations, which are usually aided by volcanographic studies obtained after field work,
and the refinement of a model that can be used for any type of lava type and weathered surface. As seen in
the previous section 5.5, there appears to be certain agreement between the model trends for a heterogeneous
mix of the different surface units in Venus and a single terrain unit like digitate radar-bright lava flows.
In order to obtain an accurate model that, after adjustment, can correspond to the different terrains that
will be imaged by VISAR/VENSAR, wider sample groups that include morphologically-distinguishable lava
flows on Venus and also other weathered surfaces of the planet should be considered, and different model
fits should be conceived for each of them separately. Only after a designed comparison study of the different
terrain responses, conclusions can be properly drawn on whether a single VISAR/VENSAR model for all
terrains can be assumed at any location or if, on the contrary, assumptions on the types of terrain when
observing the future SAR images should be used to select the more appropriate model from the different
ones devised. Thsi is also constrained from the change detection accuracy point of view; determining how
much radiometric distortion (due to model inaccuracies for the different terrains) is assumable when trying to
delineate real changes between images is the limiting factor that should decide which model to use and if the
models require further refinement (data points). In the context of this work, only simple scaling adjustments
are considered, after a proper characterization of the study areas and argumentation on the extrapolability
of their information.The adjustment methodology described above is exemplified in the following layout,
which represents a Magellan model (taken from the ones in section 5.5) and an equivalent fictional VISAR
or VENSAR one by extrapolation from reference points.
Figure 5.21: Example of the adjustment methodology presented above, which tunes the Magellan-derived
empirical models to approximations of VISAR/VENSAR images.

==PAGE:98==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
5.6.3
VISAR model: TerraSAR-X and SRTM scenarios
As introduced in chapter 2, the EO satellite missions carrying X-band SARs, SRTM and TerraSAR-X, are
probably the best-suited and available missions to extract information to generate the VISAR model. This
is, mainly, because they have similar radar wavelengths (3.1 and 0.31 cm respectively, while VISAR will have
3.8 cm), because they have acquisitions geometries that are equivalent to those of Magellan in a wide array
of locations/latitudes, and because within their products’ coverage there are a number of locations that are
expected to be physically similar to those found on Venus.
A priori, SRTM is a great match for VISAR due to same wavelength, equivalent incidence angles and similar
orbits (see section 4.4). Due to the reduced coverage of the instrument, only one pass, partially covering the
Hawaii island, was used as a study area. The scene, shown in the following figure 5.22, includes the east
and part of the south flank of the Mauna Loa volcano, which are covered by a population of lava flows with
different morphologies.
Figure 5.22: SRTM scene of the island of Hawai’i, Hawaii. The arrow to the left indicates the sensor’s flight
direction; the look geometry is left-looking. The scene is shown in a multi-looked (16 x 4) intensity image
(left) and a processed (48 x 12 multi-looked, scaled and clipped) amplitude image (right).
The rectangular area in red in figure 5.22 has been identified as a suitable study area due to the presence
of slopping lava flows without vegetation cover on one hand, and due to the precise knowledge of their
composition, morphology and location on the other.
Even though SRTM’s spatial resolution is coarse,
particularly after multi-looking, radar-bright Aa flows are still identifiable in this area. By means of visual
comparison of distinguishable lava flow terrains on the scene with volcanographic studies, it is possible
to relate radar signatures to specific types of lava flows morphologies.
This methodology, although not
automatized and spatially accurate, has been deemed sufficient to exemplify the tuning of the models in the
level and timeframe of this MSc thesis work, and it is used also with TerraSAR-X and NovaSAR-1.
Regrettably, even with rough estimates of local incidence angles and with the volcanographic information of
the scene, the finding of reference points to create a VISAR model, as defined in 5.6.2, is not possible with
the current SRTM data of this work, due to the lack of calibration of the intensity images. In other words,
97
==PAGE:99==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
since the σ0 estimates are not representative of σ0, they cannot be directly compared with calibrated results
of Magellan and other missions. Nevertheless, this scene has been used as a starting point to confirm that:
• The Mauna Loa area offers a variety of areas that present volcanic terrains with similar characteristics
to those in the Magellan SAR products.
• Identifiable lava flows can be visually related to vulcanology and volcanostratigraphic studies.
• The level of contrast between different volcanic flows, and between the flows and weathered background
terrains, in the highlighted rectangular area in figure 5.22, is comparable to those found on digitate
lava flows close to volcanic constructs in Venus. In fact, most of the Venusian locations studied in the
Magellan images present even more contrast, and this can be due to the differences in the weathering
cycles, and also in the timeline differences of the placement of the lavas.
On the same island in Hawaii, the processed TerraSAR-X products (in the way described in section 4.5),
which are calibrated well enough to present meaningful and comparable values with Magellan, have been used
this time to extract reference points that allow the creation of a first approximation of the VISAR empirical
model. The selected area corresponds to a portion of the northeast flank of Mauna Loa, from which both
incidence angle information is obtained, and volcanographic studies have been conducted to identify all the
different volcanic terrains. The study area is shown in the following figure 5.23
Figure 5.23: TerraSAR-X scene of the northeast flank of Mauna Loa volcano on the island of Hawai’i, Hawaii.
The arrow to the right indicates the sensor’s flight direction in the product’s pass, which has a left-looking
geometry. The scene is shown in a multi-looked (11 x 11) backscatter σ0 image (left) and its corresponding
dB pair (right). The area is devoid of vegetation, except for small populations of bushes.
This area, chosen for its variety of lava flows, provides a good scenario to create reference points of different
volcanic signatures as if they would be seen by VISAR; to compare them with Magellan similar lava covers
obtained at the same local incidence angles. The identification of each lava flow unit, which allows a pre-
classification and selection of the most suitable flows to be compared with Venusian terrain, can be done
visually, as a first approximation, thanks to volcanostratigraphic studies (see figure 5.24):

==PAGE:100==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 5.24: Layout of the volcanography of the northeast flank of the Mauna Loa volcano. The different
colors delineate the lava flows and other terrains, while the information provided describes each of them ac-
