Taking into account the information provided in figure 5.24, specific patches belonging to different lava mor-
phologies have been selected in the radar scene. Then, analogously as performed when finding data points
for the Magellan empirical model, mean values of σ0 and θ are extracted for each patch. It is important to
mention, nonetheless, that the θ estimates of this analysis are not corrected by the local slopes (for instance
by means of a DEM); i.e. they are estimates of θ0. This area, however, possesses slope angles with an average
of 2-4°, and therefore θ estimates should be fairly close to θ0. The following figure 5.25 shows the designated
patch areas to construct the model reference points.
The results of each of these data points provide information to address 2 different aspects: the first one is the
creation of a preliminary VISAR model by tuning the Magellan model obtained in 5.5, as discussed, but also,
they allow confirming that different lava flows might have significantly different backscatter response with
variation of wavelength; i.e. not all of them appear brighter or darker in the same proportions, compared to
Magellan. This is important because it highlights the need of addressing different terrain covers with all the
spectrum of incidence angles; if not, striking scaling errors might be introduced in the VISAR model, which
propagate into radiometric distortions that might mask out or difficult the identification of real changes.

Magellan σ0 estimates obtained in locations with a priori similar lava composition, and with similar incidence

Watching figure 5.26, it becomes evident that an exhaustive survey of lava types seen at different geome-
tries is required to properly tune the Magellan model and convert it to a meaningful VISAR model. This
conclusion provides mainly from the fact that the scaling values between the different missions’ observations
differ significantly according to the morphology of the lavas; in the case of the first patch, the scattering
response of TerraSAR-X is even below the acquired with Magellan. Different scaling implies that a larger
pool of test data and scenarios is required to obtain meaningful mean behaviors that allow an accurate-
enough adjustment of the Magellan points. Luckily, TerraSAR-X products have coverage of enough suitable
sites to provide a wide arrange of comparison points, although this has not been further addressed in this work.
By means of these new 5 reference σ0 points, a first derivation of the VISAR empirical model can be obtained,
at least between 34.5 and 38.5 θ values, following the methodology exemplified in figure 5.21.
5.6.4
VENSAR model: NovaSAR-1 scenario
Following the same procedure applied successfully to TerraSAR-X, NovaSAR-1 SAR products are a valuable
source of comparison with Magellan data with the aim of simulating, in this case, VENSAR images. Again,
this is because of its ability to map volcanic features with a wavelength of 10 cm (VENSAR is 9.5 cm) and
with similar incidence angles with some Magellan suitable locations.
Due to the limited accessibility to the NovaSAR-1 database (see section 4.6), only one product was selected
that was suitable enough for the comparison: a Stripmap mode SSC SAR image including the Anak Krakatoa
island in Indonesia.
The island offers an array of volcanic covers, with a variety of Aa lava flows, and
underwent a catastrophic terrain reorganization event in 2018 following a deadly eruption. Still, certain
portions of the island present (at least as of 2020, when the product was obtained) interesting volcanic covers
that offer good comparison scenarios with Venusian flows. The NovaSAR-1 scene of the Anak Krakatoa
island, with a patch selected for modelling purposes, can be seen in the following figure 5.27:
Figure 5.27: NovaSAR-1 scene of the Anak Krakatoa island in the Krakatoa archipelago, strait of Sunda,
Indonesia. The arrow to the right indicates the sensor’s flight path direction (left-looking geometry). The
scene is shown in a multi-looked (3 x 3) backscatter σ0 image (left) and its corresponding dB pair (right).
Since the acquisition time was April 25th, 2020, 15 days after an effusive lava-fountain-style eruption, it is
reasonable to assume that during the observation period there were new placements of fresh lavas. The red
rectangular patch designates the patch used to generate a reference point for the VENSAR model.
The choice of this area as the only suitable area to obtain meaningful, accurate estimates of a VENSAR
equivalent observation of a Magellan scene is motivated on one hand by the lack of data, particularly the
lack of processing of topographic data, and on the other, the volcanostratigraphic studies performed in the
101
==PAGE:103==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
area. Due to time constraints, similarly to TerraSAR-X, no DEM was processed that allows the retrieval
of the mean local incidence angle of the patch. The slope on this patch is gentle enough, approximately 8°
according to rough geometry estimates. Furthermore, its orientation, being parallel to the local orbital track
and hence, perpendicular to the incidence angle plane (aspect ϕ = 90°, minimizes the impact of the slope
angle on the incidence angle; i.e. roughly θ = θ0. Therefore, the impact of not knowing the exact slope field
is best neutralized in this area.
On another hand, the island’s volcanostratigraphy, albeit made before the 2018 collapse, offers indications
that this southern flank possesses Aa basaltic lava covers, without the presence of vegetation. The following
layout of the island and its volcanic features shows and describes the area selected:
Figure 5.28: Layout and justification of the patch selected in the NovaSAR-1 product for the generation
of a reference point to adjust the Magellan empirical model. Volcanostratigraphic studies in Ismail et al.
(2010) corroborate the existence of basaltic Aa lava flows in the southeast slopes of the volcano. The lack of
vegetation is evident from the pictures.
After retrieving the mean backscatter σ0 and the corresponding θ angle, comparison with the Magellan lava
flow model exposed in 5.19 shows that the difference is a factor of 3.6797; the σ0 retrieved by NovaSAR-1
and assumed similar to VENSAR is roughly 3.7 times larger than that imaged by Magellan, according to the
model. This is consistent with the increases observed when wavelength decreases as well in the VISAR case,
although the difference is more sharp than those recorded in the TerraSAR-X study.
Only one reference point has been retrieved for VENSAR, which is insufficient to characterize the full model
as the scaling of the Magella model cannot be assumed to be the same for all terrains and all local incidence
angles. Further work in this line is necessary, as described in 8.

5.7
Ratio test statistic using 2 different SAR systems
After co-registration, despeckling, and the adjustment via empirical models of Magellan images to be close
equivalents to either VISAR or VENSAR observations, the next step in the algorithm chain described in
5.5 is to form the ratio intensity image which, following the theory introduced in 3.2.3.2, allows the location
of real changes that might have occurred within the time baseline of the 2 acquisitions. As described in
3.2.3.3, a test statistic might be formulated if the histogram of the obtained ratio image follows a predictable
mathematical shape, usually a probability density function, which in turns allows extracting precise and
quantifiable estimates of which pixels do in fact represent changes. Nevertheless, the general theory pre-
sented, although working well with most incoherent change scenarios performed with a single SAR sensor,
does not take into account the possibility of image comparison with 2 SAR systems: particularly, the fact
that for the ratio image to represent meaningful changes, the spatial resolutions of both systems need to be
fairly equivalent. If that is to be the case between Magellan and the future VISAR and VENSAR images,
since they inherently possess different resolutions before any data post-processing, different number of looks
for each of the images are to be expected; particularly, the number of looks for VISAR and VENSAR are
expected to be significantly higher than those for Magellan.
This difference in the number of looks of the images that are the numerator and the denominator of the
algorithm’s ratio image is not contemplated in the basic theory, and therefore, a new mathematical framework
is created and explored in this section which should, under similar assumptions undertaken also by the
basic theory, predict well the intensity distributions of the VISAR/VENSAR/Magellan scenarios. This new
mathematical framework is extrapolable from the Venusian case to any SAR incoherent change detection
with different number of looks, and it is in fact a generalization of the equal number of looks case described
in 3.2.3.3.
5.7.1
Change detection framework in the Magellan - VISAR/VENSAR case
The mathematical framework exposed in this section, developed throughout points 5.7.2 to 5.7.4, presents a
new probability density function, a new cumulative distribution function, and describes its main character-
istics and behaviors from a mathematical perspective. Thereafter, the probabilistic framework introduced in
sections 3.2.3.3 and 3.2.3.4, including the detection probability, the probability of error, and the thresholding
methodologies, is adapted to the use of the new distributions.
There are a number of points to make with respect to the new change detection mathematical framework,
specially in order to contextualize the different terminology used and the specific problematics that arise
when rationing images with different number of looks. By convention ,the image with the highest number
of looks, expected to be either a VISAR or VENSAR image when available, or a Magellan-derived one by
means of the empirical models presented before, is placed in the numerator always. The numerator image is
given the subscript 2 while the denominator is referred to with a 1.
Always, either in the case of simulated VISAR/VENSAR images or with the actual images, 2 ratio images
and their subsequent histograms are computed: the system-change image, which is the ratio of the Magellan
image and the VISAR/VENSAR model-equivalent image, and the total-change image, which corresponds
to the ratio of the Magellan one with the VISAR/VENSAR image including true change (physical change).
In all scenarios computed before EnVision/VERITAS, the system-change ratio image is a ratio of the same
Magellan scene despeckled 2 times with different number of looks. This is because the empirical model is
used once to generate an equivalent VISAR/VENSAR image as the algorithm’s input, and later on is used
again to equate the Magellan image with it, effectively removing any contribution from the model, at least
comparatively. The total-change ratio image, in these scenarios, corresponds to the same system-change case
except for the manual manipulation of certain areas, which are considered possible physical changes.
On another hand, once VISAR and VENSAR images become available, the total-change ratio image will
103
==PAGE:105==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
correspond to the direct ratio between a Magellan image and a VISAR/VENSAR one (after despeckling
routines), which will include real change if any change has indeed been recorded. The system-change ratio
image will still be computed, according to the algorithm’s methodology, between the Magellan image and
the model-computed VISAR/VENSAR image of the same area (at this point the model is expected to be
greatly perfected thanks to real VISAR/VENSAR observations of scenes without changes).
The ratio image that is included in the algorithm chain corresponds only to the total-change image in any
case, which is the one whose histogram is fitted with the mathematical framework and thresholded in order
to select which pixels belong to the change class and which ones to the no-change one. Nevertheless, the
system-change histogram is also necessary, indirectly, in the practical computation of the results (see section
6.1 in the results chapter).
From this point, the problematics of VISAR/VENSAR and Magellan test statistics, if the mathematical
framework is adequate, relate to the same issues that can be detected overall in test statistic scenarios.
Change and no-change classes require of a minimum population of pixels to be well-fitted/characterized, and
at the same time their mean values need to be sufficiently distant to keep a low level of misclassification
error. Therefore, the total-change ratio image is expected to have at least 2 different and separated distribu-
tions/classes; one of them representing the bulk of the pixels, which do not present change and hence, if the
empirical model would have a perfect response, should be centered around a mean value of 1, and a secondary
one, consisting of the pixels which represent physical change, which can be either displaced to higher or lower
values from the no-change one. The no-change distribution is usually referred to also as main distribution,
since it is expected to possess the majority of the pixels of the ratio image.

Before attempting to calculate any specific probability of the ratio function, nevertheless, it is customary

probability density function. The derived ratio probability density function candidate p(R) = f(x) is defined
in equation 5.9:
f(x) =

To begin with, it should be noted that all the constants and variables that participate in the equation must
be larger than 0 in a real SAR intensity ratio case. The number of looks is always larger than 0 per definition,
105
==PAGE:107==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
and x, that represents the intensity ratio, must be always larger than 0 since the intensity values are positive,
finite values. Since ρ involves the ratio of the mean intensities, which are also finite and positive, modulated
by the number of looks, which are also always positive as stated before, it must be larger than 0 too.
The first important measure to be tested for this function is its continuity. Continuity is proven for all a > 0
when:
1. f(a) is defined
2. limx→a f(x) exists
3. limx→a f(x) = f(a)
This is proven:
f(a) =

(5.15)
Since all the values involved are finite and positive, f(a) exists as a finite, positive value. On the other hand,
the same can be said of the limit of the function when x tends to a:
lim
x→a f(x) =

Since a is a positive, finite value, the limit of a fraction of polynomials tends to a finite number and hence
the limit exists. From this point, it is also clear that the limit of the function when it tends to a value a
is the same value of the function itself evaluated at that point, meaning that the function is continuous, at
least for all positive values of x.
To evaluate the function’s behavior, it is important to define where does the function tend to in the limits
of its values, in this case 0 and ∞. For a probability density function, it is expected that the integral of the
function, i.e. the area under its curve, is finite (convergent) and equal to 1. This firstly implies that the
function’s limits are defined in the extremes. This is corroborated as it follows:

This limit tends to zero because being the numerator and denominator 2 polynomial functions that tend to

This proves that the limits are defined and that the integral is convergent, but this is a necessary but not
sufficient condition to prove that the integral converges to 1; for that the integration prove is required. This
is done afterwards, since it is intrinsically related with the calculation of the detection probability and the
threshold value for admitting change. Nevertheless, another condition for a function to be a probability
density function is required; it has to be always defined non-negative for any value of the random variable
x. This is straightforward to observe given the positive values of all the constants and the nature of the

function: observing equation 5.14, the terms ρL2 and B(L2, L1) are always non-negative (since the beta func-
tion with positive arguments is always positive), and since the numerator xL2−1 as well as the denominator
(1 + ρx)L2+L1 can only be also non-negative terms, it follows that the function as a whole must be positive,
i.e. with values between 0 and ∞.

choice of 2 parameters, the L2 and L1 number of looks, instead of the conventional single parameter L. The

Figure 5.29: Example of the ratio intensity image probability density function candidate, following a gener-

Some very interesting conclusions can be extracted from discussing the parameters that can be inferred from
figure 5.29. The location of the peak of the distribution and its median value are not the same. The median
value can be defined as representing the value of 50 % of area under the curve (which corresponds to the
integral function); the peak and the median are only the same for symmetric distributions, and is not the
case of this one (although it is close to symmetry). In fact, more than 50 % of the area of the function is
located on the right side of the peak value, meaning that the median value is displaced to the right of the
peak. On another hand, it can be observed that the peak of the distribution (grey point C in the figure)
does not coincide with the ratio mean unit (which would correspond to the green dot in the figure). For
generalized beta prime distributions, the mean value µ1 can be defined as [10]:

5.7.3
Statistics of a ratio of SAR intensity images with different number of
looks: numerical verification
To validate the theory described above with a practical case, numerical simulations have been performed.
2 gamma-distributed random variables X1 and X2, simulating possible values of SAR intensity images over
homogeneous areas, have been computed for different cases. N = 104 realizations of 2 stochastic processes
defined with the typical gamma distribution shape and scale parameters have been generated and corrob-
orated to fit gamma probability density functions according to their chosen α and θ values. For the first
numerical case, the variables are defined with the following parameters:
• α1 = L1 = 30
• α2 = L2 = 50
• θ1 = σ1
L1 = 6.67 · 10−4
• θ2 = σ2
L2 = 10−3
The mean values of the variables X1 and X2 are σ1 = 0.02 and σ2 = 0.05 respectively.
The ratio variable X2
X1 has then been computed by dividing the variables, and a histogram has been obtained.
Independently, following the theoretical definition of the ratio probability density function of equation 5.9,
with the values derived from its theoretical framework:

variable fits very well the calculated generalized beta prime distribution.
Figure 5.30: Case 1: Numerical simulation of the value distribution of 2 simulated SAR intensity images,
and their corresponding distributed ratio following the distribution introduced in 5.9.

• α1 = L1 = 30

• θ2 = σ2

In this case, the mean of the variable X2 has been halved, i.e. σ1 = 0.02 and σ2 = 0.025, and this also changes
the ρ parameter to ρ = 1.33. When this is the case, there is significant overlapping between the gamma
distributions, and this translates to a more narrow (higher variance) generalized beta prime distribution
centered closer to 1, since the ratio of the means is closer to 1 in this case. This case is visualized in the
following figure:

After verifying that the probability density function of the ratio variable behaves as expected, the other major
step required to be certain of the application of this function to change detection implies the computation
of the associated cumulative density function, whose value relates to the change detection probability (see
equation 5.51). In this test, a threshold value T for change detection is selected, and thereafter the probability
of the ratio variable to be larger than the threshold can be computed by evaluating the difference between the
cumulative density function at infinity and at the threshold value. This probability, as a percentage, should
correspond to the number of values of the ratio variable that are in fact above the threshold considering the
whole ratio sample.
The analysis has concluded that the detection probability above a certain threshold, calculated using the
formula in equation 5.42, corresponds very accurately to the values in the generated ratio sample of this
numerical verification.
Since the mean of the ratio variable R, with means σ2 = 0.03 (numerator) and
σ1 = 0.025 (denominator), and corresponding number of looks L2 = 50 and L1 = 30, is µ = 1.2414, a
suitable power threshold deviating from this value has been set accordingly in order to provide a suitable
range to analyze the trend. A difference between 2 to 4 dB between images corresponds to a power ratio of
1.4125 to 1.5849, which should correspond to a decreasing trend (detecting less values) as the ratio increases
and deviates from the mean; this implies hence a decreasing detection probability.

Finally, just to corroborate the a priori strange result found in equation 5.27, where the mean of the ratio
distribution was derived, and its value was found to depend only on L1 and not on L2, a mean ratio analysis

has been conducted. In it, for 2 given mean values σ2 = 0.03 and σ1 = 0.025, different values of L1 and L2

The analysis’ results of figure 5.34 demonstrate that the mean of the ratio of 2 gamma-distributed random
variables does in fact follow the predicted result derived in 5.27, i.e. it only depends on the number of looks
of the denominator. If keeping the number of L2 looks constant and computing the number of looks of
the denominator image from 5 to 25, the data and the results from equation 5.27 (model) exhibit a very
good agreement. On another hand, the yellow dotted line corresponds to the same analysis performed while
keeping the denominator number of looks L1 constant and changing L2 in the same way that L1 has been
changed before. As can be seen, the mean value of the data does not deviate from the theoretical result given
by equation 5.27, which does not depend on L2, and that in this case corresponds to µ = σ2

L1−1 = 1.25 .
5.7.4
Statistics of a ratio of SAR intensity images with different number of
looks: probability analysis
In the previous section, the mathematical and probabilistic framework of a ratio of 2 SAR gamma-distributed
intensity images was proposed, theoretically proven and numerically verified. Having a valid expression for
the detection probability of the values of a given ratio image (see equation 5.51), it is paramount to analyze
how the detection capabilities relate to different choices of parameters. In this case, following the theoretical
background of 3.2.3.3, the detection probability is linked with a probability of false alarm, which at the same
time it is set depending on the selected threshold and in this case the independent values of the 2 different
number of looks. To see how these parameters relate to each other with the new distribution results of section
5.7.2, a number of analysis have been performed.
116
==PAGE:118==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
The first one relates to the probability of detection of a certain mean change/ratio γ =
σ2
σ1 .
As stated
in section 3.2.3.3, the well-known theory of ratio intensity images with the same number of looks states
that if the threshold chosen is the same as the mean change, then the probability of detection Pd is 50 %
regardless of the number of looks. Since it is usually required to have a much higher probability of detection,
a threshold below the mean change has to be selected, which implies introducing a probability of false alarm
Pfa. Nevertheless, one interesting first result is that, on the contrary than in the case with equal number of
looks for the 2 images, the probability of detection with a threshold equal to the mean change is not always
50 %, and it varies with the choice of L2 and L1. It has been numerically observed, using equation 5.51, that
the probability rises slightly above 50 % with an increasing number of looks of the numerator image (L2)
with respect of those of the numerator one (L1). And vice versa, when L1 increases with respect of L2 then
the detection probability decreases; the following figure illustrates this behavior:
Figure 5.35: Probability of detection of a certain mean change with varying number of looks.
As the figure shows, there is a departure of Pd from the well-established behavior; as the fraction L2
L1 increases
so does Pd, and the opposite. Nevertheless, for a maximum number of looks of 250 (either L2 or L1), the
deviation accounts for roughly 1 % of the original 0.5 value, and the increasing/decreasing trends appear to
stabilize as the number of looks grows, which implies that the deviation from the 0.5 result is not significant
at practical effects. However, it should be noted that given a numerator image with an expected higher
number of looks (this is the case for VISAR and VENSAR images), the detection probability is already a bit
higher than 0.5. This helps to introduce a lower false alarm rate, when desiring to reach a certain probability
of detection, than it would in the case of 2 images with equal number of looks.
On another hand, it is possible to assess how, for a given threshold T and a mean ratio σ2
σ1 , the detection
probability and the probability of false alarm behave for different combinations of looks L1 and L2. If the
threshold T is set, then Pfa depends only on the number of looks, while Pd depends also on the mean change,
but if that is set then it also depends only on the looks’ combination. In order to show possible results, two-
dimensional computations of Pfa and Pd have been computed, with L1 and L2 as axis, for specific threshold
T example values. The following figure illustrates these results for 2 threshold possibilities:
117
==PAGE:119==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 5.36: Examples of Pd (upper row) and Pfa (lower row) as a function of L1 and L2 for 2 given thresholds
T = 1.4125 and T = 1.5849.
There are several conclusions that can be extracted form this analysis. The first is that both Pd and Pfa
exhibit the same shape, a well-expected fact since they are both solutions of the same equation, but with
opposite behaviour since Pfa decreases as Pd increases. From the shape, 2 more conclusions can be stated:
that the changes in probability are faster when the number of looks are smaller, and that changes in L1 and
L2 induce more or less the same changes of probability. However, if taking a closer look at the figure, it is
possible to see how changes in L2 increase Pd in a sharper way than changes in L1, while on the other hand,
changes in L1 decrease Pfa faster.
The fact that Pfa decreases with increasing Pd can be confusing, because it is not the same as to say that the
higher the probability of detection is, the lower the false alarm; in fact it is rather the opposite. From this
figure, it can only be stated that for a given threshold, there is a peak of number of looks that produces the
maximum detection probability with the minimum false alarm rate, but that threshold is set arbitrarily. If
selecting first an adequate Pfa, as it is customary in change detection, for a given combination of L1 and L2
there is only one threshold T that satisfies its value. That threshold T, together with a mean ratio value σ2
σ1 ,
are the conditions that set Pd for the same number of looks. In fact, consequently, the lower the chosen Pfa,
the higher the threshold, and the lower the detection probability. In other words, the Pd and Pfa of figure
5.36 are not directly comparable because they are the probability results of different ratio means ( σ2
σ1 = 2
and σ2
σ1 = 1); they are valid independent results that do not correlate to one another.
To illustrate that a decreasing Pfa corresponds to a decreasing Pd following the equivalences commented
above, numerical results have been computed. The first one (figure 5.37) shows how a decreasing probability
of false alarm corresponds to an increasing threshold T. Then, inverting equation 5.51, it is possible to
calculate a threshold T from a calculated δ value if L1, L2 and
σ2
σ1 are known. In fact, it is possible to
118
==PAGE:120==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
calculate first the T value associated with a desired Pfa and then substitute it in equation 5.51 to get the
corresponding Pd. This operation is computationally difficult and costly, as it requires an inversion of an
equation whose variable to isolate (δ) is inside a series’ summation involving very large polynomials (L1 −1
factors), which require precision up to 30 decimals.
Nevertheless, if calculating this whole process for a
number of decreasing Pfa values, it is possible to see how the associated Pd values also decrease (see figure
5.38).
Figure 5.37: Threshold increase corresponding to a decrease in the probability of false alarm rate.
As
explained, for a given combination of number of looks L1 and L2, a specific false alarm rate corresponds
to a choice of threshold value T. It is important to notice that, for a given mean change σ2
σ1 to be detected
given L2 and L1, there can be low values of probability of false alarm that are unachievable, since their
associated threshold values can be equal or higher than the mean change ratio (which yields unwanted
detection probabilities equal or below 0.5).
The probability of false alarm of figure 5.37 has been giving as the log10 of the real value for visualization
purposes. The highest value corresponds to Pfa = 0.5 while the lowest is Pfa = 10−5; the spacing between
samples is of half an order of magnitude. The fact that the logarithmic function linearizes the false alarm rates
indicates that the increase in threshold value is significantly slower than their decrease; this is a beneficial
behavior from the change detection perspective because it indicates that there is significant margin to reduce
the probability of false alarm to acceptable levels (usually around 1 %) without augmenting the threshold
values to the point that the detection probability is not desirable (below 95 % in most cases).
119
==PAGE:121==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 5.38: Detection probability evolution associated with the probability of false alarm that yields its
threshold values T (see figure 5.37). As anticipated, the probability of detection decreases with the probability
of false alarm, but favorably, the decrease in false alarm is much sharper, which allows achieving low false
alarm rates that still relate to high detection probabilities.
The conclusion of this analysis is that the new probability distribution for SAR intensity images with different
number of looks allows for high change detection probabilities with low false alarm rates, very much in line
with those that can be found in the classical change detection theory described in 3.2.3.3. In order not to
invert equation 5.51, which amounts to inassumable computation times and errors for the number of looks
expected for the real case of this thesis, the practical way to proceed is:
1. For a given image mean change σ2
σ1 , L1 and L2, compute a threshold value which relates to a certain
probability of false alarm.
2. Repeat this process for chosen combinations of number of looks that are in line with the possible looks
in the Magellan, VENSAR and VISAR images, until a desired probability of false alarm is identified.
3. Compute the threshold-associated detection probability and select the combination that ensures the
maximum detection with the minimum false alarm rate.
The approach of setting an acceptable probability of false alarm first, for then computing the associated
threshold value and the detection probability above this threshold is the one a robust change detection
strategy aims for. Nevertheless, as described in section 3.2.3.2, the choice of an optimal threshold is usually
a limiting factor in itself. In the previous results, detection and false alarm probabilities have been analyzed
under the assumption that there are no criteria to select a threshold for change. In fact, given the conditions
of 2 co-registered SAR intensity images, and if the objective is to identify changes and classify pixels as
either ”change” or ”no-change”, at least 2 corresponding classes need to be identified. A threshold ratio
value T needs to be selected then that classifies the pixels’ values and assigns them to one class or the other
(see section 3.2.3.4). In the particular case of this thesis, the simplest approach is to consider one class,
identified as no-change class, with a mean ratio value of ≈1. This situation would work only in the case
120
==PAGE:122==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
that the empirical model transforming Magellan images to VISAR/VENSAR images has a theoretical perfect
performance (see section 5.6.2), but nevertheless the value associated with the mean ratio value of this class
would be the outcome of this model and relatively close to unity. On the other hand, the ”change” class,
also called true-change class, would be associated with a secondary distribution with a mean ratio value
displaced from 1. This class would represent the number of pixels that have changed when performing the
ratio between the empirically-tuned Magellan image and a hypothetical VISAR/VENSAR image containing
true change. For the global picture regarding the classification problematics related with the thresholding
strategy refer back to section 5.7.1.
This simple scenario considering 2 classes can be used to check, and if necessary reformulate, the 2 theoretical
approaches to threshold selection exposed in section 3.2.3.4 (Minimum error thresholding and Otsu method),
but this time considering SAR intensity images with different number of looks. In the case of the minimum
error thresholding, the assumed a priori probabilities of the 2 classes are equal, and hence if considering class
distributions p(T; ρnc, L2, L1) and p(T; ρc, L2, L1), where nc stands for no-change and c stands for change,
equation 3.27 can be developed as:

The following figure shows how this threshold selection method would work for separating 2 classes with
mean values rnc = 1 and rc = 1.2 with L2 = 200 and L1 = 50. Evidently, in line with the theory exposed in
section 3.2.3.4, the closer the mean ratio values of each class, the more classification errors occur according to
equations 3.19 and 3.20. However, the results here in figure 5.39 evidence that playing with different number
of looks for the numerator and denominator images does not influence the threshold significantly enough to
shift the error probability; both constant x-lines are almost overlapping. This indicates that different number
of looks are not a decisive factor in the threshold selection per se, and that a significant distance between
class ratio mean values is the most important metric to obtain low classification errors.
Figure 5.39: Example of how a minimum error threshold should be set in a L2 ̸= L1 ratio analysis in which the

by the ratio unity. Notice how the threshold for equal number of looks (red line) has to be zoomed-out since
it is almost overlapping with the value acquired with equation 5.62.
On another hand, the Otsu method does not require any tuning from its general definition (see section 3.2.3.4)
when it comes to selecting a threshold between 2 classes defined by 2 generalized beta prime distributions,
but it does need the code computation of the minimum error threshold (with the new version explained above
for this case) to construct the bimodal histogram/distribution upon which it is used, at least in the current
version of the code implementation.
In any case, the thresholding strategy selected, including the specifically modified parts when L2 ̸= L1, fol-

the discussion presented in the results chapter (see 6).

The methodology of the different steps/tasks that are shown in the algorithm chain of figure 5.5 finishes here.
The next chapter, results, provides the analysis on the performance of the complete chain from inputs to
output, and it also describes the particularities of the code operations that allow the correct functioning of
the test statistic process.
123
==PAGE:125==
Results and discussion
This chapter, as explained in 2.5, is intended to provide the final results of the algorithm chain, after suc-
cessfully applying all the steps presented, analyzed and refined in the methodology. The results offer an
end-to-end view of the efficacy and effectiveness of the change detection strategy to the best level achieved
by each of the tasks of chapter 5.
The first point of the chapter addresses the particularities of the algorithm when applied to the set of sim-
ulated cases that are described and analyzed in the following sections. It briefly summarizes the different
remarks and specific checks that are required to apply the algorithm from an operational and coding point
of view.
Following these practical clarifications, 3 different result groups are presented. The first one addresses lo-
cations where volcanic activity has been indirectly inferred through years of analyzing past data; these are
geologically interesting locations that are well positioned as change detection targets, but do not necessarily
pose the best comparison scenario for the future VISAR/VENSAR observations with the current level of
their model adjustment. Only specific locations, which present observation geometries that are equivalent to
those used as reference points in the TerraSAR-X/NovaSAR-1 studies, are currently well-suited for simulating
VISAR/VENSAR cases. Nevertheless, this is just due to the current lack of model tuning for the complete
array of θ angles under different terrain types, and in fact, this does not directly affect purely simulated
studies as the impact of the model is cancelled out in the algorithm chain (as mentioned in section 5.2.2.2,
but also see next point 6.1). 2 of these well-suited scenes have been used to perform end-to-end change
detection scenarios with simulated lava flows; one corresponds to a VISAR-Magellan case and the other to a
VESAR-Magellan one.
6.1
Practical considerations
The generation and performance of the end-to-end change detection algorithm chain from a practical per-
spective follow a series of remarks that are described here.
First, the 2 input images need to be generated. After selecting the scene and obtaining the Magellan σ0 image
(following the procedure in 4.1.6), the same image is scaled, by using either the VISAR or the VENSAR
model value for the mean θ angle of the scene, and so converted into an equivalent VISAR/VENSAR image.
This image is manually modified by locally introducing a design/pattern of a new lava flow, taking into
account the radiometric and topographic context of the image. The new volcanic feature does not alter the
speckle pattern of the image, and it is introduced by locally raising or reducing the mean σ0 value of the image.
Since no co-registration is required, next both images are reconstructed by means of a modified Lee filter
with different number of looks (window sizes); the simulated VISAR/VENSAR image has always higher ENL
after filtering. Thereafter, the Magellan image is converted to a VISAR/VENSAR equivalent by means of
the same scaling transformation used to generate the VISAR/VENSAR image; therefore, the effects of both
scaling operations cancel out. This is only reasonable as long as working in simulated scenarios; the model
124
==PAGE:126==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
can be perfected with more EO data, but until real VISAR/VENSAR images are obtained, the chances to
experimentally quantify its performance are scant, and also the ability to construct the models themselves is
obstructed.
Once the despeckled images are obtained, 2 ratio images are formed with the higher number of looks im-
age as numerator; one is the system-change ratio image and the other is the real, total-change ratio image.
The system-change ratio image is constructed by dividing the Magellan despeckled image, scaled with the
VISAR/VENSAR model, with the original Magellan image (after despeckling). This image is an operational
step necessary for the calculations: its histogram is formed, and its mean value retrieved (this histogram can
be fitted with the distribution defined in 5.7, but it is not necessary at this point). This mean value provides
information of the model scaling operation and the several radiometric distortions introduced to the ratio
operation, mainly from the model itself but also from the despeckling routines. Also, this ratio image can
be always calculated, regardless if it is a VISAR/VENSAR simulation or a real case, and the mean value
obtained sets a reference value for possible changes that might be retrieved from the total-change ratio. The
reference value that is used later on is in fact the ratio between the model scaling factor and this ratio mean
obtained from the histogram; if the model has a good performance and radiometric distortions are minimal,
it should be very close to 1.
On another hand, the total change image is the ratio of the despeckled VISAR/VENSAR original (or simu-
lated) image and the Magellan one. Ideally it contents a real change (in this case is a simulated change, as
explained). Analogously as with the system-change image, its histogram is obtained. With knowledge of the
ENL of each of the images, an inverse beta prime PDF constructed with the appropriate images’ parameters
is fitted to it. In the situation that there are indeed physical changes, a secondary distribution appears in
the histogram; the critical coding process of the algorithm at this point is, in fact, to identify the 2 peaks
of the distributions of the histogram. If the change is small compared to the total number of pixels in the
scene, as it could be usually the case considering the fact that there is not a preselected scene size, then the
histogram presents a bimodal behavior; one distribution represents the pixels that do not change, which is
the largest of the 2 and is referred to as the no-change class, and the other represents the small portion that
does change (the class change distribution).
The occurrence of localized changes necessarily shifts the mean of the no-change distribution to a higher or
lower value to that of the system-change mean calculated before. This allows to uniquely identify the position
of the peak of the no-change class, and knowing the number of looks, equation 5.23 allows to extract the
necessary information to fit the PDF to the histogram values. Once this is computed, the secondary peak
can be identified knowing the direction of its displacement relative to the no-change class mean and as long
as the class has enough pixels as to exhibit a proper ”peaky” behavior (that allows the fitting of a PDF).
Moreover, a minimum ratio mean difference is necessary between classes, otherwise the peak of the change
class distribution might not be discernible. In this sense, if the 2 peaks are identifiable and the distributions
can be fit, there are 4 possible scenarios that are contemplated in the algorithm’s implementation, as long
as the scene is tailored enough as to have only one type of physical change (an advancing or new lava
flow appearing in the scene, for instance): figure 6.1 provides a graphical overview of these combinations of
possibilities between the no-change and the change pixel classes.
125
==PAGE:127==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 6.1: The 4 possible histogram scenarios from the total-change ratio image, in the case of 1 type of
physical change. The fitting of the change class can only be done if it has a sufficient number of pixels in one
hand, and if its mean is displaced enough from the mean of the no-change class on the other.
After fitting the 2 classes with their corresponding distributions, if possible, then the thresholding method-
ology described and analyzed in 5.7.4 is applied. The 2 thresholds are computed by the code and, provided
that one of them or both comply with the desired probability of false alarm (which is chosen as an input of
the algorithm, see figure 5.5), the one with the best detection performance is selected.
Finally, a point should be made with respect to the code design of the mathematical expressions used to
calculate probabilities such as Pd or Pe, particularly, of the adaptability of equation 5.51. Mathematical
precision to a very large number of significant figures for the numbers involved is necessary, particularly
when calculating quotients of Pochhammer and factorial expressions; the ratio of very large or very small
figures, if not precise to a very large number figure, has been observed to introduce unsustainable biases
to the probability estimates. One way to circumvent these circumstances is to address the problem with
numerical libraries and functions, and convert the expression to a double equivalent value only at the end of
the computation. However, this is not enough in some cases, and special care is needed to identify when and
how to transform each specific step of the process. Furthermore, very large number of looks yield infinite
results of the gamma function, which blocks any successful probability estimation. This has been partially
addressed by means of the logarithmic form of the gamma function, but if the number of looks is too large, the
probability cannot be calculated (with the current version of the algorithm implementation). Thankfully, the
majority of the scenarios analyzed do not require an impossible number of looks to generate the appropriate
ratio PDFs.
6.2
Test scenarios of Venusian hotspots
In section 5.1, 3 locations are highlighted as potential change detection best scenarios given their well-defined
lava flows and/or their theorized volcanic activity. As these locations are highly interesting from the scientific
point of view, a simulated scenario has been constructed for each of them according to the available Magellan
products. They represent possible change detection situations due to effusive volcanic eruptions, but they
are also used to test the algorithm and identify the reachable performance values in terms of detection and
error probabilities. The full results are presented and discussed in the following points.
126
==PAGE:128==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
6.2.1
Sif Mons scenario
Sif Mons (25° N 9° W) presents a series of interesting digitate flows, very distinguishable from the radar-dark
volcanic plains where they are placed in the slopes of the volcano. Because of that, it represents one of
the most approachable test scenarios where to apply the change detection algorithm. For this purpose, the
selected Sif Mons scene corresponds to the upper-left quadrant (400 x 400 pixels) of the framelet number
33 (1024 x 1024 pixels) of the Magellan F-MIDR product f25n351. A uniform simulated lava flow has been
added as a prolongation/advancement of the existing flow in the scene, with a mean σ0 value significantly
lower to that of the original flow (a scale value of 1.6 is used while the original contrast is ≈4), in order to
strain the algorithm’s performance. The following figure 6.2 exemplifies the results of the different steps of
the change detection algorithmic chain, from the original images to obtaining a change binary map.
Figure 6.2: Sif Mons test scenario results. The upper-left image represents the original Magellan image and
the upper-right a simulated equivalent VISAR image including a lava flow extension. The 2 scenes in the
middle row of the figure show their corresponding despeckled pairs; notice how the spatial resolution is more
degraded in the VISAR image compared to the Magellan because of the larger averaging applied. When
VISAR/VENSAR images are available, the spatial resolution should be equivalent after despeckling. Finally,
the lower-left image corresponds to the obtained total-change ratio image, after adjusting the Magellan image
with the VISAR model to match the VISAR input (ideal behavior with mean 1). After constructing the
histogram, the change binary map seen in the lower-right image is obtained by applying the Otsu thresholding
strategy.
The total number of looks used to simulate this example are L2 = 230 for VISAR and L1 = 180 for
Magellan. Nevertheless, the calculated ENL for each of the images is far lower, mainly due to texture and
pixel correlation. It has been observed that a higher number of looks, compared to the ENL and even to the
127
==PAGE:129==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
total number of looks applied, are necessary for the ratio PDF to fit the histograms. The fit is accurate for
most of the span of ratio values. The map of figure 6.2 is obtained with a change detection probability of
Pd = 0.9945 and a subsequent classification error probability of 0.0317. Since an acceptable probability of
false alarm is set to 5 % (as input), these results would be considered very successful.
6.2.2
Idunn Mons scenario
This scenario is practically identical to the one presented in the previous point, with the exception that
instead of having a very discernible lava flow morphology and a clear background, the scene is much more
complex, with a complicated network of different flows and terrains. In addition, the northeast digitate lava
flow field of Idunn Mons (from where this scene has been selected) is considered a likeable eruptive area [85],
and therefore this test scenario is more scientifically interesting, as well as more challenging from the change
detection perspective.
Figure 6.3: Idunn Mons test scenario results. The upper-left image represents the original Magellan image
and the upper-right a simulated equivalent VENSAR image including 2 new lava flow shapes. The 2 scenes
in the middle row of the figure show their corresponding despeckled pairs, as in figure 6.2.
Finally, the
lower-left image corresponds to the obtained total-change ratio image, after adjusting the Magellan image
with the VENSAR model to match the VENSAR input (ideal behavior with mean 1). After constructing the
histogram, the change binary map seen in the lower-right image is obtained by applying the Otsu thresholding
strategy.
The exact scene corresponds to the lower-left quadrant (400 x 400 pixels) of the framelet number 43 of the
128
==PAGE:130==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Magellan F-MIDR product f45s218. To process it, a L1 = 200 number of looks are globally applied to the
Magellan image, while the corresponding simulated image (a VENSAR model scaling in this case) has a
L2 = 250 number of looks. However, the same behavior identified in 6.2.1 is found again in this case: to
obtain a good fitting, the number of looks L2 and L1 necessary to construct the ratio PDFs are much higher
than the calculated ENL and slightly higher than the total number of looks applied.
The binary map obtained in figure 6.3 has been obtained with a Pd of changed pixels of Pd = 0.9801 and a
class misclassification error probability of Pe = 0.0199 (better than in the Sif Mons case). The 2 lava flows
have been constructed with a radiometric factor conversion of 0.6 times the previous cover values (lower
means), which is within the limits of the radiometric contrasts between flows observed in the area. These
results show the ability of the algorithm to identify changes with great precision, even when they are not
easy to be detected visually.
6.2.3
Maat and Ozza Mons scenario
The third test scenario corresponds to a VISAR simulation of a large but faint lava flow slopping down the
north side of Maat Mons. This is also an area, close to the Ganiki chasma rift zone, where eruptive activity is
expected [104]. The main difference between this analysis and the 2 previous ones is the usage of a Magellan
SAR compressed product (C1) instead of a full resolution one. Specifically, the scene corresponds to the full
framelet composition (56 framelets) of the C1-MIDR product c100n197.
Figure 6.4: Maat and Ozza Mons test scenario results. The upper row images represent the Magellan and
VISAR (simulated) scene. The 2 middle row scenes show the correspondent despeckled pairs, while the low
row shows the total-change ratio image and the corresponding change binary map obtained.
129
==PAGE:131==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
As in the 2 previous tests, this time the performance is also very high, achieving high detection probabilities
and very low error probabilities. Since the C1 compressed products are processed with an additional 3x3
boxcar filter to reduce speckle, modified Lee filters of sizes 3x3 (Magellan) and 9x9 (VISAR) are used to
obtain total number of looks of L1 ≈200 and L2 ≈700. However, the number of looks L2 and L1 used to
construct the ratio PDFs to fit the total-change histogram are actually lower than these values, as these are
too elevated and the computation becomes unresponsive. However, the actual values used are still signifi-
cantly higher than the associated ENL to each of the images, which according to the theoretical framework
should be the ones determining the optimal PDFs.
In this case, results from both the minimum threshold methodology and the Otsu technique have been
regarded as equivalently good results. This is because even if the Otsu method achieves lower error prob-
abilities (a negligible Pe = 0.0030, while for the minimum error method it is Pe = 0.02), the detection
probability of changed pixels is Pd = 0.9972, which is slightly lower than the one for the minimum error
method (Pd = 0.9984). In any case, both results are very similar and successful in terms of detecting change,
with almost 0 error probabilities. This is in part possible due to the ratio mean difference between classes,
which is a consequence of the 1.8 factor used to create the simulated lava flow. Nevertheless, such contrast is
actually smaller than the contrast seen between the lava fields of the area and most surrounding backgrounds,
indicating that for most of the expected possible eruption scenarios, detection of new lava flows in the area
should be very accurate with the designed algorithm.
The next point addresses a suitable VISAR-Magellan change detection in a suitable area from the current
level of the model point of view. The cases of this section were, to a certain point, algorithm demonstration
tests; the following results present a deeper analysis of the results of each of the steps, which fully characterize
the algorithm expected performance in real comparison cases.
6.3
VISAR-Magellan change detection
The TerraSAR-X analysis of section 5.6.3 used a total of 5 reference points, extracted from 5 different patches
over a lava-covered area in the Mauna Loa volcano, to partially adjust the Magellan empirical model derived
in 5.5.
Scenes of future VISAR products with the characteristics and the geometry of these points are
therefore the most suitable to elaborate a simulated change detection case, given the current model. The 5
TerraSAR-X patches had the following mean values of θ (θ0):
θT X−points = {34.6638°, 36.1169°, 37.8667°, 38.3681°, 37.819°}
(6.1)
VISAR is expected to have a fixed incidence angle of 32°. In that sense, it is more restricted than VENSAR
to construct geometrically suitable scenarios. However, with the appropriate topography on the ground, local
incidence angles of similar values to the ones of the TerraSAR-X patches are possible. The most appropriate
scene found for a change detection with VISAR is that the achieves an ≈31 −32° of local incidence angle
and depicts a volcanic terrain with the presence of Aa/Pahohoe lava flows (such as the TerraSAR-X scene).
After a Magellan product survey, a region of interest was identified in the Nyx Mons & Nefertiti Corona
area; particularly the array of lava flows slopping down in the north flank of the corona (36° N 48° E). The
corresponding product tag is the framelet number 48 of the F-MIDR f40n046. The Magellan area, with its
corresponding local incidence angle mask computed following the method in 5.3 is shown in the next figure:
130
==PAGE:132==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 6.5: SAR calibrated σ0 dB image (left) and the corresponding local incidence angle θ mask (right).
The values of the Magellan mask correspond quite accurately to those found in the TerraSAR-X patches,
making this a good scenario for the model to predict a future VISAR counterpart scene.
After constructing the Magellan angle mask, using the VISAR orbital and observational parameters, the same
mask can be calculated for the new radar. VENSAR mask results have also been calculated for comparison
purposes; the 3 masks are visualized together in the following figure 6.6:
Figure 6.6: Local incidence angle masks of the 3 SAR systems: Magellan (left), VISAR (middle) and VEN-
SAR(right). Since the VISAR empirical model is not well-developed for all θ values, finding a location that
includes the right lava flow terrains and a perfect match between VISAR and Magellan incidence angles is
difficult, as the array of usable/accurate angles is very reduced to a small window of values. A difference
of ≈6 degrees, for an area of roughness-dominated scattering, has been accepted as a good approximation
within the current accuracy and errors of the VISAR empirical model. The same applies to the VENSAR
comparison, but this is addressed in 6.4.
The calculation of the θ mask and the consequent scaling operation with the model is necessary in the future
VISAR-Magellan real comparison cases, and hence it is a critical step to be defined. Nevertheless, with the
current methodology, it does not produce an impact on the ability of the algorithm to detect changes, as it
is used also to create the input VISAR image and therefore its effects are balanced out. The scaling used in
this case is that of the reference point 3 (patch 3) because of its more accurate match with the Magellan θ
mask mean value. The σ0 of TerraSAR for this point is 0.1856, while the Magellan equivalent is 0.1364; the
scaling factor used is 1.3698.

==PAGE:133==

DTU Space
After the input creation and the mask analysis, the despeckling operations are performed, followed by the gen-
eration of the ratio histograms and the fitting with appropriate ratio PDFs. The following table summarizes
the information on the different number of looks that are obtained/considered for the analysis:

Table 6.1: Table of VISAR-Magellan change detection number of looks.
The cells with No. of looks refer to the total number of looks/averagings that are applied or expected to be
applied (VISAR). The first 2 numbers in the 3 factor multiplication of the cells correspond to the window
size of the modified Lee filter used when despeckling. The VISAR filter size is larger, as mentioned, and its
size is chosen as a compromise to balance the resolution of both SAR systems while providing as well good
levels of speckle reduction. The third value in both cases corresponds to the level of azimuth/angular looks
that have already been applied to the data, or are expected to. Approximately 9 looks are applied in the
Magellan case according to the scene’s latitude, and if taking the theoretical middle resolution image product
of VISAR (20 % of the surface) [129], 15 looks will be applied.
However, the calculated ENL values of a homogeneous portion of the scenes after despeckling show much
lower values. As introduced before, this is largely due to pixel correlation on one hand, and texture on the
other. What is really important, nonetheless, is that there exist compromise values of L2 and L1 that can
be used to construct the ratio PDFs and that are seen to accurately fit the ratio images’ histograms. This
is generally the case; for instance, in this case the table values of L2 and L1 provide good estimations of
the PDFs, and furthermore, these values are still easily manageable from the computational point of view,
as they can be coded with the techniques described in 6.1. This has been done visually in this case, but
any metric that globally minimizes the distance between the histogram values and their corresponding PDF
estimates is a good choice to automatically obtain L2 and L1. This works as long as the PDF overall shape
accurately describes the histogram, but some differences have been observed, particularly in the trailing edge
of the distributions (see figure 6.7). This is a typical indicator of radar texture, and therefore, the most
suitable scenario would be to expand the mathematical formulation described in 5.7; if this is done, more
accurate PDF-histogram matches could be obtained.
Using the L2 and L1 values of table 6.1, a system-change image histogram has been obtained and fitted, and
the mean value retrieved (in the ideal model response contemplated in this case, the value is very close to 1
and only accounts for the minor radiometric distortions introduced by the despeckling operation). Parallelly,
a lava flow change with a mean factor of 1.8 has been introduced in the VISAR image (before despeckling),
and the total-change ratio image histogram retrieved. As expected and described in 6.1, 2 classes appear,
a main one with the majority of unchanged pixels (no-change class) and a secondary one encompassing the
pixels that belong to the new lava flow (change class). 2 different threshold values are obtained with the
modified minimum threshold operation described in 5.7.4 and the Otsu method. The following figure 6.7
shows the histograms, the fitted PDFs, and the 2 threshold values:
132
==PAGE:134==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
Figure 6.7: VISAR-Magellan total-change ratio image scenario.
The histogram shows a bimodal shape
consistent with the prototypical likely scenarios identified in 6.1. With an adequate choice of L2 and L1,
2 generalized beta prime distributions have been fitted to the no-change class (in blue) and to the change
class (true-change, in red).
As mentioned, the fitting is generally good, with small discrepancies in the
trailing edge, visible in the no-change case. The 2 threshold values obtained are also visible, depending on
the method: in this case they are very similar, and hence they yield almost equivalent probability results.
A loose constraint of a 5 % error probability (Pe = 0.05) was assumed as input. Nevertheless, an error
probability below Pe = 0.015 would be considered as a great success. On the other hand, the pixels detected
as changed pixels should have a probability of detection above 95% for a good performance; Pd = 0.99 for
an outstanding performance. After thresholding, the probabilities achieved are:
• Modified minimum error method: Pd = 0.9791 and Pe = 0.0230.
• Otsu method: Pd = 0.9928 and Pe = 0.0144.
Both results are successful as they are within the acceptable range of probabilities, but the minimum error
results provide a good performance while the Otsu methodology provides an outstanding precision in the
results. In this case, since the use of Otsu does not introduce a significant difference in computation time
and complexity, the Otsu threshold is selected. This can be challenged in more complex cases that can be
envisioned in the future, for instance including different types of change classes and hence, multiple thresh-
olds; in such cases, the use of simpler methods as the minimum threshold method might be preferred.
The final summary of the results is given in figure 6.8 as a mosaic of the different images corresponding to
different steps of the algorithm, from inputs to the obtained change detection binary map, in line with the
ones presented in section 6.2.

==PAGE:135==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering

Figure 6.8: Results of the VISAR-Magellan comparison scenario of the Nyx Mons and Nefertiti Corona area.
The upper row images represent the Magellan and VISAR (simulated) scenes. The 2 mid-row scenes show the
correspondent despeckled pairs, while the low row shows the total-change ratio image and the corresponding
change binary map obtained. The binary map obtained corresponds almost exactly with the simulated new

The conclusion from these results is that a successful detection of changes that consist of single new or
advancing lava flows with similar characteristics as the ones observed throughout the different Magellan SAR
products is possible and likely, between VISAR and Magellan. This success, however, is constrained by the
precision of the different algorithm steps, particularly by the accuracy of the VISAR empirical model, by the
level and disposition of radiometric distortion of the despeckling routine, and by the fitting performance of
the mathematical and probabilistic frameworks. The next section provides a similar test scenario analysis,
but this time with a VENSAR-Magellan case.
6.4
VENSAR-Magellan change detection
Another possible combination that exploits changes within a long acquisition time baseline is the comparison
of Magellan with future VENSAR imagery. In section 5.6.4, a reference point for the VENSAR empirical
model was obtained from a suitable scene in a NovaSAR-1 product of the Anak Krakatoa volcanic island. The
average local incidence angle of the NovaSAR-1 scene was calculated to be 26.4261°, with a corresponding
average backscatter of 0.2734 coming from an area covered by Aa lava flows. The closest match with the
same average angle and physical characteristics was identified in the Magellan records as an F-MIDR product
134

MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering

in the Tethus Regio, a digitate lava field to the northeast of Melia Mons (66.0° N 120.0° E); specifically, the
scene of framelet 29 (1024 x 1024 pixels) of the F-MIDR product f65n126. With further investigation of
the SAR products, more suitable locations are likely to be identified, particularly one with a more suitable
VENSAR-equivalent geometry; nevertheless, this scenario has been deemed good enough for a test case. The
scene, together with the calculated Magellan local incidence angle mask θ, is show in the following figure:
Figure 6.9: SAR calibrated σ0 dB image (left) and the corresponding local incidence angle θ mask (right).
The mean values of the Magellan θ angle in the scene correspond quite accurately to those retrieved in the
NovaSAR-1 patch, making this a good scenario for the model to predict a future VENSAR observation.
The mean σ0 value retrieved from Magellan with this geometry, which is the same that in the reference point
of the VENSAR model, is σ0 = 0.0743. Consequently, the scaling factor used to produce the input VENSAR
image is 3.6797 (the VENSAR image is significantly more bright than the Magellan one, see section 5.6.4).
The VENSAR local incidence mask, which is of not use if the model is not perfected, but that can be used to

Figure 6.10: Local incidence angle masks of the 3 SAR systems: Magellan (left), VISAR (middle) and

As introduced in the equivalent figure 6.6, it is very unlikely that both Magellan and

is perfected with enough reference points across all the possible θ angle spectrum, it should be able to predict
any σ0 regardless of the geometry equivalence (or not) with Magellan.

These values are not as good as the ones obtained in the VISAR scenario. The minimum error threshold,
which works assuming equal probabilities a priori
between the distributions and scaling the value by their
pixel population size (see 3.2.3.4), is clearly too displaced to the right of the minimum area intersection, prob-
ably due to the sensibility of the scaling when the 2 number of pixels of the different classes differ significantly.
This induces a generally insufficient detection probability and a too large fraction of pixel misclassification.
The Otsu method, on the other hand, works as suposed to and achieves an outstanding performance above
the 99 % of detection probbaility; however, the error probability is higher than the desired maximum of 5 %
decided as input of the algorithm. This scenario is mainly due to 2 factors; one, that the change class posi-
tion and shape is at the limit for the fitting operation and the consequent thresholding to work properly (see
section 6.5), and two, that the designated thresholding methodology is not accurate enough for the scene’s
characteristics. In this eventuality, the proposed outcome is that the change detection can be flagged as
belonging to a different quality level, or else try again with different despeckling routines and cross-check for
errors, if any. On another hand, the high error probability obtained is a direct consequence of the unmodelled
thickness of the change class trailing edge; therefore, a much better performance could be achieved if this
behavior, mainly a result of texture according to the author, is properly included in the PDF derived in 5.7.
Even if the error probability is above the usually accepted limit, the visual results appear to show great
correlation between the retrieved change and the ground truth of the simulated lava flows, meaning that the
results are probably good enough for lava flow area quantification, and hence, for estimations of effusive flow
rates (see 3.1.3.2 and 6.6). Therefore, the VENSAR-Magellan test scenario demonstrates a successful change
detection operation, as long as in a real case the empirical model exhibits a high prediction performance.

to the scene; even if the error probability of the Otsu thresholding method used is above 5 %, this does not
appear to have a significant impact besides maybe a slightly smoothing of the edges and a small population
of lonely misclassification errors. Moreover, notice how even the tiny change in the left of the image is well
detected.
