6.5
Performance assessment and error analysis
The algorithm described in detail in chapter 5 and used to generate the results discussed in the previous
sections achieves a very high level of change detection performance. Nevertheless, it also accumulates errors,
and most of them, related to the accuracy of the VISAR and VENSAR empirical models, have not been
directly observed and their consequent impact has not been assessed. Therefore, such high performance is
expected to be degraded when dealing with real VENSAR/VISAR images, as the model, even if enlarged
and perfected, won’t be able to perfectly predict σ0 values for all geometries and all terrain types.
Also, on another hand, even if the accumulation of errors is low, the fitting operation used for the test statistic
of the ratio images has limitations related with the relative shape and position of the different classes. This
has been previously introduced in section 6.1. These limitations are:
• Class pixel number: a minimum pixel population is required for each of the classes, as the histogram
needs to be representative enough for the distribution to be fitted according to a peak location that
can otherwise be diffuse. Low pixel numbers are usually associated to the change class, i.e. when
prospective changes are too small in relation with the scene size.
• Ratio mean distance: a minimum distance between the mean ratio values characteristic of each
histogram class is necessary for 2 reasons; for the misclassification error to be low, and for being able
to identify both distribution peaks. If the classes are so close that the peak of one distribution is found
under the curve of the other, then the code cannot discern the classes and cannot properly fit the ratio
PDFs.
A performance tool has been designed for the purpose of determining the limits of these 2 conditions that
allow a scenario to be solvable (introduced below). Previous to that, it is pertinent to list all the different
source of errors that are expected or likely to affect the future VISAR/VENSAR-Magellan change detection
cases, whenever the observations are available and the empirical models are refined beyond the reference
points obtained in this work. Cronologically, they are:
1. Radiometric calibration errors related with the SAR missions to Venus:
• Inherent radiometric resolution differences translate into a limitation of the achievable change
detection when analyzing contrasts in between flows and other terrains: the Magellan radiometric
resolution was 1.75 dB while the new missions are expected to achieve 1.1/1.2 dB.
• In the same line, the accuracy in the estimation of the thermal noise of the different missions
varies. This translates into different correction errors when computing σ0, and can be responsible
for uneven noise errors due to uneven accuracy throughout a selected scene (see noise estimation
method of the TerraSAR-X mission in section 4.5.2).
2. Co-registration errors: errors in the accuracy when referencing 2 products in the same area are
expected due to the different orbital and observation geometry knowledge between the missions (lower
in Magellan compared to VERITAS/EnVision). Furthermore, co-registration methods also introduce
errors to a certain degree.
3. SAR image reconstruction errors: different levels of overall radiometric distortion when using
despeckling filters of different sizes. The impact of these differences can be severe if they are unevenly
distributed throughout the scene, as they can reduce or mask system-changes subsequently predicted
by the empirical models. Nevertheless, as seen in section 5.4, the errors introduced, at least in the case
of the modified Lee filter, are low relatively to the σ0 values. This type of errors have the vantage that
they can be studied before the VISAR/VENSAR data becomes available.
4. Model errors (model design):
138
==PAGE:140==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
• Inaccuracies in the retrieved local incidence angle values due to errors in the Magellan topography,
which introduce biases in the Magellan empirical model.
• Inaccuracies in the EO data used to create reference points for model scaling: radiometric distor-
tion when calculating σ0 and inaccuracies in the local incidence angle knowledge (lack of DEMs,
for example).
• Prediction differences arising from slightly different wavelength values between the designated test
data and the real missions. Differences in wavelength between NovaSAR-1 and VENSAR, for
example, are likely to introduce model errors; at least their radiometric difference with the current
basic level of processing achieved in this work is striking. This stresses the need of conducting
specifically designed campaigns to generate VENSAR and VISAR -equivalent datasets (see chapter
8).
5. Model errors (model application): as explained, a perfect model response implies the accurate
prediction of the system-induced σ0 transformation for any pixel θ value and any type of terrain. Since
the exact characterization of all terrain units in Venus is unlikely, it is expected that the VISAR/VEN-
SAR model won’t be able to homogeneously predict σ0 values everywhere, introducing different biases.
The idea of perfecting the model is to minimize this bias locally and globally, and when the VIS-
AR/VENSAR data becomes available, locations that can be selected (visually or automatically) as not
having any change can be used to improve the performance significantly. The most important factor
to take into account is avoiding the masking of real changes, in the same line as severe uneven radio-
metric distortions might. The likeliness of this scenario can be assessed by comparing the contrasts of
well-performing change scenarios with the system-induced contrasts of different types of terrains.
6. Mathematical framework inaccuracies: differences between the real histogram behavior and the
designed ratio PDFs to be fitted to it. Such errors have been already identified in the VENSAR test
scenario of section 6.4, where the error probability Pe has seen to be too large due to a trailing edge
fitting imprecision.
7. Threshold methodology imprecision: errors inherent to the accuracy of the threshold selection
method. As seen, the Otsu method implemented has yielded good results, but the minimum threshold
methodology does not seem to work properly in some scenarios. An implementation of a more advanced
technique could reduce threshold selection errors.
8. Histogram fitting limitations: some scenarios might not have the adequate properties to allow the
resolving of the histogram fitting, following the 2 conditions stated above. If this happens, more than
an accumulation of error, the process might not be computationally feasible. This point is addressed
in the subsequent lines by means of a newly designed performance tool.
All these errors might impact the ability of the algorithm to detect change. Particularly, the model-related
errors are the ones that pose a more critical threat to the success of the process because they are the most
likely to block the ability to discern localized real changes from the system-induced ones.
When it comes to the fitting process, the ability of the code to conduct it depends on the histogram follow-
ing one of the 4 cases presented in figure 6.1 with the 2 conditions stated above. These conditions can be
summarized as the ability of the change class to present a peak location that is distinguishable on its own
and with respect of the no-change class (since this class is very similar to the system-change class that is
parallelly constructed, it is considered equivalent here and referred to equally in the following figures).
A magnitude referred to as Quocient Factor is defined as the ratio between the number of pixels that corre-
sponds to the change class ratio mean value and the number of pixels of the same ratio value that are found
in the no-change class. If this magnitude is 1, it implies that the peak value of the change class is at the same
level as the change class curve; therefore, it requires being above 1 (1.1 has been established as a good barrier
value) for the peak of the change class to be discernible. The value of this factor depends on 2 measures that
139
==PAGE:141==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
relate to the conditions expressed before: the radiometric difference between the class peak ratio values on
one hand, and the number of pixels of the change class peak value itself. Given these relationships, a 3-D
graph can be created that provides values of the Quotient Factor as a function of the 2 variables for a given
test scenario total-change histogram. The other external condition that affects the graph is the number of
looks applied to each image when characterizing the ratio PDFs; different combinations of L2 and L1 might
achieve different performance limitations.
The following figure 6.13 provides a simulate histogram scenario that has been assessed with the mentioned
3-D graph, with L2 = 50 and L1 = 40.
Figure 6.13: Performance assessment of a purely simulated histogram case with a change and a no-change
classes.
Random gamma-distribtuted vectors have been used as images to produce a total-change ratio
estimate; the numerator image possesses a mean value of σ0 = 0.12 and L2 = 50, while the denominator is
defined with a mean value σ0 = 0.08 and L1 = 40. The Quotient Factor results do not have a smooth shape,
as the histogram always presents a ”patchy” behavior due to a limited spectral resolution (number of bins, in
this case 103). The figure shows the behavior of this factor with respect to the 2 metrics defined in the axes.
Only for a relatively large ratio mean difference and a large enough number of change ratio mean pixels, the
performance of the fitting process is envisioned to be successful.
Figure 6.13 sets the limits of the fitting process, but actually these are rather loose when comparing with the
conditions seen in most test scenarios analyzed throughout the chapter. The ratio mean differences observed
in between classes were usually larger, of the order of 0.75-1 (in the linear scale, the smallest one is found in
the Idunn Mons demonstration scenario, and it is 0.4). Moreover, the number of pixels found in the change
class peak was also higher, being the smallest one 55 (for the VENSAR-case of section 6.4). As these test
scenarios were designed taking into account the observed Magellan σ0 values of lava flows and background
terrain, it is likely that for most change detection cases the limiting conditions for the fitting performance
are not reached, which reaffirms the robustness of the method.
140
==PAGE:142==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
The analyzed performance test takes into account L2 and L1 values that are far lower than those that have
been found as the right values to fit histograms in the majority of cases reviewed. A case of, for example,
L2 = 220 and L1 = 200 is a more likely scenario. The following figure 6.14 computes the same scenario but
with these changes:
Figure 6.14: Performance assessment of a purely simulated histogram case with a change and a no-change
classes, equivalent to that of figure 6.13, but with L2 = 220 and L1 = 200.
Figure 6.14 shows that actually the performance conditions are less tight when the number of looks increases.
This implies that the change class number of pixels in the peak can be lower that in the case of figure 6.13,
and that the distance between class ratio peak values can be also lower, and still the algorithm code will be
able to fit the distributions. Hence again, the algorithm robustness is increased with the number of looks,
and possibly the conclusion is that for the expected number of looks and the contrasts observed between
expected lava flow changes and other terrains/flows, the ability to fit the histogram is secured.
6.6
Notes on change quantification from a science perspective
In chapters 2 and 3, emphasis was focused in defining the goals and the framework of this work around
the design of a change detection strategy. However, the objectives defined in section 2.2 also state that a
comprehensive framework that directly links scientific goals with the benefits of the ability to detect changes
need to be explained and derived from the strategy.
This is briefly addressed here. The main scientific goals of the community, in line with the VERITAS and
EnVision scientific goals, are to determine the level and nature of the current surface and subsurface ge-
ological activity, as well as the sequence of geological events that generated the planet’s range of surface
features; they also include the assessment on whether Venus once had oceans or was hospitable for life, and
the understanding of the organising geodynamic framework that controls the release of internal heat over the
141
==PAGE:143==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
history of the planet [148]. The precise quantification of volcanic activity is a basic factor that can be used
to develop models to predict and constraint these possibilities.
As described in detail in 3.1.3.2.2, obtaining a precise enough estimation of the lava volume after an eruption
can be used to infer lava effusion rates, which, if obtained in large enough samples, can be used to temporally
and spatially assess the planet’s heat and mass transfer, which in turn can be used to corroborate or reformu-
late the Venusian resurfacing model. The change detection methodology of this work provides, if successful,
a precise delineation of the area that has changed, and also the location of this area in the Venusian surface.
Furthermore, with the refined Magellan altimetry products of this work (and/or with future topography de-
rived from VISAR interferometry, VENSAR altimetry and also stereographic observations), the underlying
terrain features of the same area can also be retrieved.
With the topography and the change area, the volume of the lava flow can be estimated, or at least con-
strained to a great extent, assuming the Venusian surface conditions and the lava morphology according to its
radar and emissivity signatures, as well as the surrounding lava field context. In this sense, one complication
would be the presence of lava tubes, which requires of additional work addressing their likeliness in Venus
and the overestimation that they could pose when calculating lava volumes. The same lava conditions and
morphology can be used to provide constraints to the timeline of the eruption, narrowing down an estimation
of the eruption time. If this cannot be directly modeled, a more coarse estimation is constrained by the
temporal baseline of the observations and the limiting case modelled with the highest degree of uncertainty
of the eruption process factors. A good estimation of the lava volume and the eruption time should serve to
calculate the lava effusion rates; these would in turn point towards a specific magma rheology and composi-
tion, and would provide an estimation of the heat and mass transfer, as mentioned.
Another factor that can be assessed once the changed areas are delimited and located is whether erosion and
weathering play an important role in the generation of radar-detectable changes. If these cycles of modification
are timewise too long, surface morphology is not expected to change enough within the VISAR/VENSAR-
Magellan temporal baseline, and therefore the erosion rates cannot be constrained directly from radar sig-
natures alone. In fact, as mentioned in 3.1.3, the slow layer modification of the surface helps to identify
new effusive material from volcanic eruptions, as it does not have enough time between observations to be
smoothed out and become, for instance, layered material of the plain radar-dark volcanic areas that dominate
the Venusian surface. However, in another hand, the advancement of wind-transported material, or the gen-
eration of new deposits, around wind streaks and/or in dune and microdune fields, might be detectable. The
mechanism of the change detection algorithm itself pays no attention to the physical nature of the terrain
and the possible changes, and therefore from this point of view it is adaptable to any type of Venusian surface
change. However, the changes need to be radiometrically discernible by the radar; they have to be contrasted
enough for the radar radiometric resolution. This should be assessed in a global surface study that identifies
a wide sample of candidate scenarios and studies its Magellan σ0 values, as performed in section 5.1 for an
array of volcanic locations. Nevertheless, if some of these locations have already been identified in studies,
there is no a priori reason to believe that the algorithm could not be applied to these cases.
142
==PAGE:144==
Conclusions
The work presented in this MSc thesis work has mainly addressed the detection of lava flow changes from
different observations of synthetic aperture radar backscatter coefficient images, in the framework of the
VERITAS, EnVision and the Magellan missions to the Earth’s sister planet, Venus. The optically and phys-
ically thick atmosphere of the planet, the lack of human factors, vegetation, and water cycles in the surface,
the broad and extensive evidence of volcanism, particularly of effusive eruptions, and the scientific relevance
of volcanic measurements to constraint and precisely define the obscure geological and climatic history of
the planet, altogether, make SAR lava flow change detection in Venus a powerful and well-suited tool to be
developed for the science community.
Theoretical and empirical models that link lava changes with the appropriate scientific variables that model
the planet’s surface and atmosphere exist. From this perspective, comparison of future VISAR/VENSAR
imagery and Magellan products offers a unique change detection opportunity, but important challenges exist
from the radar perspective, mainly revolving around the significant technical differences between the consid-
ered radar systems. These have been identified, studied and overcome, to a certain extent, by a definition of
a change detection algorithm chain and strategy that addresses the difficulties and optimizes the detection
possibilities.
The surface of Venus has been widely surveyed and analyzed from the radar perspective. Different volcanic
lava morphologies have been identified; their locations are predictable and logical around the Venusian proto-
typical volcanic features; their shapes are radiometrically well characterized and are in general distinguishable
from other terrain units; and furthermore, their morphology and composition appear to be very similar to
most volcanic lava flows found on Earth. Since terrains are therefore a priori delineable according to their
σ0 values, and also the different radar images considered are not comparable from a coherent perspective, an
incoherent test statistic based on the fitting of a ratio intensity image with an appropiate PDF, followed by a
suitable thresholding method that separates changed pixels from those that do not, has been defined as the
core of the change detection strategy. A new mathematical framework that targets homogeneous gamma-
distributed SAR intensity images with different number of looks has been analytically derived, numerically
verified, and studied from the probabilistic framework point of view. Previously, studies on image reconstruc-
tion techniques to reduce speckle and homogenize the spatial resolution of the 2 different images have been
performed; segmentation techniques have been discarded, although further investigation is required, and a
modified Lee filter with different window sizes has been chosen as the best compromise option for the task.
On another hand, the changes between images that can be solely attributed to changes in observation ge-
ometry and wavelength have been studied as a critical step, within the algorithm chain, to be defined and
refined when working with future VISAR/VENSAR images and Magellan. The modelling of the system-
induced transformation between σ0 images has been defined, as a first rough estimation, by means of the
point-adjustment of a Magellan-derived empirical model. This model provides a global estimate of the Mag-
ellan σ0 values for the whole range of local incidence angles θ, which in turn have been derived by means of
a specifically-designed Magellan topography processing, which greatly reduces the impact of spuriously low
radius, mitigates the impact of the so-called ”Venetian blind” pattern, and allows the smooth derivation of
the slope field of any area on the surface. The Magellan model characterizes the behavior of the prototypical
143
==PAGE:145==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
radar-bright digitate lava flows observed in the Magellan SAR scenes, but also provides estimation of the
radar response of a heterogeneous mix of different terrains. The reference points to scale the Magellan model
are obtained via comparison scenarios with Earth observation data, using sensors that are a priori identified
as sharing a majority of radar design factors such as wavelength and incidence angle. Local estimates of the
VISAR model for certain look angles have been obtained with a TerraSAR-X σ0 scene of the northeast flank
of the Mauna Loa volcano. SRTM scenes are also suitable for VISAR comparisons due to the very similar
wavelength and were processed in this work, but the lack of radiometric calibration made them unfit for
comparison purposes. On another hand, a single VENSAR reference point was also obtained in a suitable
area in the slopes of the Anak Krakatoa volcanic island, as seen by the NovaSAR-1 synthetic aperture radar.
These points allow the characterization of the model transformation (Magellan to VISAR/VENSAR) for
specific scenes that possess their corresponding mean local incidence angles θ.
The end-to-end algorithm chain has been tested, and its performance analyzed, in a variety of Venusian
locations that include easy lava flow morphologies, volcanology interesting scenarios, and the most suitable
scenes for examples of VISAR/VENSAR comparisons given the geometry of the reference points. For simpli-
fication purposes and due to the fact that the scene size can be chosen arbitrarily, only a single radiometric
change associated to lava flows has been introduced at a time. The changes have been manually added to the
model-scaled VISAR or VENSAR image as a transformation of the mean σ0, preserving the speckle pattern.
The probabilistic framework revolves around the ability to detect the pixels that change (change class) with
respect to those which do not (no-change class), and this has 4 possible scenarios according to the size of the
changes and their radiometric mean; they might correspond to brighter but also darker patches, although
the observation experience dictates that most new or advancing lava flows will generate brighter values as
the background terrain is usually radar-dark. In general, all performed algorithm tests provide outstanding
results regardless of the location, the shape and the mean change of the lava flow. This is, nevertheless, sub-
ject to a perfect model response, which cannot be fully assessed until the VISAR/VENSAR images become
available. Discrepancies between the equivalent number of looks of the despeckled images and the required
number of looks to provide good fits of the histogram classes with the ratio PDFs are observed; the required
L2 and L1 are much higher, and in any case a small trailing edge misalignment is visible, which can degrade
the performance in terms of the misclassification error probability. This has been attributed to a strong
presence of texture, which has also been corroborated in a survey study.
A specially-designed performance tool that addresses the constraint of the ratio histogram fitting process has
found that, for the majority of radiometric differences between surface units and for the expected number of
looks values that characterize the PDFs, the algorithm should be able to provide precise results if the extent
of the changes surpasses a pixel population of 20/25 pixels, a circumstance that is expected. Moreover, the
possible sources of error have been enumerated and discussed, and a good and homogeneous accuracy of the
model response has been identified as the most critical performance for the strategy to be successful.
In terms of the work objectives, described in section 2.2, the results are satisfactory. All technical tasks have
been successfully implemented to a greater or lesser level. The primary objectives are accomplished in the
several test scenarios provided in chapter 6, although further exploration of performance quantification is
needed once the new mission products become available, and the ability to translate newly detected lava flow
areas to precise estimates of effusion rates has to be further studied. The particularities of the SAR image
inter-comparison of different radar systems have been also addressed, although points that have profound
impact in the future performance, such as good co-registration and radiometric calibration differences between
the missions, require of a dedicated separated study. The adaptability of the algorithm to other Venusian
surface changes requires also further analysis, but it does not appear to be an intrinsic characteristic in the
algorithm, as defined in this work, that blocks this possibility. Overall, the objectives of the work have been
proven feasible, indicating that VENSAR/VISAR-Magellan SAR change detection is within our science and
technological capabilities.
144
==PAGE:146==
Future work
While the results of the change detection algorithm are overall satisfactory, there is a wide margin for im-
provement and for additional studies in a variety of issues. The current state of the algorithm appears to
work well, in fact, but only in a narrow range of possible change detection scenarios. Multiple problems and
errors have been identified throughout chapters 5 and 6, most of which require improvements that go beyond
the scope of this work. In fact, this MSc thesis should be regarded as a VISAR/VENSAR-Magellan change
detection methodology demonstration only, setting the framework for a more refined and precise study that
is able to minimize possible errors in each of the steps and expand the algorithm’s robustness to all possible
changes in the Venusian surface.
Several points have been raised in this sense. An important one is the issue of radar texture. In section
5.1.2, texture was discarded as the information-bearer parameter for terrain classification, but at the same
time, the observed differences between the total of number of looks applied to a scene and the correspondent
calculated ENL pointed towards a significant presence of texture in the images. The fact that the L2 and L1
values required for a proper histogram fitting in all the test scenarios computed are much larger than the ENL
values, meaning that the distributions required are more narrow than they are supposed to be, and that the
fitting is observed not to be precise in the trailing edge, which is a commonly observed behavior when texture
is present, strongly suggest that the σ0 images might be better approximated by means of textured-image
estimators such as approximations of K distributions [64]. Therefore, a useful future study is the adaption
of the current ratio PDF of images with different number of looks with a framework that accounts for a
ratio of 2 different K distributions. Since K distributions arise by compounding 2 gamma distributions, such
modification might be still significantly related to the current state of the art; by all means, a study designed
to properly expand the framework is highly anticipated.
On another hand, most of the work that is envisioned to be necessary according to the tasks identified in
the change detection algorithm refer to the generation of a proper model that simulates future VISAR and
VENSAR images. The Magellan empirical model should be expanded to include estimations of all possible
terrains, and a mathematical framework should be designed to assess the variability of the radar σ0 response
according to the morphology and observation geometry. The a priori identification of different terrain units
was based in assumptions and visual inspection; a theoretical framework that designates terrain units ac-
cording to a rigid set of qualities is required in order to optimize the model and make it physically justifiable.
This thinking can be extrapolated to the construction of the VENSAR/VISAR model, largely based on mod-
ifying the Magellan model. The modelling technique presented here was very basic, consisting in a simple
point-to-point linear scaling according to the observations obtained in a suitable EO environment. First, the
Earth observation datasets used to generate the model should be enlarged and, at the same time, more precise
knowledge of the underlying topography and a refinement of the radiometric calibration is needed. Conduct-
ing specially-designed campaigns to generate VISAR/VENSAR test data, maybe by means of airborne SAR,
could be extremely useful to obtain data that provides the best Earth matches with future observations from
the missions. On another hand, and in line with the identification of surface units in the Magellan products,
the designation of the areas/patches for the generation of the points should be make according to a rigorous
method, instead of by finding a roughly suitable area with appropriate geometric characteristics, as has been
145
==PAGE:147==
MSc Earth and Space Physics and Engineering
DTU Space
done in this work (due to time constraints). Lastly, instead of simple scalings, which are unlikely to cover the
complete range of θ angles that are possible by VISAR/VENSAR, a theoretical framework with prediction
tools based on machine learning algorithms that are able to optimize and predict gaps in the purely data-
driven results might be also helpful to solidify the models’ estimations.
There is a series of factors that should be reviewed and that are important, although probably require less
work than the 2 points already highlighted. The despeckling technique selected, although it showed good
performance, is relatively basic compared to the wide array of advanced reconstruction techniques available
for SAR images, and hence further investigation might be performed in this sense.
Furthermore, even
though segmentation was discarded under the suspicion that it might introduce uneven errors masking real
changes on one hand, and also that the test statistic framework that allows for precise detection performance
quantification would not be able to perform in the case, pursuing the design of a segmentation strategy is
indeed encouraged, even if it is only for comparison purposes with the despeckling approach. In the area
of the test statistic itself, the results have only introduced changes that, although with different shapes and
dispositions, are centered around a single radiometric mean change. This implies only a distinction between
2 classes, but there is the possibility that several localized, radiometrically different lava flows have occurred
simultaneously in the same scene during the observations’ temporal baseline. In that eventuality, the total-
change ratio image is likely to present several change classes, each of them with a different ratio mean and
positioned at a distance from the no-change class following one of the 4 scenarios described in chapter 6. If
that is the case, the change detection strategy pursued here is likely to hold, but the fitting and thresholding
operations will have to be adapted to the number of identified classes and their interesections. On another
hand, simulated co-registration scenarios could be devised by applying a transformation to Magellan scenes
and then devise the optimal methodology to colocate them. If successful, this would provide an estimation
of the impact of co-registration errors in relation to the other different errors of the algorithm, and could
lead to the minimization of the global error. Finally, the adaption of the change detection framework into
test cases involving wind-driven sediment transportation is supported, requiring dedicated studies of the
radar signatures of wind streaks, dune fields and other possible features.
If these analysis suggest that
changes/contrasts, from the radar perspective, are sufficient to be detected, then the entire algorithm might
be successfully applied to these other surface change mechanisms without critical modifications.

Appendices
10.1
Appendix A
To find a pixel’s latitude and longitude coordinates using a sinusoidal projection, the code uses the pixel
number of the SAR image framelet (size 1024 x 1024) and a series of parameters given by the dataset,
following the instructions of [165]. The parameters, which are all given in each F-MIDR product header
(following a VICAR format), are:
• SAR Pixel size (Psz): pixel size of the product’s latitude.
• Projected longitude PLON: longitude which bisects the total MIDR area.
• Specline constant SPEC.
• Projection sample constant PN.
Being x, y the indices of each pixel of the SAR image framelet (from 1 to 1024 each), the latitude and
longitude values associated to each x and y can be found with the following equations:
d = 6051000
Psz
(10.1)
lat =
SPEC −y −0.5
d
 180
π
(10.2)

x + 0.5 −PN
cos(lat)d
PLON
 180
π
(10.3)
A similar procedure can be performed with the Mercator projection, using the same parameters extracted
from the header as the ones used in the sinusoidal case. The latitude and longitude values with Mercator
projection are:
